"Sensuous, swift, full of sparkling twists, [Daugharty's] is a voice so rich that a single page can be thrilling." New York Times Book Review, 1995




Massacre at Moniac Crossing

A Novel
By
Janice Daugharty






www.janicedaugharty.com
Cover art by Wes Sewell








There was a danger whenever I was on home ground. It was the danger of seeing my life through someone else's eyes-comparing it, even, with the rich domesticity of others, their pileup production of hand-knitted garments and buttery cakes. Of seeing my own work as ever-increasing rolls of inky-black barbed wire-intricate, bewildering, uncomforting. So that it might become harder to say that writing was worth the trouble.
Alice Munro












PROLOGUE


The Folkston Recorder
July 10, 1977
The Inside on the 1904 Rebellion at Moniac Crossing
By
Claude Miller


With the exception of one Mrs. Randolph Harrell, of Moniac, Georgia, most of the main participants in the following event have long ago died out, moved away, or refused to talk about that mad Sunday in which their plowshares were beaten into swords, so to speak. For those remaining, who I was unable to interview, for whatever reason, please know that this story is not intended to sensationalize private matters, to revive your pain, or to accuse unjustly. I've written this with only the best intention and that intention is solely for the sake of history and truth, though I admit to having been overly curious about what really happened that fateful evening of September 16, 1904, at Moniac Crossing. 
Early on that clear, hot Sunday morning, most of the residents of the remote Southeast-Georgia turpentine community gathered at the Moniac Crossing depot to catch a train to St. Augustine, Florida. Moniac is located on the straight-away of that Georgia jog into North Florida, neither here nor there, but on the southeastern corner of the Okefenokee Swamp. Go west about a quarter mile out of Moniac and you are in the twin community of Baxter, in Florida. Go east and you will cross a bridge over a wide bog where Georgia feeds out into Florida again-scrub oaks, pines and palmettos thriving in flatwoods muck-then the sandy East Coast and the Atlantic Ocean. 
For many of the hardworking gentlefolk of Moniac it was church-day, so those pious few who couldn't get around their consciences stayed home. The occasion was a day excursion, for blacks and whites, in the "sun and fun capital," as St. Augustine was known back then, at the expense and invitation of Nathan Shirley, a wealthy, shrewd, but by certain accounts, unscrupulous turpentine man who moved to Moniac from Macon at the turn of the century. 
According to Mrs. Harrell, Shirley had laid claim, by hook or crook, to a major portion of the turpentine and timberland in the area, and before long just about everybody in the county, black and white, was indebted to him; just about everybody, black and white, was working for him. Like others Shirley gained power, earning the title of "bossman," or "gov'nor," only by merit of his swagger and bluster, plus having the most cash, cattle, and men. 
Local cattlemen would claim an area of woods for his hog and cow range. That range belonged to the landowner but it was the duty of the cattleman claiming the range to burn off the wiregrass each winter, to tend the area in general. In return for using the woodlands he might pay off the landowner with cash or provide him with a beef to butcher each year. Farms and yards were fenced to keep livestock out, rather than in. It was the farmer's fault if somebody else's cows or hogs got in and ate his crops; it was his responsibility to keep up his fences. Under these guidelines, the Open Range Law worked to the advantage of all. But Nathan Shirley's philosophy was that if cows and hogs ranged on his land they were his, regardless who they had previously belonged to. 
"When he came to Charlton County, he brought enough men to take by force what he couldn't buy, then sweet-talked a number of us into believing it was for our own good," said Mrs. Harrell. He was never stingy with the poor but resourceful residents of the turpentine community, according to Mrs. Harrell, whose very home places Shirley came to own. But in return for his generosity he demanded respect, and as Mrs. Harrell pointed out, respect couldn't be bought. Nathan Shirley would settle for awe and the utmost loyalty, "to his face, at least." This gift trip to St. Augustine on that long-ago bloody Sunday would likewise provide Shirley with a local audience to witness and carry home the image of him boarding a yacht owned by one of his rich merchant friends in St. Augustine. 
None of those who loaded up on the small steam-powered passenger train, belonging to the Georgia Southern & Florida Railroad, could have known that when they arrived home that night at Moniac Crossing, they would have fallen heir and witness to the most terrifying and soul-revealing experience of their lives, their own Civil War reincarnate.

CHAPTER 1

What stood out in Merry Gay Rose's mind when they boarded the train to St. Augustine that morning were the church bells ringing and the birds singing and the same dozen or so cows that came up to the commissary from the woods every sundown still lazing in the leaning shade of the big white oak. Flies buzzed over them, and the added warmth of their milk-rich breath might have been fueled from burped cuds of grass taking on their body heat in the process of passing from one stomach to the other. Truth or myth, standing, the cows looked ribby and poor, but lying with their polished hooves tucked, they appeared to have more than one belly, all spread on the scarffed gray dirt like pen-fed beef. 
But those were the same things Merry Gay would notice on any Sunday morning at Moniac Crossing-not the odd things. The oddest being the fool shoes her mother Bernice was wearing as she stepped up on the coach connector ahead of Merry Gay. Almost as odd was the scary, unreal fact of taking a train to some faraway place to sing, not to mention singing for Nate Shirley, whom she despised for his hooking and crooking her family and the other old families of Moniac. He even took pride in having cheated the town out of its land by manipulating titles and plot lines, by becoming the town banker and calling loans at every opportunity.
And the queen-mother of pride: his own mother, the picky, famished-looking Miss Moxie, now approaching the ladies' coach a few steps ahead of her lately appointed servant Bernice-cherished widow of the dethroned Great Hoss Rose. Always one for putting on airs, Miss Moxie was sliding one long bony hand with her white handkerchief along the cage railing. She was dressed all in black and her hair was so black it looked indigo in the daylight. 
So, why was Merry Gay on this train bound for St. Augustine this morning? To sing, to play? Maybe because Nate Shirley had insisted and like everybody else at Moniac Crossing she found it easier to go along with him than to refuse; and too the trip was free, the only payback in the offing from the man who owed them.
Merry Gay stepped left off the creaking iron grate covering the car couplings and through the door to the stifling ladies' coach, which smelled of rank bodies and stale lavender water and the hot wooly upholstery of the high-back seats.  The coach was already packed with fanning women in a church-day profusion of plain, feathered and flowered hats. They were jabbering and cackling with their handbags and wicker snack baskets on their laps. Children squealed and chased from one end of the coach to the other, despite their mothers' scolding. One solemn little boy in knee britches, seated on the left side of the aisle near the entrance, was opening and closing his dirty hand on a buckeye fruit kept in his pocket for luck. The highlight of sunrays through the east window shone across the buckeye to the floor and the jewels on the shoes Bernice was breaking in for Miss Moxie. 
Bernice had to ask the two women on the shady right side of the coach, four rows back, to please let Mrs. Moxie sit there, out of the sun. Her pale tender skin blistered with the least little exposure. Merry Gay, behind her mother, could tell she didn't like asking the other women to move, which amounted to the same thing as telling them to move, but neither did she like being paid companion to a Methodist snob and her a plain old Baptist.
When Bernice had Miss Moxie settled in next to the window, breathing into her lilac-scented handkerchief, she sat too, clearing the aisle for Merry Gay and Nathan Shirley's granddaughter, Priss, and the other women and children filing into the coach behind them. Merry Gay had sidled into the side by side seats across from her mother. She sat next to the sun-filled window overlooking the freight-loading platform of the depot. 
Two giggling girls about Priss's age shuffled along the isle, sliding their feet and pumping their fists, making choo choo sounds with their mouths. Priss shook her blond head at such silliness. Sounds of other feet scuffing on the iron floor, much laughing and a random baby crying somewhere, melded into the high whine of locusts, the church bells, and the sighing steam of the boiler at the head of the resting train, just in from Valdosta. The chemical odor of sun-leached creosote from the cross of the tracks, along with the wood-smoke and steam-heat, rode the air through the window. Merry Gay had to bat her eyes against the strong light and grit.  
Priss, twelve and tall for her age, sat next to Merry Gay.  She had followed Merry Gay about all that summer, the older girl helping Priss search for lost treasures, braiding Priss's long flaxen hair and stringing edible necklaces of the wild berries growing in the swamps and woods where their living came from, where the trains came from. A big sister/ little sister relationship had formed after Merry Gay had gotten over trying to supplant her adopted sister Tommy, who'd recently married one of her own race and moved across the tracks to live among the Negroes. It had taken some time for Merry Gay to get used to Tommy changing races and households. It had taken more time for Merry Gay to get beyond viewing Priss as a Shirley and a pest. 
Trouble with the Shirleys was always festering, largely unsaid but about to erupt. Priss was never looked on as one of them though. She was just Priss-happy-go-lucky sprite, child of Moniac Crossing-by the grace of God, one-of-us. There was nobody who didn't love her. There was nobody who didn't either question or mourn her Shirley bond and blood: Priss loved them all, her family, spoke well of them all, except for Miss Moxie. Even warped Jay was "Uncle Jay," a joke, but a good one. Priss didn't understand how vile he was; she didn't need to. He was a blood Shirley, one of them and none of them, same as Priss.
Outside, Nate Shirley, Priss's daddy Wendall, and her uncle Jay stood aside from the separate throngs of white and Negro hands. All in their dark Sunday suits and hats and waiting till last to board in the smoking car, which they would share with the smokers of a baseball team from Macon, plus the Negro turpentine hands, and the white hands who smoked, all the way to St. Augustine, two hours away.
Merry Gay's grown brothers and a buddy, definite smokers, elbowed through the crowd and swung up onto the connector between the ladies' coach and the smoking car. Merry Gay could tell they'd been nipping already by the way they had bolted up the steps, grinning and gawking, daring any of the men on the platform, or the conductor, to suggest they wait their turn to board.
Buck, the oldest, stopped in the doorway of the ladies' coach, slim hips cocked and holding to the frame each side. He had clipper-cut brown hair-compliments to Mama!-and eyes the color of iron rust. He was tall and angular with stringy muscles showing below the rolled sleeves of his blue shirt. Women ran after him, and men ran from him; and with each fight over him or with him, he seemed to feel a little closer to his goal-King of the Mountain, or, in this case, the highest hill in the flatwoods of Charlton County. But mostly Buck just drank and cut the fool and to hell with what anybody thought. Including Mama. 
He idled along the aisle, grinning, till he got to the single empty seat in front of Bernice. He plopped down, worn work boots in the aisle and long arm crooked on the seat-back behind him.
Speaking low and for her ears only, he said, "You wouldn't happen to have a little cash on you, would you, Mama?" 
Merry Gay could smell whiskey on his breath from where she was sitting, next row over by the window.
Bernice's blue eyes blinked, her smile stretched. "Well, I?"
"If I were you, Bernice, which I'm not," Miss Moxie started, "I wouldn't give him a dime for more whiskey. Mark my word, you'll wish you hadn't."
Buck ignored her.
Bernice flashed her signature all-purpose smile at Miss Moxie; then, stringy blond head atremble, she began digging in the dingy white crocheted purse on her lap. 
"Fifty cent, a dollar," Buck said low again. "Whatever you can let me have."
Bernice took her tiny black change purse from her bigger purse, like mama and baby purses.
"I wouldn't if I was you," Miss Moxie said and dabbed at her sharp face with her handkerchief, "which I'm not. Be trouble before the day is over if you do, mark my word."
"Well, I?" Bernice stared at the woman's hawk profile. She snapped open her change purse and pinched a quarter and placed it in Buck's open palm.
He kept it open. "I was kind of looking for some cash," he said out of the corner of his mouth. "Pay you back end of the week."
"If I were you, Bernice," Miss Moxie said loud.
"WHICH YOU'RE NOT," Buck said louder and stood.
Bernice stood, pushing at his narrow back while he shuffled toward the door, glaring around at Miss Moxie, now mortified and showing it by jacking her head toward the window as if to deny him her face.
At the door, he balked. Staring down at Bernice's feet. "Mama, you look like a whore in them shoes."
"Buck!" Merry Gay scolded. 
A woman in the back of the coach laughed. Somebody else gasped. Priss slunk low in her seat, elbowing Merry Gay. 
Bernice faced them all, smiling with her head atremble as she made her way back to her seat. "Them boys," she said, "if they ain't a mess!"
Merry Gay had long ago learned to separate herself from her troublesome brothers, emotionally at least. Besides she had other things on her mind. She had never been to St. Augustine before, had never been on a yacht. She felt light-headed and swarmy-eyed from the heat, and edgy about singing for Nathan Shirley's bigshot friends, about singing in general for the first time in over a year. 
The last time she'd sung had been at Tommy's wedding, about a month before her daddy died of a heart attack. Losing them both, so close together, had come as a jolt. A sudden silence, their absence, had somehow pervaded her soul. Her singing spirit withered, her voice dried up. Her mother still got up in the morning and milked the cow and made the beds and cooked and cleaned without a snag in her stride, same as always. But Merry Gay felt stuck, waiting, longing for life the way it was. Only then could she sing again.
Fear like hot water washed over her. She should get up and leave the train now. She felt sick with embarrassment imagining trying to sing for Nate Shirley and his friends. 
She scanned for Tommy and her husband Will among the Negroes on the freight-loading platform of the commissary. When Tommy got there everything would be fine. Priss could move across the aisle, to the seat ahead of Bernice, and Tommy could sit next to Merry Gay. They would whisper and laugh, like in the old days, like they still did sometimes when they got together. Nothing had really changed.
The commissary, which served as depot and post office, as well as meeting place for the community, was a boxy white two-story building with a spindled rail balcony on the second floor. The Masonic emblem, a raised square and compass on the gable face, was all that remained of the local order after Nate Shirley closed them down. Not that he had to have an excuse for doing anything, but he claimed to have needed the big room upstairs where the Masons usually met to house his newly hired men without families (mixing was a sure recipe for trouble among the husbands and wives).
 As if in atonement for his pulling rank on the Masons, or maybe to placate the whole community as well as show off his patriotism, Nate Shirley had stuck an American flag above the Masonic emblem and it now whipped like a kite against the blue sky. 
Canvas bags of incoming mail were tossed from the train each morning and bags of out-going mail were scooped up with hoops from where they had been hung on a tall pole. Passenger trains stopped only when there were passengers to pick up; freight trains regularly stopped for shipments of distilled turpentine, tar, and pitch barreled and bound for the big naval store yard in Jacksonville, Florida. They stopped for the stacks of squared-off crossties and the mounds of peeled wood poles to be loaded on boxcars.
The Negroes waiting that Sunday morning were the same men who hauled and hewed the crossties from trees, who chipped boxes and dipped gum and mended the iron hoops holding the wooden staves in place on the gum barrels; the same men who hauled the raw pine gum or resin to the nearest fire still to be distilled into spirits of turpentine. They looked strange dressed up at the commissary, almost on level ground with the white men they worked for or alongside, except that the white men wore holstered pistols about their waists and the Negroes did not. 
Tall in a tall black hat, Will spotted Merry Gay in the window and waved.
She stuck her head out, blond hair fanning in the gusting steam of the train. "Hey, Will, where's Tommy?"
He framed his full pink mouth with his hands. "Yonder, cross the tracks." He pointed east. 
Tommy was just sitting in the Shirley's wagon, lead-lines in her small hands, watching the crowd gathered at the depot. She had on a white blouse and her long black hair was caught back in a white ribbon on her neck. She looked small and alone, distanced, even before she shook the horse into a trot, wagon and rider weaving into the sun-flecked shade of oaks along the lane. 
"Where's she going?" Merry Gay called out to Will.
"She haf to go over to Fargo for the doc for Miss Wanda."
"Oh, that's right. I forgot, the baby's sick." Merry Gay had known that the little Shirley baby, belonging to Nate and his young second wife, was sick, but didn't see why Tommy, who kept house for them, had to be the one to go for the doctor and miss the train trip to St. Augustine. 
She drew her head inside and hung it the way Will did everyday when he saw her.  She was afraid to look out again. What if she'd started a fight? She bit her bottom lip. It wasn't as if she was that friendly with Will usually; he was a Negro man, she was a white woman. Too many people at Moniac Crossing, even knowing the circumstances of Tommy's birth, upbringing and marriage, would still view Will and Merry Gay as being suspiciously friendly. If not for the excitement of the trip, Merry Gay knew Will would never have waved and shouted at her. It was like seeing an enemy in a strange town and the surprise encounter causing him or her to seem like an old friend. Not that Will was an enemy, but he would always be a stranger by virtue of race. She and Tommy had talked about it and decided there was nothing to be done about it. 
Priss, who was wrenched round in her seat, talking to one of the other girls, spoke back over her shoulder to Merry Gay. "I forgot. Tommy said to tell you not to look for her on the train this morning." 
"I saw her yesterday. Why didn't she tell me?"
Priss shrugged her shoulders, sat straight in the seat. She looked magnified in the sun, too blond and too bright-eyed. "I guess she thought you wouldn't go without her. I guess." She looked down at her small boneless hands on the lap of her white linen dress.
"Is that why you didn't tell me either?" Merry Gay was a little miffed at both of them. "You thought I wouldn't go?"
"I guess."
Merry Gay spoke louder, sharper. "Well, I'm here. Tommy doesn't have to hold my hand everywhere I go."
In that clear instant voice, and not even batting her blue eyes, Priss said, "We can hold hands if you want to." Then she flashed a shy smile, babyish suddenly, and held out her right hand.
Merry Gay laughed. "That's not what I meant, silly."
"That old croup again," Miss Moxie across the aisle said to Bernice. "I do hope that colored gal doesn't get off and forget the way back and that baby ailing."
"Tommy?" Bernice said. Not really a question but a comment.
The Roses had raised Tommy after her mother Nancy died in childbirth. Bernice had wet-nursed Tommy, just as Nancy had wet-nursed Bernice's two boys. Then she had kept Tommy to raise with her one-week older Merry Gay, until Tommy turned fifteen and married Will Thomas. Nancy and Bernice had delivered each other's babies-six among them, two boys for Bernice and four girls for Nancy. The two women had grown up together in Moniac, on the white side of the tracks. Finally Bernice had a girl, Merry Gay; a week later, Nancy who had all girls birthed another girl. On her birth-bed, deathbed, Nancy asked was it another girl and Bernice lied, knowing that Nancy had started hemorrhaging and the bleeding would stop only after her limp, clammy body was drained; Bernice said no, it's a boy. At last Nancy had birthed a boy. With her final breath, she said, "Name him Tommy, after my sweet old uncle, bless his soul." 
At first, Merry Gay's daddy, Hoss Rose, and her brothers, hadn't agreed for the little Negro girl to stay. But Tommy's daddy and four sisters had moved on with one of the sawmill crews, not even stopping off at the Rose house to say good-bye. Then Hoss and the boys grew to love the tiny pale big-haired girl, teasing that she got that white skin from nursing Bernice, whose cream had finally come down.
But Merry Gay had been witness to the breaking of the Great Hoss Rose's heart and consequently his falling in love with Tommy. Merry Gay had the measles and Bernice had sentenced her to what she called the measles room, the small barely-used middle room, right side of the hall. Burlap sheeting over the single window and Merry Gay feverish and itching and red-speckled as her brothers when they caught red bugs in the woods. She was daresome to touch the rough brown screen of burlap for a bit of light and to ease her boredom, to even rise from the iron-steaded bed. But she could hear her daddy's boots tromping up the hall, past the parlor on the left, then backing up as if testing the floor for weak joists. 
From what Merry Gay gathered from where she lay, and was later told by her daddy, he had seen tiny Tommy in a gray sack shift sitting on the cold hearth in the parlor, tears running down her face. Sad-eyed, soot-smudged, lonesome. Neither spoke. He crossed the room and squatted before her, a white giant and her so small she could have been a fairy hatched from the brick chinking. 
"You miss your sister, don't you?" he said finally.
She only nodded, black eyes wide and shimmering with tears.
He lifted her under the arms and placed her chest to his chest, head on his shoulder and her bare feet dangling like a crane's at his waist. Rocking her as he walked and her sniveling, burying her face in his neck as if by hiding her eyes Bernice in the kitchen, should she walk out to the hall, couldn't see her. 
At the middle room on the right, Hoss opened the door easy to the sick-room light, shushing Merry Gay now sitting up in bed and watching. He tipped-toed over and lay Tommy on the other side of the bed, pulling the covers from beneath her, then over her stiffened body. 
All hell broke loose when Bernice caught him tiptoeing out of the room and, just before he closed the door, saw Tommy in the bed with Merry Gay, one speckled as a fawn and the other too light-skinned to be flesh and blood, much less legally Colored.  
He stood before the door. Merry Gay and Tommy could see the knob twisting, while Bernice bawled him out for deliberately exposing Tommy to the measles, after all her hard efforts to keep them apart. 
Eyeing each other with their arms crossed on the pulled-up covers, the two girls listened and smiled. "Little thing was bound to get the measles by and by," Hoss said, "and there's worse things, if you ask me."
"I'm not asking," Bernice shouted. "Now get on back over there to the commissary and tend to your own business."
Neither had Bernice asked permission to keep Tommy after she was weaned. In fact Bernice seemed not even to know that she had made a decision to keep the child. One day simply led into another and who was counting, days or children, anyway? Keeping the baby was the right thing to do, the practical thing to do. And neither Bernice nor Nancy could afford to be anything but practical.
Merry Gay often wondered whether Tommy would have been as welcomed by her daddy and the boys if not for that white skin inherited from Nancy, who left a legacy for her daughter as having had a grade of resin named after her. XX, or extra clearest, was purist and brought the best price; W.W. grade, or water-white, was the next best grade, followed by W.G., or window-glass; then the lowest grade of filmy pale resin, "light as Nancy McCall's complexion." Not worth a dime, the label, and really not meant to flatter, not even meant to draw attention to the Negro woman's light skin but as a handy analogy. 
If anybody had been figuring bloodlines, they would have found that Nancy was part Creek Indian and Bernice was her own blood kin, give or take a dram or two. But the point was, as far as Bernice was concerned, that the whole blood business didn't make much sense. Seeing as how it had to do with how much or how little pigment, how dark or how light, that placed you here or there and made you a grade more or less valuable, like resin. She just loved Nancy and Nancy loved her and the two of them together lightened the load of raising their separate families. One husband being light and the other dark was what had placed them on opposite sides of the tracks.
Seated behind Miss Moxie and Bernice on the train, the Elton twins, chaste with their faded red hair up in buns, agreed that Mr. Shirley was a real gentleman for inviting everybody to St. Augustine for the day. "Say what you will," one said, "but he's a gentleman."
A woman behind them said, "Huh!" Meaning she knew the truth, they all did, and Nate Shirley was no gentleman but a scoundrel. 
Merry Gay stared out the window again. Nathan Shirley in his crisp white shirt and gray striped vest was laughing with his two sons and the train conductor in a green visor. The Negroes had inched closer along the sunned-wood platform, waiting to board. Sweat like grease on their dark faces. Will with his hat in his hands-no blood from fighting, as Merry Gay had feared when the two had earlier exchanged greetings-and he was smiling. A handsome, smart buck, everybody said so, not a mean bone in his body.
When Tommy fell for Will she began going to the Negro church on the west side of the tracks. Their preaching always ran over the hour set aside for the white churches on the east side of the tracks, and Merry Gay would wait for her at the commissary to walk home. The commissary was located on the south end of the west side of the tracks, facing the Rose house across the tracks, bare dirt yard running into the yard of the first row shanty on the right and on the left into vine-swagged woods. 
Especially at night, the Negro churchgoers would linger over singing, stomping on the wood floor of the narrow, one-room church house. Sounded like drumbeats, and the whites would settle in for the night to the foot-stomping rhythm, not really minding, not really disturbed, but used to it like the ticking of their clocks, like the blast and rumble of the trains that shook their very beds.
Seated on the dark front steps of the commissary, in an aura of lightening bugs, Merry Gay had watched Will place his great hands on Tommy's pinched waist and lift her up to sit on the well rim, eyes never leaving her eyes. The spot where they stood seemed to glow in the light of the sandy white dirt, or was it from something burning inside them? Even the keening of insects seemed spun of that glow, voice of some un-expressible magic. A chamber of mystery and silence, at once too shrill and too silent, too light and too dark. Together they were no longer Tommy Rose and Will Thomas, born to suffer and then die. They were living and this was forever.
Hand over hand Will drew a bucket of water from the well and set it on the ground at Tommy's bare feet, then gently began dabbling water on her feet, anointing each, then drying each on his shirttail. Lifting her down then, he pressed her into the well wall with his body-tenderness spent, and in its place pain, or an urgency that could pass for pain. Her arms hooked around his neck. They didn't speak. They kissed. 
Merry Gay had closed her eyes. She shouldn't have been seeing this. But branded on the backsides of her eyelids were Tommy, petite in the long bleached muslin dress, bustle caught up with a pink ribbon, that Bernice had made for her that summer; and Will so tall he had to bend down, but a perfect match. Him so handsome and dark, and her so pale she could pass for white.
 Tommy was startlingly beautiful, at first glance, but a closer look revealed a face too starkly broad, too white. Too much face, too much hair. Her skin was the absorbent texture of blotting paper, her black liquid eyes were wide set, and her lips were too flat and pale for her to be full-blood Negro-everybody said that. The kiss looked daring, straddle-of-the-tracks dangerous. But everybody at Moniac Crossing knew that Tommy was Negro and must have expected that one day she would cross those tracks. Still, they reacted to her pairing off with a Negro man with the kind of shock generally reserved for whites only. 
Merry Gay secretly viewed Tommy's crossing over as a betrayal, but only until she realized that Tommy would be coming and going, crossing back. All that had changed was the swapping of bedfellows and Merry Gay having a young Negro brother-in-law who hung his head and called her ma'am and never ate at the same table with his wife's adoptive family. 
Not that Bernice didn't ask. And people didn't know whether she was stupid or crazy, but on the off-chance that that smile of hers might mean she knew something they didn't, few dared to offer an opinion. Besides, she was the widow of the Great Hoss Rose and that stood for something. That alone granted her the possibility of a Solomon-like wisdom by association with the man they all loved. 
One huge sigh and the train whistle tooted twice; the brass bell clanged and the train rocked and lurched southeastward along the tracks; couplings rankling and clanking, jolting car to car along the line. One of the twins squealed; her sister told her to hush. The other women laughed, faces red with excitement and heat. Building a head of steam, the locomotive increased its speed and chuffing and the morning sunlight softened as it sifted through the fine leaves of the chinaberry trees before the passing neat shanty-rows on the Negro side of the tracks. The small frame houses, separated by paling or rail fences and lanes running north and south, on Merry Gay's side, ran out to green pines and palmettos and embedded black streams. Sun-glades divvying the dark flowing water of the north prong of the creek, as seen from the trestle, as seen from the train.
 Merry Gay and Tommy had walked across the trestle many times, staring down at the meandering creek but they had never been over it on a train before. It didn't seem right without Tommy, especially with her husband aboard the train, moving away from her. It seemed cruel and unnatural for the two to be parted on this special day. If Merry Gay were married she'd hate to be split up that way. Not that she had any intention of marrying: several boys and men were interested in her and she had been interested in a couple of them. But her brothers always found fault or went behind her back and threatened her suitors. It was just as well, she thought, just as well because for now all her passion was pent up as song. Songs locked up in her soul but still living, and soon she'd have to pull one out to entertain a group of people she'd never seen and couldn't care less about.  
"It's most unseemly," Miss Moxie had crowed to her son, "for that white child to be singing at a colored wedding. But then what can you expect and the two of them raised up together?" That was at the commissary the Saturday before Tommy's wedding on Sunday, a year ago. "First, they try to pass that gal off for white, then next thing you know she's marrying a turpentine hand, black as the ace of spades. If I was you I'd be careful letting our Priss hang around with that other Rose girl after this."
"Merry Gay's got guts and a certain kind of tough cracker class," he said from the other side of the counter. "She's resourceful. Hope it rubs off on Priss and maybe her head will amount to more than something to hang a fancy hat on."
Miss Moxie placed one hand atop her black straw hat, getting his meaning. 
He went on, "My daughter-in-laws are the bad influence, well-bred but good for nothing."
Merry Gay, walking across the porch, stepped through the door with a basket of eggs to swap for sugar and one of the books he had loaned her. 
"Well if it isn't my songbird," he called out.
Ignoring Miss Moxie, who in a fit of embarrassment at being caught appeared to have taken a real interest in something on the floor, Merry Gay handed him the book.
"How did you like Kipling?" he asked in that sunny fake tone. 
"I liked Kim."
"India, yes. You should go there someday." 
She cut him short by pushing the basket of nest-warmed eggs across the counter toward him; she was too busy to hear him brag about his travels today. And that's exactly what he would be doing: talking about a story set in some place he had been; luring her into talk about the characters, which Nate Shirley would subtly, inadvertently, turn into himself.
The locals laughed behind his back about him informing them of an astronomer investigating the effects of the sun on weather and the effects of weather on tree growth-something they'd already learned by experience. They could have told him that it takes eighty-five barrels of raw gum or resin to distill into one barrel of spirits of turpentine. He tried to tell them it wasn't necessary to streak so deep into the pines to make the gum run, only down to the cambium layer, and even proved it. Plus, deep gashes lessened the life of the pine. The old-timey technique of cutting boxes into the pines for the gum to collect in was about to be replaced with the hanging of clay pots called Herty cups below the streaked faces. Boxing was sure death to the pines. The town took him at his word but never admitted it. They tuned him in when he suggested cooking needles, bark and other trash with the resin at the fire still before straining it through the cotton batting and wire mesh, instead of the other way around, to get an improved grade of resin and make it yield more per barrel. They tuned him out on the three-rings study to date past events. It was Shirley's foresight that led to hauling the gum-sopped dross, or cotton batting, away from the still, rather than piling it to one side for anybody wandering past with a grudge and a match to set fire to. Had he not talked down to the other men and taken advantage of their innocence they would have welcomed his book knowledge. They would have shared with him what they knew. Instead, they took his big talk as one more weapon he could use to swindle them out of their knowledge and skills, same as he'd used his money and power to swindle them out of their land.
"What'll it cost me for a song this fair morning?" He always said that; Mary Gay never sang for him though.
"Mr. Nate, you'll have to come to the wedding tomorrow if you want to hear me sing."
And he had, bringing Priss with him to spite Miss Moxie.
Seated on Merry Gay's left, aisle-side of the ladies' coach, Priss tugged her arm, then whispered into her ear that she had to be excused. Merry Gay stood and Priss stood and stepped out into the aisle. 
Bernice caught Merry Gay's arm, shaking it, pulling her close and whispering in her ear, "You see the boys, tell em I said behave." Not really a message, only showing what was on her mind and it wasn't altogether her paining feet in Miss Moxie's shoes. Other than maybe the Elton twins and possibly the Shirley women, Merry Gay doubted there was a woman on board who hadn't seen their men turn celebration into disaster. The way it went, the men would start out a bit cold to the notion of being included in the daytime doings of women and children, when usually they would have been hard at work; then they would warm up by having a friendly drink or two, then heat up gradually by heaping more whiskey on the fire in their heads, merriment quickening into bonfires of rage with the least provocation, or even no provocation at all.
"The boys," Merry Gay's brothers, both over twenty but under thirty, would always be boys as long as they drank and felt sorry for themselves and begrudged the Shirleys taking over the timber business in and around Moniac Crossing.  Bernice often reminded them that their daddy didn't fade from life sitting on the porch because he was forced to; he'd been stubborn and refused to work for the Shirleys or any other man. He could have gone to snaking and hewing crossties for the railroad, among other things Bernice could name. But Merry Gay knew that was just brave talk; she knew that Bernice's smile wasn't really smiling. Bernice begrudged the Shirleys too for hiring the Rose hands by paying off their debts to the commissary, therefore indebting them to him, so they would have to work for him to pay off what they owed.
Moniac Crossing, like most turpentine camps, minted its own money in the form of commissary checks and coupons. But unlike Hoss, who encouraged the men to square up and stay square, Shirley saw to it that they were enslaved to him by debt. They owed him, he owned them. He was "the man," the man with the money. Money talks, and before long Hoss and the boys were forced to listen and even Charlie and Buck went to work for Shirley. Not only did Shirley end up with Hoss's land, his turpentine hands, and his commissary, but he ended up with the be-knighted sons of the Great Hoss Rose.
It was a known fact that Nathan Shirley had been serving his hands moonshine whiskey each morning before they headed out to the woods; that was one more reason he had been so successful in winning over the labor from the other turpentine men.  A gut full of hundred-proof shine could ease you through at least half a day of lugging the leaden buckets or reaching overhead to pull a streak with the heavy irons-some claimed they could actually pull a neater streak while on shine. The boxes they carved in the pine trunks to collect the gum would be sloped so that the bossman could roll a fifty-cent piece straight from front to back. Some claimed that come end of the week, a gift jug of blue boiling shine would be waiting at the commissary for the man who had dipped the most buckets of gum or chipped the most boxes. And the claim proved true.
Hoss himself had mourned the great Casey Jones with his harmonica on that very porch, imitating the whippoorwill call of his train's whistle, drawing half or more of the community to hear how Jones had died a hero in Vaughn, Mississippi, that April night in 1900. How he had thrown the switch on the engine into reverse after spying a stalled freight train on the tracks up ahead, ramming the rear of it anyway, dying himself, but saving all passengers aboard by reducing his speed. Not too many men who would do that.
Later, Hoss would sit out on the porch, picking out a ballad about Jones on his banjo, and again the community would gather to hear the Great Hoss Rose, who was just such a man himself. He loaned them money when they were broke; he sawed their lumber free when their houses burned down; he gave them credit at the commissary even when they were in debt to him. 
One of their rascally sons, perched on the edge of the well in the commissary yard, trying to step onto his tomwalkers, fell backwards, tumbling into the well, and it was Hoss Charlie who rescued him by tying off a rope to the white oak and shinnying down and hauling the boy up with one arm. 
So, the commissary was where Hoss Rose went to confront the scoundrel who had stolen all he owned and then capped off the blow to his pride by stealing his sons. On a Friday at sundown, no less. Knockoff time, payoff time. Hoss must have been sitting there on Bernice's porch, across the tracks, watching the hands pass in and out of the commissary. Fuming over the fix he'd found himself in-bereft of fortune and future. Reduced to sitting on the porch like a woman, where generally he would have been in the thick of the man-talk and kerosene heat of the commissary lamps, at the desk in back of the store, on his right the small cold wood stove standing in its framed bed of sand, spat snuff and cigarette butts. The blue speckled coffeepot he started each morning would smell of stale coffee, grounds he had to strain through his teeth. And before him on the desk, the green commissary account book that held all manner of information about the men he worked. Just looking at the names crossed off the pages, the men gone on, was a lesson in history and they all had a story, and he cared more for them, all of them, than to prop them up with shine to sweat a little extra cash from their sun-burned hides. 
Rumor was, on slow Mondays, common in the quarters as a result of the turpentine hands' hard drinking on the weekends, Nate Shirley would dope a jug of shine with Carter's Little Liver Pills, a cold remedy called 666, the vile, harsh purgative known as Black Draught, and whatever else remained in the out-dated, rat-gnawed boxes on the commissary medicine shelf. He would go shack to shack, offering the concoction as his special cure for the hangover.  Not really an offering: all who suffered from the affliction were ordered to partake. Hoss Rose regarded Nate Shirley's forced dosing as punishment, one more attempt to lord over them his control. Some even claimed Shirley would sit his horse out in the woods during the day, spying on the workers squatting and shitting and puking till they passed out.
Anybody watching that hot summer evening of a Friday would have seen Hoss Rose rise from his chair on the front porch of his house, somewhat slumped over but still tall and bear-broad. Somewhat defeated but not whipped. He still had his strength, though he'd months ago quit fighting, and he couldn't have told a living soul who his title as area bully had gone to. He didn't care. 
Of all people, Charlie Rose, who his daddy never could get to work a full day, was grinning, walking out of the open double doors of the busy commissary, tarry right thumb hooked through the handle of the prize glass demijohn jug of shine. Brother Buck, behind him, was looking down, counting his cash. Both of them tarry, filthy, smelling of sweat. 
Still grinning, Charlie raised the jug in greeting when he spotted his daddy standing on the doorsteps-he was always slow to catch on. Buck, pocketing his money, looked up at his daddy, what few wrinkles he had more like grains in wood, those up-tilted pupil-black eyes. "Uh oh!" Buck said and sidled left out of the doorway where he'd been standing, out in the open and looking for a place to run.
Hoss planted one heavy work boot on the porch floor, leaning forward to shift his weight and step up. "Ain't no hard feelings, boys," he said. "Didn't have a thing in the world left for y'all to do no how."
He sounded sane as any man; Charlie bought it. He uncapped the jug, walking on out to the porch, and in a flurry of candle-flies and tar and sweat, he offered his old man the first drink.
"You dumb son-of-a-bitch!" Buck called out to Charlie and started for the north end of the porch, almost there when the jug of shine rolled into the backs of his boot heels and he had to jump it to keep from being knocked down. It rolled on toward the end of the porch ahead of him, puking whiskey that looked like water but fizzed like Red Devil lye and bleached a running scallop design on the wide floorboards, still there to this day.
At his back, Buck could hear what sounded like bone crunching, then a cross between a moronic grunt and a groan. When he looked around he saw Hoss Rose step high over Charlie, curled on his side on the floor and nursing his jawbone, and grab Nathan Shirley in the doorway by the collar of his white broadcloth shirt, socking him between the eyes, then shaking him out like a rag before letting go.
He landed sitting, rocking in one of the porch chairs. A blue knot, like a Cyclopean eye, in the center of his forehead. 
Not another word; Hoss didn't say another word. It was like a demonstration, a last hurrah, and he could go back across the tracks to his front porch and rock and watch. Boys be-damned!
Merry Gay remembered the days of her family's self-sufficiency but they didn't seem half as fine as her daddy on the front porch or driving her to sing over three counties after he "tired," as he called it. He would stand her on a wooden crate at church, when she was little, or on a stump at family reunions and fish fries and like a bird she would sing. Just for him. He played the guitar and the banjo, would change-up from time to time, never noticing that his daughter's four-octave soprano was neither a guitar nor a banjo voice, but more suited to the accompaniment of a piano or flute (that's what Nate Shirley said, offering to play for her while she sang). She didn't sing the way she talked; singing was a different command from the brain. It was as if she were two people, a trained classical singer in the corn-fed body of a country girl.
Reeling along the aisle of the ladies' coach, Merry Gay and Priss met the ticket-taker coming through the exit door and asked for directions to the lavatory. He told them to go through the smoking car, pointing behind him, and they would find the lavatory at the start of the next car down the line, the dining car. 
Holding to the backs of the seats and swaying with the motion of the train the girls pushed through the closed bottom section of the door leading out to the brief shuffling grate over the couplings between cars. Plastered to the extended rooflines above the steps, left and right sides, were decals reading, "Watch your head." Hot air, grit and cinders, but the air smelled fresh, of pine. Stepping through the bottom section of the door to the smoking car, the fresh air and the labored iron ratcheting of the wheels stood out in contrast to the merriment of men with drink in their bellies, tobacco smoke, sweat and whiskey, and the raw volatile musk of the male animal.
There was a sudden simmering silence-men on hold till the ladies could pass through the car.  
All except for the baseball team from Macon, who Merry Gay and Priss had seen at the Moniac depot. Grouped, talking, smoking and sipping Dope Cokes. Canvas bags of balls and bats and uniforms were spread on the floor and in the seats. Priss, in the lead, flashed them a pretty smile, twinkling from her blond head to her dainty shoes. They parted for the girls gathering their dress tails to keep them from touching the men on each side and lowering their eyes to make themselves invisible. Merry Gay had held her breath, fearing the foreign boys would speak and get her brothers started. She could see them in the crowd near the middle of the car, Buck drinking from a pewter flask of whiskey, and her other brother Charlie standing next to him, dumb-looking in his thick glasses and his mouth parted, the tip of his pink tongue held between his teeth.
Placing Priss in front of her and walking side to side and fast, shoving her along, Merry Gay eyed her two brothers. Buck with his drunk-eyed wild look appeared stuck in mid-guffaw. How he always looked when drinking. No message from Mama for Buck, who wouldn't listen if an angel from the Lord brought it. But let one of those men speak out of the way to his sister and he would come unstuck in the blink of an eye. Charlie too, meaty, red-haired and bearded, blank-eyed behind his thick glasses. Not that either of them should have worried: only a man in his wrong mind would speak out of the way in the presence of Shirley's favorite granddaughter.
Priss got to be his favorite grandchild, for one thing, by being old enough to have outgrown the foolishness of childhood; for another, she sweet-talked and teased him, daring him to scold her, and she never minced words when she caught him in the wrong.
The week before, Nate Shirley had stopped the four doorstep girls belonging to one of his top Negro hands on their way up the heat-wavy lane to the depot. They were all frocked up and had train tickets in their hands. When he'd asked where they were going, they'd said to Jacksonville for the day. On the spot, west side of the tracks, Shirley had taken away their tickets and accused their daddy of sending them on ahead to another turpentine camp, of trying to skip out on what they owed him in loans and groceries at the commissary. He was on to them, Shirley said, and knew that a big turpentine man up around Gainesville, Florida, had sent them the tickets to leave in advance and that their daddy would be sneaking out in the middle of the night without either him or the other turpentine owner bothering to pay off the debt. 
The girls were crying, clinging to one another, across the tracks from the finest house at the Crossing, the Shirley house. Priss, leaning on the white paling gate to watch and listen, pushed it open and ran barefooted across the brilliant sand of the east lane, up the rail-bed of sharp rocks and over the hot tracks. 
Having heard the whole thing, she was almost in tears herself at seeing the girls so upset. "Papa Shirley!" she shouted. "How dare you make these girls cry? What if some man made me cry like that?" Hands on her hips, she stepped up between them and her grandfather. "Now, give them back their train tickets." 
From out of the north woods the rumble of the train was swelling, and they could feel the dirt throbbing beneath their feet from the vibrations of the tracks. 
"Priss," he said, "you don't know these people. How they jump camp in the middle of the night and leave owing you."
"Well, it's a free country," she said. "Besides, that's between you and their daddy. Now give back their tickets and go stop that train this minute."
Grudgingly, he had passed out the tickets to the four girls; he'd even flagged down the train for them to board. Priss waving good-bye to the girls and Nate Shirley knowing he'd seen the last of them. He'd seen the last of his top hand too unless he could waylay him come nighttime after Priss was safe in bed and asleep. Otherwise, he would have to wait and travel to Gainesville, to confront the other turpentine man, which could result in a killing.  This wasn't the first time this sort of thing had happened, and last time the two turpentine owners had pulled guns on each other. Shirley had stayed up many a night to guard his labor. 
He feared no man.
But all this bubbly scrap of a woman had to do was speak and he was sop.
The Negroes in the smoking car now were laughing, playing cards, staring out the west windows at the dashing green sun-flocked woods, as Priss and Merry Gay passed on their way to the lavatory. What white women? We ain't seen no white women! Negroes on the west side of the train and whites on the east side, like the railroad tracks at home. 
It hit Merry Gay with a pang that none of the Negro women were along, and she felt stupid for not noticing before, for not catching on to why. She figured that Tommy knew and hadn't told her and she felt more cut off from Tommy than when she had married Will. Tommy had truly switched sides. If Tommy had been on this train to St. Augustine, would she have had to ride with the men in the smokers' car? Or maybe some other car set aside for colored women?  What would have happened? What would Merry Gay have done? She felt like Priss, always mouthing off about what was fair and unfair but powerless to do anything about it. 
Stepping from the smoking car to the solid air and broken ratcheting, Priss shouted to Merry Gay, "Hey, what was the meaning of that? I wanted to talk to those boys."
"No. Buck and Charlie are drinking, itching to fight somebody."
"Oh!" she said as if she understood, but didn't. She was growny acting but innocent and pure as a baby.
Pushing through the door to the dining car and the ladies' lavatory on their left, the girls met Priss's Uncle Jay and her daddy Wendell Shirley, hands in their pockets, rattling change, passing through the trembling wine-velvet curtain partition a few yards up the narrow corridor, heading for the smoking car, either to smoke or keep order. Gold chains attached to their watches looped down their dark vest fronts.  From the dining car came the sounds of silverware clinking on plates, mellow laughter and morning voices.
 A tall shambling Negro man pushed open the door the girls had just passed through. Seeing the Shirleys, he switched gaits to a sudden loose swagger, then tipped his beret and said, "Gov'nor, sir," to each in turn, and walked on toward the men's lavatory on his right. 
"Hey!" Wendell shouted after him. "Shorty, you!'
The man wheeled and stopped with his arms out as if expecting to be shot.
"Get on back to the smoking car. You people's got a place to go in there. This is white-folk territory. Get it?"
Dread of passing again through the gang of whites standing inside the door was written all over Shorty's face. Like Priss and Merry Gay in the smoking car, he lowered his eyes and walked on through to the door. Invisible.
Yes, Merry Gay was glad Tommy hadn't come; she could imagine all sorts of disasters. She felt like Peter betraying Jesus because if Tommy had been along and somebody had graded her by race Merry Gay didn't know what she would have done. If Tommy had been placed in the smoking car and not the ladies' coach, if she'd been stopped from going inside the ladies' lavatory, what would Merry Gay, who hated trouble, have done? Her sure cowardice was almost a taste, an odor, like the staleness of the closed hall seeping up through the stringent toilet chemicals.  
In the din of outside racket, as the tightly-sprung door slowly opened and closed, freeing the Negro man, Wendell Shirley had to lean closer to the girls, cupping one shiny shell-like ear for Merry Gay to explain about the lavatory. He took out his gold pocket watch, checking the time by the Roman Numerals behind the gleaming watch face, then shook his finger at his daughter, as if to say hurry up, go back to the ladies' car. Both girls nodded.
While Merry Gay swayed with the train and waited outside the lavatory door for Priss to finish using it, Nathan Shirley popped through the wine velvet curtains partitioning off the lavatory from the dining car. His perfectly symmetrical face broke into a smile. He had to be sixty or sixty-five years old, she guessed, but he looked younger than his son Jay because he was trimmer, big-man tall and with a clean haircut and nails. His skin was so scrubbed, he looked waxed. Not one loose hair or whisker or word, except sometimes when he was talking down to her, then he would pipe up with that impeccable but phony cracker drawl. Mouthing in the racket of the wheels trundling along the tracks, he said, "My little songbird. Make me proud, you hear?" 
Merry Gay's nerve endings flared at the thought of singing in front of the rich strangers on the upcoming cruise. She was used to her own common folk; whether or not she'd ever met them before, they were her people. Maybe she felt safe with them because she knew they had little to compare her to except themselves, or to the other girls who sang in the various church choirs. But Merry Gay's was the purest, brightest voice in the land. That is, in the land of her people.
She'd overheard her mother talking to Nathan Shirley while ironing his fine white shirts, saying, "Merry Gay was born to sing and she's going to live to sing. I doubt she'd let you pay her for singing on your cruise."
Nate Shirley had trained Bernice himself to iron his heavily starched broadcloth shirts, using the Chinese method: lay the shirt front-side up, smooth and flat on the padded board, and match the side seams with precision. Then sprinkle with water and press hard with a hot iron from hem to the seam connecting the collar. Iron one side of the shirt front and then the other, then open up the shirt and sprinkle liberally again with water, ironing the back panel inside-out. Finish with the sleeves, yoke and collar. 
Merry Gay would like to have accepted the money he'd offered. She'd never been paid for singing before, and as she told her mother, "You get paid for ironing, Tommy gets paid for keeping house, Nathan Shirley gets paid for a barrel of gum dipped by my brothers, who get paid for dipping it for him." But deep down, she felt that her singing was different, something given and not earned. She was like a songbird for real, in a way: singing because she could. She made her noise through singing, she added to the voices of her world. Sad or happy songs, her soul was purged.
Nate Shirley had traveled the world over, he bragged, and everywhere he traveled he learning something new to bring back to the South. Like Chinese ironing. But in spite of going to the opera and hearing the great choruses throughout Europe, he said, he couldn't learn to sing. He could play the piano and the violin, but he couldn't sing. That was his sole act of humility and was worth more than money to Merry Gay. Through her singing her deceased father had something his enemy Nate Shirley could never have, a song he couldn't sing, a songbird he couldn't buy.


CHAPTER 2

At the depot in St. Augustine, the party bound for the yacht was picked up by three open black motor cars, driven by smiling Negro men. They called the Shirley men "gov'nor," and the ladies they called "the misses," not really addressing the women and girls but speaking to each other or the Shirley men. Except for the Shirleys, who owned a motor car, few of the train passengers from Moniac Crossing had ever ridden in one. The roads around Moniac were too boggy and rough to accommodate motor cars. 
"Say dey's a bad herrican moving in," one of the drivers said to Nate Shirley.
"Well, we need the rain, don't we?"  
"Yes suh, sho do now. But dat wind?dat wind a diffent matter."
Nate laughed and winked at Wendell on his right. "Playing up to me for a tip," he said to his oldest son, then to the driver, "Say you don't like the wind, huh?" 
Caught up in the racket of the train's gusting and belching steam, shoe soles scuffing on the dusty deck of the depot, everybody laughing and shouting, Merry Gay almost forgot why she had come. Fear pushed back and in its place excitement. 
The Moniac folk looked dearer to her, lost on foreign ground, and made her want to protect them.  She'd like nothing better than to get back on the train with them and head home. At the same time, she liked the newness of the experience-if only she could spend the day with these people she knew. Watching them in their finery, blinking in the open coastal light like animals stepping out of the woods, Merry Gay felt proud too. They were honest people, humble people, doing their best, looking their best. Even their awkwardness was endearing.  
Merry Gay and the six Shirley children were packed into the third motor car; Miss Moxie, Bernice and the wives of the Shirley boys loaded up in the second car; and Nate Shirley and his two sons in the first car, waving their hats in the soft sea air at the Moniac folk who had set out on foot from the depot along the cobbled streets of the city for a day of sightseeing. Everybody laughing and talking; and the motor cars chirring like big crickets past the old fort and along the palm-shaded boardwalk of the Intercostals Waterway.
Merry Gay called out to her brothers. Buck and his best buddy Mike Ruis were loping along, with half-blind, hefty Charlie trailing behind, flanked by Will and another young Negro turpentine hand from Moniac Crossing. The white men yelled back and waved at the car, then grouped before the arched stone entrance of the gates to the city.
Did they resent her riding in a motor car? Did they resent her being chosen to go on the yacht? Well, she resented them for having a whole day before them in this colorful, historical place. Not that the history and color would be much appreciated by her brothers. In other words, the Wax Museum wouldn't be on their list of places to visit. They would probably head straight for the river, which Merry Gay could see shining like a silver ribbon on her left, and pitch rocks. Well, they might go to the museum just to poke fun at the wax figures-whatever was beyond them was beneath them. 
Never had Merry Gay seen so many flowers, rainbows of flowers, triangles of flowers, circles of pure color that made her long to wade barefoot through just for the velvety feel and not to crush them, though that would be a luxury too.  She had to memorize each color and fragrance for Tommy, find a way to convey the blends of colors and smell, to make her see. Though in truth, Merry Gay preferred the acid tang of the damp packed sand of her home-place. She liked the shade of the chinaberry trees, umbrellas of lacy green leaves that yielded tiny hard berries for the chickens to peck. And there was nothing more beautiful to her than the mimosas at Moniac Crossing with their layered ferny fronds and poufs of sheer pink flowers like birds' crests.
About as unlike as twins can be, the Eltons, at a crossing up the street, were standing, one in a spinsterish dark dress with standup collar buttoned high on her neck, and the other in a soft pale blouse and striped pink skirt. They were hooking each other's arms and their picnic baskets, trying to get up the nerve to cross between the clip-clopping horse-drawn buggies, surreys and pony carts, which were commonplace and therefore possible to safely cross in front of, and the motor cars happening up like dark hulks in a bad dream, which they'd had little experience with, save for the Shirley's car that seldom left the port alongside the big white house, except when one of the Shirley men got tanked up on a Saturday night and spun snakes and cut ruts along the lane before the Eltons' safe and solid front porch facing the tracks of Moniac Crossing. The spinsterish twin, the teacher, would request prayer for them on Sunday at the Baptist church. Safe too from the revenge of the Shirleys at the Methodist Church, unless Nate Shirley made a surprise visit on the off-chance that Merry Gay Rose might be singing at the morning service. It was like that man absolutely craved that girl's singing, they said, where any normal Christian would listen and let it slide on into the sermon. 
The baseball team from Macon was walking, two by two, boys with their canvas bags of balls and bats slung over their shoulders. Out of her family's view, at last, Priss stood and began blowing kisses at them. She was decked out in white linen with dainty embroidered blue flowers; the blue grosgrain ribbon gathering back her long blond hair had long been shed because Merry Gay, her sixteen-year-old idol, wasn't wearing one.
The boys waved and walked on. The cars turned east and trailed up the arched wooden bridge over the waterway, a magnificent height and feel of flying out into the hazy blue yonder. Later, Merry Gay might tell Tommy that it was like swinging on the tree swing when it reached its pitch, when you could see only sky and your heart leaped up with mixed thrill and fear. 
She would never tell Tommy this. She would never get the chance.
Priss's fair-skinned face was blistered by the time they reached the dock where the cream and walnut yacht sat swaying on the lit green water. Already, finely dressed couples and grouped ladies and men were chatting on the upper deck behind the railing of the fat but sleek boat, holding crystal glasses of rain-water yellow drinks out before them like something that had to be held in order to talk.
Mary Gay was hot and sweaty, her yellow cotton dress soiled, limp and wrinkled from the train ride and the wallowing of the Shirley children. Where a song usually bubbled up in her throat there was a lump. A big bumpy frog ready to leap onto her quivering heart like a lily pad.
"I can't sing," she said to Priss in the back seat while her younger sisters and cousins were unloaded and spit-shined by her mother and aunt.
Priss's queenly mother Sara was wan with pearl-colored hair and thin lips, and was wearing a dark belted skirt and a pleated crepe blouse to match her hair. The two cherub toddler girls in the front seat belonged to her. The other daughter-in-law of Nate Shirley, Muffin, Jay's wife, had one baby on her hip and another in her belly. She wore her black hair upswept in a soft nest with a small bun in the center. No smile, no nonsense. Her wide inky eyes and rouged cheeks gave her a hard coarse look, the opposite of Priss's mother. But puffed up with progeny and pride, she made a good Shirley.
Merry Gay suspected the Shirleys had hired Tommy to keep house because of her being high-yellow and clean and having a quaint name. Something the wealthy class, in Merry Gay's opinion, took stock in; only the very rich and the very poor bestowed nicknames. One of the Shirley grandchildren was called Cupcake; her mother was called Muffin. There was a Lamb and a Skippy in the family, where west-side of the tracks lived a Negro child called Piggy and a young woman called Honeybee. Tommy had told Merry Gay it was funny how "quality" did that, adding how "funny" it was too that they wanted big fine houses and fancy clothes, but had to get poor white trash and niggers, who they looked down on, to take care of it all. Funniest of all, to Merry Gay, was Tommy's choice of words. They'd been taught that "nigger" and "colored" were classes. Like "white trash" and "white." Simply said, niggers and white-trash were dirty and lazy; coloreds and whites were clean and smart. The label "nigger" loosely used was a sign of ignorance and meanness. When had Tommy dropped such distinctions?
"What you mean, you can't sing?" Priss squinched her blue eyes against the sun. 
"Not in front of all these people." Not in front of him and all these people, was what Merry Gay really meant. This very minute Nate Shirley could be telling them she was better than Enrica Caruso, the great opera tenor, and they would laugh when they heard her sing. 
"You sing all the time?you used to," Priss said. "At church I mean."
"Not like this. Not for this type of people."
"You told Papa Shirley you would. I came just to see them when they hear you."
"But they're different, they?"
"Well, they eat just like you and me. They probably fart like Papa Shirley."
"Priss!" her mother scolded, reaching inside the car to drag her out.
Merry Gay swallowed the frog and felt a bubble of air in her gut, which she felt later while she sang would surely be expelled as gas.
What seemed peculiar to Merry Gay and Priss was the yacht never leaving the dock where it was moored at St. Augustine Beach. Maybe somebody else noticed, but nobody appeared to think it odd. Their loud talking was like hand clapping up close, but out by the railing of the yacht on the upper deck, where Merry Gay and Priss sat to eat and watch the sun-glade out over the twinkling green water make a path to the dusky seam where water and sky meet, the sea breeze and waves sounded like cupping their hands over their ears, and the cry of gulls ruled. Flocks of white birds skimmed the bothered water, their alarming cries the only sign that possibly a hurricane was brewing beyond the rounding-off of sky, water and light, some little spasm of wind and rain making up and spreading from that seam of dusk.
The girls' plates were heaped with chilled chicken salad, tiny buttery green peas and dainty squares of gingerbread. Woozy from the swaying of the train, then the motor car and now the boat, they refused the steamed shrimp, its smell too akin to the fish stench of the soupy gray oyster bays each side of the dock leading out to the yacht offshore.
They were both blistered. Their faces felt like the mean hot globes of the Aladdin lamps at home. They fished ice chunks from their glasses of sweet tea and pressed them to their cheeks. The other women looked cool under their parasols and straw shelters of wax-ribboned hats, some floppy with broad brims and others flat and rigid like a man's. Checks of sunlight played on their powered faces. Their children had been left on the lower deck with two hired women, to be fed, entertained, and put down for naps. 
These women were slim and lovely in pastels of tiered voile and banded-lace skirts that flared at the hem or swept the deck with a hint of train. Narrow shoulders, sleeves full and soft, closed at the wrists by snug buttoned cuffs. When did bustles go out of style? Merry Gay wondered, feeling hers bunch under her buttocks like a pillow. She would sit as long as she could. 
On the edge of the mingling group, Bernice stood smiling as always, like a gap had been streaked into her lower face, while Miss Moxie gestured to her with a glass of champagne, smoking cigarettes, talking and laughing. Bernice's bunions pressed against the sides of her shoes, slim as a cobbler's last, and every time she shifted feet the jewels flashed like mirrors in the bright sun, glancing off the polished wood walls to the galley and the deck.
Miss Moxie had ordered those slim jewel-studded cream-leather heeled shoes from Paris; then commissioned Bernice to break them in for her. How pinched her feet looked, to Merry Gay, how pained her expression, how odd those shoes worn with her latest homemade two-piece cotton frock, a black print stripe, that smelled sharp of dye and had to be hot! Merry Gay's yellow cotton dress had been fashioned from the same pattern as her mother's, except cut-down, slimmer, and the gathers of the skirt sucked in where Bernice's pooched out. But for their bustles.
 The bunion humps on the sides of Bernice's big toes had already imprinted themselves into the supple leather. For the last longest, she had been sewing Miss Moxie a cream silk dress, laden with gold braid and tassels by the pound, for her to wear with the shoes when she came out of mourning. Both women were wearing black, both in mourning for lost husbands. Both had bunions in the same exact location-how come Bernice to be commissioned to break in those shoes. But that was all they had in common. 
Bernice was blond and had the spread, bony hips of a brood cow, and her bunions caused her to step heavy side to side. Her head trembled and she smiled whether happy or sad, a tic likely formed from her determination to keep smiling regardless. But it was as if that smile had hardened like wax during those years of making do and trying to train her children up in the ways they should go. It worked with Tommy. It worked with Merry Gay, big-boned, blond and sunny and blessed with the gift of song, but not her hell-raising older brothers.
Flashing crooked discolored teeth, Miss Moxie raised her glass to Bernice as if to toast her. At Miss Moxie's throat was a black Cameo mourning brooch that served as focal point for poor captive Bernice. 
"Baptists don't drink, do they?" Priss asked Merry Gay.
Merry Gay laughed. "Not out in public, no."
"You know why your mother was really asked to come along?" Priss's long blond hair was whipping at the small gingerbread and cream cheese sandwich in her right hand.
"I think I do."
"To keep Gramma Moxie from looking lonely and like nobody loves her."
 Merry Gay bit into her gingerbread, knowing it would soon be time to sing and her small scalloped teeth would look brown and rotten. Her uneven little-girl's teeth were all that saved her from being too pretty-maybe beautiful-but that didn't stop her from smiling. Priss had been right about why Bernice had been invited, she thought. She could see that now. Also it was clear to her now that her mother hadn't hired herself out as companion to Miss Moxie for the money; and as for those shoes, she wouldn't have rented out her poor damaged feet to break in those shoes for Nate Shirley's mother even if her family was starving.
Bernice had come along for Merry Gay, to keep her company, to look out for her and cheer in case nobody else should cheer her singing. In case her daughter couldn't sing as good as her own family thought. She loved to hear Merry Gay sing, but felt that her daddy shouldn't have encouraged her in something so impractical, which could only be used for church and only up to a certain age. More lasting and of greater value were those things that all women had to know to get by: caring for a family, cooking, sewing, learning the ways of men so she could cull a dependable, kind husband from the lot.
"Gramma looks like a crow. We don't even like her."
"Priss!" But she did look like a crow in all that black. Staring Priss straight in the face, Merry Gay could see her fine symmetrical features beginning to pinch into her mother's sharp face, to lose that soft glib girlishness. Then Priss would smile or say something clever or so honest and insightful it made Mary Gay sit up straight with sudden understanding and she would know that Priss's beauty would always shine, even through the skin of her mother's pinched face. Priss would always be Priss followed by an exclamation point because she would speak outright and with the rapt detachment of a spoiled child. But she wasn't spoiled.
Priss tried to walk like Merry Gay; she tried to talk like Merry Gay, to sing like Merry Gay. She would never be creamy, soft and buxom like Merry Gay, but she had grown as tall as Merry Gay in the past year. The added inches in height seemed to have come from her body's narrowing in. Straight up and down with no hips or breasts, yet beginning to push out in places like the moon filling in till it reached half-full, then filling out till it got whole.
Nathan Shirley, in a blinding-white starched shirt with stiff collar and cuffs, was laughing with his sons and a tall blond man, all in charcoal striped trousers and solid charcoal vests. Jay was short and stocky with that unique Shirley skull that was too perfectly rounded in back and stopped short of the nape like a helmet. Wendell stood tall as his daddy, the spitting image of his daddy. Both had the olive skin and thinning pale brown hair of their father, regularly trimmed and scrub-faced, but Wendell was apparent heir to the Shirley throne. Wendell rode the turpentine woods with him; Wendell went where his daddy went, a weak shadow. Jay slept and drank and gambled and often went missing. Nobody seemed to worry, except Priss, who tried to keep her entire family in place and in line.
The blond man laughed and slapped Nathan on the shoulder. Then he stepped up on a platform at the bow of the yacht and began clinking his fork on his glass. The American flag was flying high in the wind behind him, a swift snappy wind that swept smooth and steady across the deck. Merry Gay and Priss had been introduced to him earlier; his name was Roland Mason and he was owner of the yacht. Up close his teeth were thick and white, looked false as his smile. The line of his lips blurred into his tanned freckled face. His hair was the texture of straw and more straw-colored than blond. The laughter and talk faded out to mumbles and the murmuring of the sea and waves slapping underneath the boat. 
"Is everybody having fun?" he called out.
"Yes," they called back, laughing, cheering.
"Good, good. Don't forget there's plenty of that good St. Augustine shrimp left, so nobody leaves without seconds."
Much shuffling of shoe soles on the waxed deck, but mostly everybody was drinking. No wonder there was shrimp left on the buffet in the galley; they probably hadn't been touched. A waiter in a white suit passed with a round silver platter heaped with ice and shrimp and spicy tomato sauce in crystal dishes. Another passed with a sweating bottle of pale wine coddled in a white towel, and all held out their glasses for refills.
The straw man on the platform at the bow of the boat struck up his tune again on the glass. Ting! Ting! "By now most of you have probably met my good friends the Shirleys, my guests from Georgia. Nate Shirley's a buddy of mine from our Princeton days."
"Go Princeton!" A dandy in loden trousers, with a British accent and a fancy cane, raised his glass. It flashed on the walls and deck, dancing with the water-lights from below and the glancing, winking jewels of Miss Moxie's shoes on poor Bernice's feet. 
"Another Princeton man." Roland Mason beamed. "You folks might like to know that good ole Nate here brought to our fair city his entire turpentine village. They're out painting the town red this minute, so be careful where you step when you start home."
Everybody whooped and laughed. Somebody slapped Nathan Shirley on the shoulder. Twin spots of rouge on her face, like sunsets, Muffin flopped onto the bench along the blade shadow of the east galley wall. The black butterfly tie at the throat of her white blouse had flown.
The man on the platform dinged his glass again. "Okay, okay, pipe down. I've got a special treat for you. Nate brought along some good ole down-home Georgia entertainment. Little cracker gal, doubles as au pair for his grandchildren, sings like a songbird, he says. Goes by the name of Gay Merry."
Everybody laughed, swaying with the boat and balancing their drinks. Somebody said, "Shit!" Somebody else said, "Shush!"
Merry Gay, still seated along the railing overlooking the ocean and rinsing her teeth by sloshing tea between them, swallowed it and got strangled. Her face was a red she could see.
"Her name is Merry Gay Rose." Priss stood, almost shouting it, parting her blowing hair from her pink face. 
The crowd of adults slowly turned, eyeing the sassy child. Her mother, fanning and sweating and all but passed out from the heat and the wine, said, "Priss, nobody asked you."
Nate Shirley was pacing the deck below the platform with one finger over his smiling lips and an elbow resting in the other cupped hand. Smug-looking, object of attention-he could take a joke.
"I didn't get it," Merry Gay whispered to Priss on her left.
"It's something crass. Maybe dirty," she whispered. "They're picking at Papa Shirley, they do it all the time. Probably nobody else got it either, most of them, but they'll make like they did to keep from looking stupid. Boring's what they are."
"Okay, folks," the buddy on the platform said. "It was only a good-natured joke among friends, I assure you. We all know that Shirley here will debate until he's blue in the face that art deserves a higher place than science. In no way does what I said reflect badly on Nate's little discovery.
