Let's make Miss Merry Gay Rose welcome, hear?" He stepped down, adding, "And eat some of that shrimp, please." 
Everybody clapped who didn't have a drink in their hands: Nate Shirley, Priss and Bernice, who looked on the verge of a stroke but was still smiling.
"Show em, Merry Gay, show em!" Priss hissed.
Merry Gay started to leave. The first reference was the true one or it wouldn't have been in the head of Nate Shirley's friend to start with. To use her to tease Nate Shirley, to boost their entertainment and egos, was cruel and unnecessary and she was doubly insulted by Roland Mason's attempt to correct it. She was the possum in the potato salad at an otherwise forgettable picnic. She should get up and leave by the stairs to the lower deck and out to the dock to wait for the Moniac crew. They could carry on here without her. But, no, cracker child that she was, she could sing and she could prove it.
 She had never before used her voice for revenge, but had often used withholding her voice for revenge. If not for money and not for revenge, then why had she agreed to sing for Nate Shirley this time, on this cruise? She hadn't even planned what to sing, had hardly given it a thought. It was as if she had planned not to sing after fooling him into believing she would sing, making up some lame excuse like she'd been doing since her daddy died last year. Not because she felt guilty for giving what was best of herself, what her daddy treasured most. Nathan Shirley had weakened himself by letting her know how valuable her singing was to him. He, himself, had pointed out her power over him, her only power. As long as she could sing, he wouldn't damage what he craved. He wouldn't bring her and what was left of her family down like everybody else he had ruined. Like wives she had heard about who control their husbands by withholding their loving, she was withholding her voice to make herself more desirable, to get back at Shirley for destroying her daddy, the Great Hoss Rose, and just because it felt good.
She had to walk through the crowd, Priss at her side and holding to her hand. The bustle made her feel that she was toeing in, sticking out her butt. What she should do was sing off key, screech out a silly song. But she had too much pride for that, and besides she was bound to control Nate Shirley, even if in the end he might bite her too. She could smell stale rose perfume and sweat mixing with the salt air. Her hair felt like rope blowing against her neck and cheeks.
She dropped Priss's hand and lifting her skirt stepped up on the platform, staring each and all square in the eye, fearing she must look ready to spit, but trying for tolerance, even pity. She would like to make them think she didn't care, that they didn't faze her. But that would be unbelievable and she would trip herself up if she tried. "Eat the shrimp, please," she said and smiled. From the crowd came a chuckle or two, but those soon faded when nobody else joined them.
Nate Shirley, standing below her, had his hands in his trouser pockets, proud and sly and so sure they would love her that he couldn't resist turning to check the expressions on their faces for before-and-after comparison. 
Merry Gay stretched as tall as she could, sucking in the tainted air with a prayer. And without another word she opened her mouth and let fly the choicest, sweetest, longest notes ever to the onset of "The Star Spangled Banner." She couldn't say why that song, unless it was inspired by the American flag snapping in the hot wind at her back, its rope maybe preserved with Moniac Crossing's very own tar. The boat itself likely treated with her own pines' pitch-she could smell it now, cleansing but roily in the heat. Maybe she had picked this song because it was difficult to sing, because it seemed to fit and was a song she knew, and it felt satisfyingly spiteful; though she doubted Nathan Shirley would catch on that she was mocking his having hung an American flag above the top balcony of the depot and commissary after playing dictator to the Masons and the town by laying claim to their meeting place.
 She sang the song so piercing and clear that her own skin prickled, cherishing each distinct word of the lyrics sure to haunt memory, yet with her unique inflection and an occasional quirk to remind her audience that she was indeed a cracker child, the best they'd ever heard or ever would hear again, cracker or not. While she sang her eyes switched from her mother, to Priss, to the waiter with the platter of shrimp nobody wanted. Then fixed on the sunglade out over the ocean, that golden gathering of traveling light, water and song.
Her mother was smiling for real now, nodding at Merry Gay with her back to Miss Moxie, who looked like a smoking black stove flue.
When Merry Gay was done singing, when the final notes played out like a crystal clapper on a silver bell, she bowed and stepped down, the applause and pleas for one more song, just one more, the sweetest sound she'd ever heard. And the sweetest sight? The chest-strutting, sidling-up-to-plead of her father's enemy, Nathan Shirley, who couldn't afford another song.

***

It was between four and five o'clock when the three black motor cars returned the Moniac yacht guests to the St. Augustine train depot. Days were getting shorter and dusk would soon claim the sun burning behind the halted black train and the soot-smudged, yellow clapboard station with dark green and wine trim in the foreground. The station had stacked turrets along the front and a slate roof like turned clay. A rack of navy clouds, made darker by the facing sun, hovered in the east, moving up like night. Merry Gay longed for home and the smell of dirt and the rain if it came. The smell of shrimp was lodged in her nose and the ruins of the oyster bays would stick in her mind so that forever-after no matter who came home with a croaker sack of fresh oysters in the shell to roast over hot coals, she wouldn't be eating them. 
The train would pull out for Moniac Crossing in about thirty minutes and already the engine was building up steam, northwest along the tracks, men piling on coal in preparation for the return trip, the arm of the bellied water tower lowered over the spout of the boiler.
From the rear seat of the third motor car in line before the depot, Merry Gay could see the Negro and white Moniac men milling on the loading dock running in an ell along the south end of the depot station to the area between the tracks and the station. The ticket taker, conductor and turnstile keeper began shouting and waving their arms, wading into the midst of the cheering men, parting them and exposing a Negro man and a white man locked in a tussle, gouging and socking when they could work their hands free, but mostly riding atop each other and rolling on the dock.  
Hell bent on restoring order, Shirley and his sons were out of the front motor car immediately, walking fast with their arms swinging, Nate Shirley with his fists clenched. His face was as blistered from the sun on the yacht as Priss's and Merry Gay's.
Out of their car now too, Priss and Merry Gay lingered along the freight-loading ramp to the left of the station with the other spectators, trying to see into the crowd of men who had closed the gap on the scuffle. The other women, including Bernice and Miss Moxie, went straight inside the turret lobby of the station with the children to use the lavatory and bathe their hot salty faces, but mostly to prevent exposing themselves to the indelicate matters among men. 
All the racket of the city seemed concentrated on and around the platform. Shouting, cursing, wild men's laughing and hooting.
Merry Gay would have bet that she knew at least one of the men fighting-Buck. She could see Charlie, bloated and numb-looking, leaning up against a railing on the north end of the loading dock. Next to him, Will Thomas was puking over the railing into the sooty ballast and oil-sopped earth. 
The two tussling men were parted and on their feet; Merry Gay could make out that the white man was for sure Buck and the Negro man was the Shirleys' main woods-rider. They looked like mad dogs with hackles raised on their backs. The smell of vomit and alcohol and soot rose on the hot damp air aided by the steam of the engine. The noise was so dense it blended, all sound just one major racket, indistinguishable, unbearable as a sawmill.
Nate Shirley was quarreling with the ticket taker and conductor, whose faces looked sick-green under their visors. Like Shirley's boiled face if he'd been wearing a red visor.
Merry Gay and Priss edged closer along the freight-loading ramp, but carefully steering clear of the drunken men, trying to hear what Priss's grandfather was saying to the men in charge of the train.
Buck, sloe-eyed drunk, staggered over to the north ell of the dock where Charlie in that isolated melancholic stance characteristic of the half-blind was leaned up and grinning with his teeth parted, gazing at the sky through his thick glasses as if seeing glory. He was wont to gaze up and grin like that, making you wonder whether he didn't have some changing scene to distract him or some rosy illusion captured in the dense lens. The hood of dark clouds in the east was coming together overhead with the fading blue sky in the west, a faint line dividing day from night, like the line between ocean and sky, like the line encircling the moon.
 Back to the crowd, Buck straddled his legs, undid his fly, and began pissing between the spokes of the railing, Charlie on his left and Will on his right. In a flash, Buck and Will were hugged up like lovers.
On the siding between the train and the loading dock, a Negro Pullman with a flatcar load of baggage in route to the baggage car near the head of the train stopped to watch them, thumbing his billed beret up on his forehead for a clearer view.  
Buck and Will waltzed over to Charlie, bumping into him and jarring him loose from his stupor. Slowly, grinning, he began feinting side to side, knees spread and feet apart, while reaching inside his pants pocket and pulling out his pocketknife. Thumbing open the blade on his knife, he began hopping toward Will and then Buck, no longer joined and on their own, as if to gut them. Finally Buck spun behind Charlie and caught him in a headlock, then a couple of men blocked Merry Gay and Priss's view; and when the men moved, the girls could see Buck and Charlie with their arms overlaid on each other's shoulders, buddies again, drinking from the same brown bottle of whiskey. Will was vomiting again.
Priss started laughing. Then Merry Gay.
Next thing, Buck and Charlie were dancing; they danced through the heart of the crowd and up to Nate and Wendell Shirley, standing next to the ticket taker at the turnstile near the center of the dock railing. They were collecting the white men's pistols and ordering them aside, shoving the Negroes on through the turnstile, down the dock steps, and up and onto the train. Single file, they stumbled right and into the smoking car. 
The white men began shouting, hooting, jeering. "Hey! What the hell you mean letting them niggers load up ahead of us?"
"Yeah, what the hell?"
"Somebody's got some explaining to do. Right, Shirley?"
Merry Gay noted that the main protestors were of the Moniac old families who'd been hoodooed out of their land by Shirley and his boys. Shirley's hired men, the ones who came with him from Macon, and those who he later hired on, were moving out away from the protestors to keep from being associated and losing their jobs. As the protestors were bound to do. Drunk as most of them were, they appeared sober enough to know this and to not give a damn.
Even the baseball team, who had been on the train that morning when the Moniac folk had boarded for the day-long excursion, had wandered onto the loading dock unnoticed by Merry Gay and Priss. They were cursing and shouting like the men from Moniac.
"Are the baseball players drunk too?" Priss asked Merry Gay. Her blue eyes looked bluer in her blistered face.
"I don't know." Merry Gay looked all around. "What happened to your Uncle Jay?"
"Oh, no! He's probably slipped off downtown to drink and gamble.  Aunt Muffin's gonna have a fit if he misses the train home."
At last, Wendell and Nate Shirley and the conductor and ticket taker began filing the loyal white Shirley men through the turnstile. In rebellion some of the Moniac protestors leaped over the railing next to the turnstile. The men in charge turned and shouted for them to board the train. They sailed up the steps, still shouting, mistakenly staggering into the left car, instead of the right. In short order, they emerged, herded like cattle from the ladies' coach by a robust shouting steward, across the brief coach connector and into the smoking car. The sun shining through backlit their bodies as they scurried past. The men on the dock, who'd remained loyal to Shirley, and those pushing through the turnstile, laughed but their laughter soon changed to shouting and cursing at the Moniac gang.
Again the train crew separated the men, this time with clubs striking the heads and backs of a couple of the protestors. In a few minutes, they scrambled in line behind Shirley's men passing through the turnstile, cursing and puking their way through and onto the train and into the smoking car.
The women and children came out the rear exit of the station and stood on the dock, watching and waiting for the men to finish boarding the train. Priss's mother Sara and her aunt Muffin were fanning with cardboard fans and fussing with the younger children. Sara's blond head wagged side to side, searching for Priss who she'd maybe thought had followed her into the station. Miss Moxie, tall and withered-looking from the sun and wine on the yacht, tripped while walking across the dock and the other women gathered round to help her up. Her face was beyond blistered, peeled red. Bernice was carrying her ridiculous jewel-studded shoes as if no longer on payroll-given in, given up, to hell with the old grouch.  
After the men had boarded, including the Shirley men, and the steward had closed top and bottom sections of the door to the smoking car, the women and children were allowed through the turnstile and escorted up through puddles of vomited sweet-and-sour whiskey and briny urine. They had to hold the children in their arms while lifting the hems of their skirts to keep them from trailing in the mess. Wearily, Bernice sat on the single train step and tugged on Miss Moxie's shoes again. Then she stood, climbing up, and tramped purposely on through the vomit and into the ladies' coach.
When Priss and Merry Gay boarded, the racket in the smoking car had died out to talking, even laughing. Nate Shirley's senatorial drawl could be heard in scolding blunt speech.
"Papa Shirley!" Priss giggled.
Minus the scolding tone, it was the same voice he'd used to lecture Merry Gay on "applying methods of analysis to problems of ethics." Some book entitled Principia Ethica which he was reading and said he would pass along to her when he got through. So far, she supposed, he hadn't finished it and she as glad. She'd had to look up each word in the dictionary at school to figure out what he was talking about, and in her opinion if somebody had to study on fair dealings and doing what was right there was a good chance he either had a bad upbringing or didn't have good sense or wouldn't be apt to change just because some writer had worded it a different way. 
Going through the door of the ladies' coach, they met Bernice, looking panicky, about to exit again. "You girls, what's going on? Where's the boys?"  
"They all got in a squabble over something," Merry Gay said, "but Buck and Charlie are fine." 
"Just showing themselves, Mama Bee," Priss said, patting her arm.
Bernice, shaking her head and mumbling, turned to go to her seat. "Lose their jobs if they don't look out, then we'll be in a fix." At the rear of the coach, she took her place next to Miss Moxie, in the midst of the Shirley women. Sara stood, shaking a finger at Priss, then sat again. A baby cried out to a sleepy whine, then silence inside the coach. Outside the train whistle sounded its brassy bell and bright toot and lurched forward with hasty chuffing.
Merry Gay and Priss took the front seats on the right. Priss, aisle-side, placed her head back on the cushioned rest and closed her eyes. "Mama's gonna skin my hide," Priss said and sighed.
"You shouldn't have been out there, hearing and seeing such." Merry Gay at the open window batted her eyes against the grit and steam, waves of hot and cool air and shadows of the rectangular cars on the walls of buildings and the cobbled side streets. Two boys in hickory-stripe shirts and short britches were standing underneath the pot-bellied water tower, throwing rocks at the train. She waved to them. One reared and aimed a rock at her window. Too late. The train was gone, chuffing and chugging toward home.
Her throat was raw from singing-octaves higher than she had ever dreamed of singing. She smiled and closed her eyes, remembering the guests on the yacht begging for one more song, then coming up to her in earnest searching to know her, Nate Shirley's discovery, his cracker-gal songbird.
When she opened her eyes, she could see through her window the thick green woods accented by the orange sun striking the treetops. Dusk was gathering in the vine-swagged woods, then golden light out in the openings of crossroads and fields. The train had veered west from its northwesterly course and if the storm clouds were following Merry Gay couldn't see them. She didn't want to see them, in spite of their needing rain in North Florida and South Georgia. That need was announced by the tart cool air overlaid with smothery heat, by the dust she could see through her window chasing a mule-drawn wagonload of dry ear corn. 
All was quiet in the ladies' coach, except for the rhythmic clanking of the train, the jostling motion rocking everybody to sleep. 
Three seats behind and to the left of Merry Gay and Priss, Miss Ellie, knotting her handkerchief, was seated aisle-side of her bossy sister, the teacher, whose stern pulled-down face was tilted toward the window, projecting a closed-to-conversation look. You could bet she wasn't asleep, maybe on the verge and studying up some scheme to make somebody miserable. Probably Miss Ellie, eager to get home and see to her deformed grown boy left in the charge of a hired Negro woman for the day.
Down the line, crisscrossing sides: two girls, about Priss's age, were leaning, heads together, sweetly sleeping; the little boy with the buckeye charm was folded over on his mother's lap while she smoothed his hair from his un-sunned forehead. The Shirley women, Sarah and Muffin, looked elegant in their weariness, peaceful at last with their children sleeping.
Behind them sat Miss Tillie Knight, a widow friend of Merry Gay's mother, who sat behind Miss Tillie with the widow Miss Moxie. A mystery, so many widows at the Crossing, waiting more than living, having waited on husbands all their lives. Graded-down status, even with plenty of work for the widows to do; plenty of women and children in their families to help make-do. Were the women blessed or cursed by having outlived their husbands? 
When one of the widows had died recently, at the age of 88, her family dividing up her belongings had found a small-denomination confederate bill beneath a tin cake can of meager coins. Written across the bill in her steady younger hand, was the message, "Save all my children and grandchildren." Her "A's" shaped like "O's"-confusing cursive. The bill not even creased by changing hands, she'd saved it for them so long. Or was it a prayer written on the most expensive paper she had handy? And how ironic that after her holding onto the bill all that time it had become worthless as money, made worthless by time and war.
Picking from the lot of women on the homebound train-wilted hats and empty picnic baskets in the aisle-Merry Gay noted a couple more widows in various stages of sleep or drawing on memories banked in their nodding heads. Had they had fun today, or were they merely drained, she wondered? Maybe wishing they'd stayed home. 
She wondered if Tommy was upset about missing the trip to St. Augustine. Or if, as Merry Gay had earlier thought, she had stayed home deliberately to avoid being graded by race as to where she would have had to ride on the train. Would she be angry with her husband Will, who never drank, for getting drunk? 
And what was going on in the smoking car this very minute? It sounded peaceful enough, but she doubted that. She doubted this seeming stillness and peace extended throughout the entire train. Peace in which to sort the memories of the day. Later, memories of the day would be banked in Merry Gay's mind as better times: before her living nightmare began and everything else would be after.


CHAPTER 3

What started out as a dare turned into disaster, that hazy August morning, when little Merry Gay and Tommy smeared themselves black with chimney soot. All but for the creases behind their straight girl-knees was black as night, black as the skin of a sun-blackened turpentine hand. Except that the girls' palms and soles were black too. Hand prints on the door led out of the parlor. Bare foot prints on the hall floor led to the porch, and on the porch floor leading to the steps, down the red brick walk to the picket gate as they made their way to the commissary across the tracks to surprise Hoss and make him laugh and play with them like he used to. 
They found him straddled his stool behind the counter, swapping notions with a man on the other side who had little bitty ears and a fat head. Actually, it was like his ears had sprouted out the sides of his head, mere blossoms in the act of unfolding. 
Big laugh, and Hoss said, "Yeah, and I can just hear Bernice when I tell her some ole head doc in Austria says it's good for my health to act out my unconscious desires." 
Grinning, the man leaned into the counter, cocking one heavy leg.   
Hoss looked at the girls in the doorway, sun raying around them, black as night, black as two sun-blackened piccaninies. One, teeny as a tree frog, but with enormous hair; the other, slightly pudgy, blond hair gone pewter with soot and straight as string. Their faded yellow-print sack shifts were soot-smudged, slept-in. "What the??" he said.
The girls whooped, stamping their feet. "We got you! We fooled you!"
Hoss laughed, but his heart wasn't in it. "My girls there, always trying to pull stuff on me," he said to the man who was pushing his glasses in place on his nose and smiling at the girls indifferently. 
Hoss reached over into a round glass jar of candy, fished out four pieces of horehound and passed it across the counter to the girls. "I ain't in no mood; you girls run on along now. Your mama gets back from Aunt Lonnie's, she's gonna take a switch to y'all."
A gentle giant, bald and bright, with thick blond eyebrows accenting his lit blue eyes, he looked eager to be rid of them and the man too. With Bernice gone to her sister's in Folkston, he was lost. He could handle the turpentine woods, the coloreds and the commissary, but the slack-less business of tending children and cooking and cleaning was as alien to his nature as preaching. Buck and Charlie, he didn't even bother to keep track of. The girls made him crazy, looking to him for food, clothes and fun.
"Lighten up, loosen up, live!" he would tell Bernice when she griped about having to do it all herself. From across the tracks, at the commissary, Bernice's household had seemed to practically run it self. One chore spinning off another-breakfast, dishes, laundry, dinner, dishes, children, supper, dishes. Now the house was a wreck and the cow hadn't been milked in two days. He couldn't wait to hand over the girls to Bernice again and go back to being daddy-fun, funny and closed to complaints and favors.
The girls started out of the store with their candy.
"Hey," Hoss called after them, "don't y'all go back in the house with that smut on you, hear? Go down to the creek and wash up."
Tommy looked at Merry Gay going down the porch steps. "To the creek! Mama's gonna die if she finds out."
"Don't tell her," Merry Gay said, sucking on her candy. "Don't tell her he tried to sweet-talk that hussy into cleaning up the house either."
Leaf smoke was on the air, children's laughter arching over the Crossing, dogs barking and women calling. Sweet-tart candy on their tongues, the girls ambled on back across the tracks, a little afraid of going to the creek without their mother, a lot afraid of not doing what their daddy had ordered.
 For a fact, Bernice would switch them for smearing themselves with soot, but she would whip them too for going to the creek by themselves. Hoss wouldn't whip them either way, but he might turn them over to the woman their mama had called a hussy. Then all hell would break loose when Bernice got home. One thing was clear, and getting clearer, they had to wash off the soot.  
They were at that point beginning to feel silly; half the town had seen them and come down on them for making a mess. Plus, the soot made their skin itch and draw like alum. Women on their porches threatened to take a switch to them. Another woman sweeping leaves shamed them for disgracing Bernice, who was off doing her Christian duty by seeing to her sick sister. Even the children, the girls' former playmates, threw sticks and sicked their dogs on them.
In summer, they never wore shoes, except when they went to church or had to walk with Bernice the long half-mile to the creek. This time they had no shoes, hadn't even seen their shoes since the day Bernice left for Folkston, a day's ride from Moniac Crossing along the same dusty, winding, rutted road they were now walking. Soles so blistered from the hot white sand they had to walk on the sides of their feet. To walk in the straddles of dead grass or along the weed-choked road shoulders was to invite snakes to bite their black feet. Ahead and all around, nothing but gauzy trees in the sun-haze, baked weeds and the clicking of dry grasshoppers now and then hopping from the road shoulders to the wagon-tracked strips of dirt. Smells of pine tar and wood smoke from a wild fire rumored to be burning in the nearby Okefenokee Swamp. It had been burning most of the summer and would continue to burn till the fall rains set in.
 Alone now, just two little black sisters, they could hear coming up on them from behind wagon wheels trundling and the musical jangling of pots and pans. They were getting set to cross down to the creek embedded in woods on the right through a patch of bright yellow Brown-eyed Susans when the mule-drawn rolling store appeared around the last curve. 
The top-heavy body of the wagon rocked over ruts, and when it stopped all that was stored behind the lift-up walls along the sides jangled on like children in a rhythm band were playing inside.  The tall, skinny man on the bench of the wagon was wearing a tweed wool suit and a gray bowler hat, the brim of which pinned his drab black curly hair down over his ears. He looked like a scarecrow. 
"Purty morning for a walk, ain't it girls?" he called down in a mock-friendly crackled voice, still holding to the lead-lines. The gray mule drooped, belly, back and eyes. He looked asleep.
The girls nodded yes, twisting their bodies but with bare feet planted in the yellow flowers that made them look blacker. They'd never seen this man before but his being a stranger wasn't the problem. It was the fun-making tone of his voice, and he was ugly, next-to-the-ugliest man they had ever seen, which involved them in a way that beauty could not, being there was so much to mentally fix and cause them to feel superior, yet sorry for him: his black eyes drooped like the mule's, black beard scattered over a long jaw. His teeth were crooked and rotten and squeezed into a narrow horseshoe shape so that it was impossible for him to close his mouth. Pale skin dingy with smoke and dirt, plus he had the rounded fingertips of a thief. 
"Y'alles' mammy know where y'all off to?"
They only stood there, but reached for each other's hands.
"How old are you, sister?" he asked Merry Gay.
"Nine," she said. "Almost ten."
"And you, little girl?" he said to Tommy, who looked tinier with her wild fat hair.
"She's nine too," Merry Gay said, not waiting for Tommy to answer. They were out of candy and Tommy was gnawing on a finger to make do. 
The man took off his hat, fanning with it. Wiggling his thin crooked nose and sniffing the air as he stared off at the woods. "Y'all wouldn't happen to know where a fellow could get some cool water round here, would you?" His voice rose high, then low, crackling. He reached down to the floor of the wagon and lifted a round crock jug with a flat bottom and shook it. "Fresh out," he said.
"Creek's down the bank there," Merry Gay said. "Spring bubbles out of the ground on this side."
He leaped from the wagon with his jug, legs like tomwalkers and shadow like a pine. He stopped the other side of the drowsing gray mule, waist high to the animal and gazing over, gaping at the girls. He ducked down and stood again, and the girls began to laugh. Again he ducked down and when he showed at last he was at the back of the wagon with its little flattop house aboard.  A rolling store. Or was it? Maybe he had stolen children inside, breathing, waiting.
The girls laughed anyway, swinging their arms from linked hands.
He had their attention then and he proceeded to take a quarter from his pocket and make a fist and when he opened his fist the quarter was gone. Then he reached up and pulled it from behind his hairy left ear.
Tricks over, the man walked past them through the flock of yellow flowers, heading down-bank toward the creek. He stopped, looking back. "Y'all right pretty for two little nigger gals," he said. 
They giggled. At least they'd fooled him.
"Hey," he said, "y'all wouldn't mind showing me where that spring's at, now would you?"
"I'll show you," Merry Gay said, following him loping down-bank against the slope, Tommy close behind.
His long legs folded at the knees and his heels kicked high in black shoes. It was a steep drop through green ferns, reeds and willow scrub to the narrow black slow-moving stream. Cool wet green smells heightened by contrast with the hot dusty road. Dark stobs had taken on the color of the water and reddening tupelo berries floated like fishing corks.
The girls watched as the man knelt on one knee and positioned the mouth of his jug over the clear pulsing spring, laying the round dirt-colored crock on its side in the routed sandy stream coursing through the brush to the glaring opaque water of the creek below. 
"We fooled him," Tommy whispered to Merry Gay.
Filling his jug, the man glanced back at them. 
"Let's go bathe." Merry Gay led off downstream along the bank.
They'd been loosely cautioned not to talk to strangers, but it was sometimes hard to determine adult stranger from mere adult, who they were supposed to be polite to. What was it Bernice always said? "Most men I wouldn't trust with my chickens." Something like that. But any man this ugly would have to be safe. He probably had a good heart. They'd learned in Sunday school that God looks at the inside not the outside.
Playing in the waist-deep pool of black water, wearing only their sack drawers, some thirty minutes later, laughing, having scrubbed their bodies and heads with sand, they had forgotten the rolling store man until they looked up at the bank and saw him squatting there, gumming a twig, watching them. He was holding a different jug, another earthen crock, taking delicate swigs, like a man enjoying his whiskey of an evening. Yes, whiskey-any man drinking whiskey was a threat, according to Bernice.
Following a good scrubbing, Merry Gay's sooty hair had turned scare-booger beige and Tommy's skin was algae green, matching the rock walls on the far side of the stream that blocked them should they decide that they needed to escape the ugly stranger. But not yet. For then, it was only a sense that he might be up to something, though unfathomable, that could result in a fall, a rip in their tender skin, bleeding. 
"Why you reckon he's watching us like that?" Merry Gay said low to Tommy on her left. She sank down on her knees, lipping the cool tickling surface of the wobbling water. 
"I don't know," Tommy said, still standing but hugging her thin upper body. "I wanta go home. I want my mama."
"How do we get past him?"
Tommy's hair was still fat, even wet, glittery and bushed out bigger than her whole green doll-like body. She wouldn't let Bernice cut her hair because it might hurt and there was no way to comb it, short of yanking it out by the roots. So, it just grew there, bunching up into itself at her bony true shoulders, like a hair sack, or the living pelt of an animal that had been skinned.
They couldn't escape by way of the east bank because of the high rock walls. They could gator crawl-slow, slow, not to draw attention to themselves-down the creek and head up-bank on the east side, but that seemed scarier because they'd be further from the road they'd come in on. The man might follow and do to them whatever it was he had in mind, whatever it was that Bernice feared some man doing to her chickens.
Already he was standing, as if waiting to grab them when they came out of the water.
"Let's just stay underwater till we get downstream around that curve," Merry Gay whispered. "Maybe he's playing with us, and what if we hurt his feelings? He'll take it as a slight because he's ugly." She tasted the water, mineral, bitter, but soft, staring down at her reflected scare-booger beige hair. She didn't look all that good herself, she decided.
They gator crawled up to the curve, terrified to look around. When they dared, they saw the man following along the bank as if he was playing a game only he knew the rules to.
"He is following us," Tommy said, whimpering, water balancing on the level under her chin. "What does he want?"
Merry Gay didn't answer, she didn't know. Maybe it had something to do with things whispered behind women's hands. Once she'd overheard Bernice and a neighbor lady talking about a little colored girl being "messed up" by her own daddy. "Oughta cut out his balls." Balls were what hung between Merry Gay's brother's legs, like pickled eggs in the jug at the commissary, when they swam naked. 
They felt trapped in a cave or a well, closed in by rocky banks of green that shed a queer green light on the black mirror of water. The combined ringing of katydids and the cheeping of frogs skimmed across the surface, a sound so shrill and alive it made their ears pop, their bodies cringing in fear and waiting for the blue sky overhead to go dark and whatever would happen to swoop down on them with talons of mythic birds.
"Let's run," Merry Gay said out loud, jumping to her feet and running on downstream. Splashing water, kicking high, till she and Tommy reached a deep eddy and had to dog paddle. Sudden silence in the absence of their splashing and the unreal presence of the man on the shore as if he had been there all the time, on the shore, only a few yards away, waiting for them to come out.
Sure enough: "You girls bout as well come out. You're liable to drownd in that ole deep hole."
"What you want, mister?" Merry Gay yelled.
Tommy was crying low, breathing fast.
He laughed; then spoke in that up-and-down grating voice. "Y'all in the habit of fixing up like little niggers? Fooling people?"
Merry Gay and Tommy turned, paddling upstream till they reached about two feet of water streaming over white scalloped sand and rock. Tired, hungry, terrified of the man who had turned now too, greeting them with a pull on his jug not twenty feet from where they were standing. The sun had moved from the center of the creek to the treetops behind him.
"Y'all done sprouted you some titties, I see." The man cackled out, setting his jug on the bank, and proceeded to shuck his boots, coat and pants.
The girls took advantage of his lapsed attention and again began slogging upstream, holding hands. Through sparkles of splashed water they could see him moving with them, hobbling barefoot along the bank with long white hairy legs sticking from his white under-shorts, his chest concave, ribby sides. Almost back to the place where they were bathing when he had happened up, they stopped, breathing hard. There was no way out.
Tommy began to cry loud. Open, blatting. "Ma-ma, Ma-ma, Ma-ma."
Merry Gay told her to hush. Then shouted, "Our daddy's gonna be here any minute. He's Hoss Rose, from up at the commissary at Moniac Crossing."
"Moniac, huh?" The man stepped out into the water, tender-footed and concentrating on what lay beneath. A stob, a chunk of soaprock. 
The girls angled toward the bank at the point where they had come down from the road. Moving, gazing back; moving faster, gazing back; Tommy calling, "Ma-ma, Ma-ma," every breath. Little and wet and smudged with soot, a green girl. Splashed water seemed to hang on the air.  Suddenly the man began stepping high and quick, headed for them, cutting them off as they frantically tried to reach the bank and run toward home, only a half mile away that seemed like the end of the world.
Almost there, almost to dry land, Merry Gay looked back and saw him lift Tommy from the water, her wiggling and screaming, but caught. The water hanging on the air began to fall like rain. Merry Gay wheeled and rushed at him, beating his pale pimpled back with her fists and clawing hair and flesh with her nails. "Put her down."
Tommy slipped from his grasp into the water, sank and rose, strangling and screaming, and he grabbed Merry Gay's flailing hands, holding them tight at the wrists, pinching off the blood.
It was Tommy's turn to fight him. She latched onto his upper right arm with her teeth. He let go of Merry Gay and hooked Tommy by the hair, lifting her from the water like a scruffed cat. Both girls squalling, beating, he sank back into the water, sitting up to his chest as if it was over, the game was.
Again they ran for the shore, holding hands and galloping. He sprang after them and caught Tommy's ankle and they went down again. Beating, flirting water, strangling.
He let go of Tommy's ankle and caught both girls by the hair, at the crown, dragging them back toward the deeper water. Laughing, holding them out, a safe distance from kicking feet and grappling hands, like gator prey.
"Ma-ma, Ma-ma, Ma-ma!"
A loud explosion sounded, resounding in echo like a blast of dynamite, and tremors vibrated from the man's hands to the girls' heads and the jarred water. His face bloomed with blood red as roses. He let go and sank back again, this time, head and all, red foam rising with bubbles.
On the bank where the girls had come down, Bernice stood in her gray traveling suit and hat with a tall waving feather, rifle out before her.

It should have been over then but it wasn't.
At first, the town seemed only scared, scared for their children to leave the house. Then the teacher, one of the Elton twins (she was called The Other Twin because her sister, Miss Ellie, was simple-minded but sweet and The Other Twin was a witch) shifted the focus, pointing out that Merry Gay and Tommy should be separated. Merry Gay smearing herself with soot was a sure sign of budding psychological problems. Bernice was to blame. There were certain ingrained behaviors in Negroes; Negro girls knew too much, were hot-blooded, and would ruin a white child like a sandspur seed in a corn patch. Merry Gay's innocence was on the line. They weren't children anymore. Then the aggregate blame dwindled down to Tommy. She had seduced the man with the rolling store, endangering precious white innocent Merry Gay. 
A group of church ladies came to the Rose house to pray that Bernice would own up to her mistake in letting her own daughter be raised with a Negro child. What about the future? What about when Tommy got grown (thirteen was the agreed age) and began teaching Merry Gay all kinds of tricks. Merry Gay painting herself black was a bad sign; pray it wasn't too late. Wouldn't Negro boys come sniffing around and Merry Gay, having been raised not to respect the difference, fall prey to one of them?
Bernice threw the women out.
Man to man, the men talked to Hoss at the commissary. It was his duty to separate the girls, seeing as how Bernice didn't have the heart. One man said his wife had a pet milk cow and when the cow quit giving milk, the wife refused to let him sell her. He sold her anyway. For the wife's own good. Women were soft-hearted and weak and it was up to the men to protect them from their own God-given sweet nature. Bernice would get over it. Once Tommy was gone, Bernice would be fine. 
Hoss cussed the men out and ran them off.
The Other Twin expelled Tommy from the one room school, where she and Merry Gay went, for being a bad influence on her other students. Little girls weren't allowed to play with either Merry Gay or Tommy. Those same little girls were prompted to say that Tommy had indeed been "talking dirty."
Merry Gay wouldn't go to school without Tommy; Bernice could send her but she wouldn't stay. She would sneak back and she and Tommy would huddle in their bedroom or underneath the house, terrified of being separated. The rolling store man no longer seemed the villain or even a problem; they'd never doubted that they could lick him, together they could do anything. 
For the girls, there were boundaries then: Underneath the house on a floor of rat-smelling dust, with an overhang of cobwebbed, foot-adzed beams, they would listen to their mother's footsteps. They would listen for any word about what would become of them-jail, whippings, separation. Anytime now, Bernice might stop walking the floors, their ceiling, and decide to hand them over to the enemy. Hoss might give in; after all, the girls had been such trouble while Bernice was gone.
They didn't cry, were too scared to cry. Sometimes Bernice would mumble to herself. Other times she shouted at Hoss and he shouted back. Hoss was to blame for not watching the girls better; Bernice was to blame for being gone. Buck and Charlie's feet thundered through the house. The Other Twin was possibly the only person, except for Hoss, who Buck and Charlie feared. She'd long ago flogged one of their buddies with a buggy whip and left him for dead on the schoolyard. They had scooped him up and left that day, reading, writing and rithmatic be-damned. 
Tommy and Merry Gay listened for their own names-surely they were to blame. They never heard that though. They never heard even the rolling store man blamed.
Hoss and Bernice passed along comments from their neighbors, who said what to whom. What to do? Bernice said ignore it; Hoss said confront it. "Don't, the girls won't never be let alone to grow up normal."
Underneath the kitchen floor, there was solace in the aroma of a cake baking. There was solace in the fact that their mother and daddy were standing over them, watching over them.
Merry Gay, seated flat on the dirt, facing Tommy on her filthy knees, pulled from her skirt pocket a rolled book. "I brought you your reader." She passed it to Tommy.
"You'll get in trouble." Tommy took the book, smoothing it flat on her lap. Still, the pages rolled up. They were never allowed to take books home, and any student caught rolling a book would be switched on the back of the knees till they bled.
Tommy had whacked off her hair with Bernice's scissors and she looked shrunk, stubble sticking up all over her perfect head. She looked like a dust bunny beneath a bed. She handed the book back. "I don't want it."
Their spotted dog was baying at them, barking and pawing up dust along the northeast corner of the kitchen. But outside, it was raining, slow rain dousing the fire in the Okefenokee and soaking the frost-burned weeds and parched dirt of the yard. A tinny patter and smack all around. 
"You're the best reader. The Other Twin always says so." Merry Gay tried to give the book back to Tommy, to make her take it.
"You sing the best," Tommy said.
"I just sing."
"I just read." Tommy took the book from Merry Gay and tossed it at the furiously baying dog. He backed up; then lunged again, barking.
Tommy said, "I'm not going back to school."
"Me neither."
Tommy's inky eyes blared. "What did I do?"
"I don't know." True, Merry Gay did not know, but she knew what was being said. "They don't know either."
"I'm a nigger-that's it."
"What the hell is that?" Hoss asked Bernice in the kitchen above the girls.
"Hair. Tommy's hair. She cut it."
"Why?"
"I guess she figured wouldn't hurt no more than what she's been through already. Or maybe she was just punishing herself."
Hoss didn't answer.
Merry Gay, under the house, said, "Daddy'll fix it. Him and Mama."
The dog was getting closer, seemed likewise to have turned against them, after them feeding and playing with him all that time.
"That dog's driving me crazy," Hoss said to Bernice. "What ails him?"
"He's barking at the girls under the house."
"Merry Gay! Tommy! Get out from under there." Hoss stamped his boots; the girls' ceiling rocked.
"What happened?" Tommy asked. "I mean at the creek that day."
"Like Mama said, not anything we did."
"Then why??"
"I don't know."
"Where will I go?"
Merry Gay had heard that Tommy might have to go live across the tracks. "I'll go with you. I'm the one to blame for daring you to let's be little colored girls and fool daddy."
"He's not my daddy. Mama's not my mama now."
Merry Gay said, "Buck and Charlie put a dead cat in the teacher's well to get back at her for what she did." 

A town meeting was called at the Masonic Lodge, upstairs over the commissary. No Negroes, no children, only white adults who would determine the best action in light of Bernice and Hoss's refusal to separate the girls. Their preacher was there; their teacher was there; the whole town including Hoss and Bernice, who were a bit late, a lot hot. Meeting already in progress, they sat on the last row. Heads turned to acknowledge their presence in the open room warmed by a black iron heater blazing to the left of the podium up front, lanterns hanging from the solid raw walls producing a shadowy orange cast on the faces of their neighbors-strangers. It was cold out, the first really cold night, and they were all bundled up in heavy coats reeking of mothballs that the burning oak in the stove couldn't thwart. 
In deference to Hoss and Bernice, or maybe out of fear, the man at the podium, a long-tall farmer with a planed face, toned down what he was saying, but stuck to his speech on the dangers of mixing whites and Negroes, the cursed offspring of Ham. "Anybody ever seen blackbirds and bluebirds mix?"
"No," they all said.
The Other Twin, tall and thin and tight-lipped with a hard knot of faded red hair on her nape, turned to eye Bernice and Hoss. Her twin, Miss Ellie, beside her was smiling obliviously. Funny how a smile can soften a face; how that smile redeemed her from permanent twin-ness.
"What we got here's a clear-cut case of bad judgment," the farmer said, shouldering his heavy green coat over clean bib overalls. "We let it go, and who's to say a few years from now we won't be mixing all up and down the tracks. Before long won't be no white race, we don't put a stop to it now."
Mumbles of agreement sounded throughout the room. Eyes cutting back at Hoss and Bernice with their arms crossed, seemingly willing to be hauled to the cross.
Then Hoss stood, shedding his coat to the bench behind, scratching his right forearm under the rolled sleeves of his brown-plaid flannel shirt as he stepped over Bernice's crossed feet and out to the aisle between the pine church pews, headed for the front. Under his right arm he was carrying a small white stuffed pillowcase with pink embroidered flowers, top folded down; hearing side to side his neighbors whispering behind their hands. Hateful remarks toned down as before out of respect and maybe fear of the great man.
The farmer sat down on the front pew, waiting, gritting his toothless gums. Let it be known he was a fair man, agreeable to hearing both sides. Not that it would change his thinking.
Hoss was still scratching his arm when he stepped behind the podium and placed one heavy work boot on the base, and the pillowcase of-what?-on top of the slanted rectangular top and leaned into the podium with his arms resting on the bag like a cushion. "Ole canker sores," he said, scratching, "Got blistered riding the woods yesterday." He stood tall, packing his shirt into his dark twill pants, belt warped from supporting his hard flat belly.
Everybody stared at the pillowcase, as if expecting some sleight of hand from Hoss. 
"Okay," he said, blue eyes bright in his bright face. "You got a point here, maybe. I been thinking, me and Bernice, and maybe we all oughta try cleaning up our houses."
 Suddenly he wheeled like a heated preacher, speaking louder."YOU!" He pointed at The Other Twin on the second row, who looked stricken by his abrupt tone-change and by being singled out. Her right hand flew up to her heart. 
"It's a known fact, Miss Faye, that your own daddy, The Colonel, had slaves and was known for taking his business to the quarters." 
Toning down again, he addressed the crowd then. "Know what I mean? Beg your pardon, ladies, but ain't no nicer way to put it." 
Miss Ellie nodded as if he'd just paid Daddy a fine compliment. 
People throughout the hall began hanging their heads, mumbling. This was not how it was supposed to go. Besides, Hoss seemed to be hinting at the long-ago scandal in the Elton house in which a hideously deformed boy baby all at once appeared as if dropped by the stork on a household of virgins. Apparently, Miss Ellie had gotten with child while being courted by an out-of-town lawyer. Nobody knew for sure, but shortly before the baby got dropped, both twins had gone on an extended overseas trip after the old colonel had broken up the affair between his simple-minded daughter and the lawyer. 
Then?well, the rest was history and best laid to rest. Poor Miss Ellie, so sweet, so fun, was doomed to unwed motherhood and the object of the town's salty sympathy. Not to mention being cast as a living example of what might happen to other loose young women if they didn't behave.
Hoss was practically shouting now, scanning with those cold Irish eyes for his next victim. "YOU!" He pointed straight-armed at a scrawny sun-tanned man scooting down, second row on the right. "You look like a man's got some colored in you, how come you don't burn up out in the corn field walking behind that mule." 
The man rose to his feet as if to dispute Hoss but sat again as Hoss moved on side to side with his long finger pointing at this, that and the other one. Stopping on a woman on his right who was tall but stoop-shouldered and for the record had up till then held her peace. Hoss lowered his voice, almost apologetic. Almost. "Miss Becky, rumor is your own grandmother had things to do with one of the colored field hands back before your mammy was born." 
Moving quickly now that deadly finger, before Miss Becky could protest, though she was sobbing low, scandalized, Hoss stopped dead center between the eyes of a stalwart but short man with white hair, who held Hoss's deadly stare. "Mr. Miller, ain't it a fact you was wet-nursed by a colored mammy after your mama died?"
Mr. Miller nodded, even smiled. "Wouldn't be here now if it wadn't for her."
Not a sound in the room but the ticking of the heater whose fire was dying down.  Cold now seeping in through the walls that had absorbed the heat of so many righteous battles they should have been riddled as by woodpeckers seeking burrowing beetles. 
"Me," Hoss continued, "I'm Irish. Proud of it. But wadn't too many years ago, my people was looked down on like dogs. Men in my family didn't own no slaves-too poor, for one thing. But they wadn't a bit above taking some poor lil ole colored gal when they got a little drink in them."
A few men laughed, nudging each other.
"So," Hoss said, leaning into the podium again, eyeing them side to side, "let's all sort out our younguns, get their blood lines straight and get em settled in on the west side of the tracks. Don't matter where you love em or not. Get over it! This is business. We can come out lily-white on the east side of the tracks; and while we're at it, let's don't let no more coloreds work our gum-woods for us. We'll do it ourselves. We'll buckle down and a few generations from now our blood'll be so white won't even run red when we cut ourselves."
"You just fooling with us now," one of the men shouted.
"Yeah," somebody else said.
The farmer on the first row yelled out, "I got Indian in me, same as your wife. Ain't nothing wrong in that. Ain't like having nigger blood."
Hoss slammed a fist on the podium. "It's worse out west."
The crowd roared their disagreement. Then leveled out to a thin hum as if waiting for what Hoss would say next. 
"What's in the sack?" a woman asked.
Hoss held up the stuffed pillowcase. "Oh this," he said, opening it and peeping inside first, then reaching in as he spoke. "This here's just a little something I thought y'all might get a kick out of." 
Slowly he pulled out a clumped nest of black hair and held it up for them to see. "Tommy's hair," he said. "Wouldn't let Bernice cut it cause it might hurt. Then the other day, she took Bernice's scissors and cut it off herself." He looked at the hair which looked knitted into one piece. "Bernice says she figures Tommy come to the notion that after all's been done to her, wouldn't hurt her no worse to cut off her hair."
 He placed the hair tenderly back into the pillowcase, leaving his hand inside. "I don't know about y'all," he said, staring out at the crowd, "but a lil ole youngun doing that kinda breaks my heart."
Again he pulled out his hand, bringing with it a tangle of straight blond hair like corn silks. "Merry Gay's hair," he said. "She cut it off because her sister cut hers off, same way they did when they took a notion to paint theirselves with chim'bly smut, playing with me. That's what younguns do. But most of the time ain't a bunch of grownups making a big fuss over it. Still, and all, me and Bernice want to do the right thing." 
He dumped the pillowcase sack of hair to the top of the podium, parting it carefully so that the blond lay on one side and the black hair on the other. "So, y'all sort out your younguns, who's colored and who's white, and we'll do the same." 
 Here, Hoss stood back and began scratching his arm again, as if he was done talking. "You, preacher," he said finally, nodding to a feeble bald man seated next to the teacher, comforting her. "You bring us a written guarantee that everybody's took an accounting of their younguns' blood-black, red or white-and put them where they belong. Then me and Bernice will let Tommy go."

***

Angels turned devils at ten, in the town's eye, Merry Gay wouldn't go to school and Tommy couldn't go to school. Or maybe she could go, but Bernice and Hoss had gradually over the winter lost the drive, desire, or ambition to test the town. Actually they had dropped out of society all together, dazed as bears wandering from their caves before the spring thaw. People under the spell of conjoined dreams. Of course, nobody had brought forth proof of pure bloodlines, and neither had they sorted their children by color as Hoss had demanded. He hadn't expected that. He was just mad.
Then even that passed.
He began opening up the commissary when he felt like it. Bernice whipped up britches for the girls-strange two-panel garments with drooped crotches of flannel outing ripped from gathered skirts. Even a pair for herself of yellowed black-and-white hound's-tooth wool that looked brown, which she had cut down from a pair of Hoss's old suit pants worn before his lost respect. With her stick legs and thick body, nobody could dispute that she was the ugliest woman ever at Moniac Crossing. And the meanest (she beat up the hussy with an ugly stick, a thorny stout switch growing in the nearby woods). And the laziest (fall leaves on her porches and hall would stay till the March winds blew them away). And the sorriest mother (she didn't cook and she let her girls loaf and eat candy any old time).
It was as if these were the real Roses and those before had been only propped up people about to fall. It was as if they were?well, white trash. A hardened leather work boot, minus half a sole and tongue, lay moldering at the front gate, kicked about now and then but never picked up.
Tommy and Merry Gay would eat candy on the commissary steps in the frosty morning sunshine while the other children at the Crossing passed on their way to school. Schoolgirls in black stockings, jealous and showing it by sticking out their tongues. But mostly Tommy and Mary Gay loafed. Not really into mischief-it takes energy to get into mischief. They didn't go to church. Tommy's wooly gapped hair grew out to a bush. Nobody in the household could resist touching it, as if traumatized by its brittle bulk, or like a charm that might change their hard-luck lives. Both girls looked like boys, or like hobos freighted in on the trains blowing through like the winter wind. Tommy's eyes looked bruised underneath from lack of sleep. She claimed the devil was chunking dirt rocks at her all night. At first Bernice would sweep the crumbling clods of hard dirt from beneath Tommy's bed into the fireplace, but she soon quit and let it pile up.
Buck and Charlie, in their twenties then, had to buckle down and take charge for the first time in their lives. They had to keep up their daddy's turpentine business during the day, come home whipped and disgraced by being reduced to children, or women, whose usual job it was to tote in firewood. 
The girls were in britches, loafing on a cold March Monday morning while the wind caught its breath. The lane before the tracks was wind-swept, the sun at eight o'clock so bright it blinded them as it welled up behind the bare branches of shed water oaks and pecan trees. From the third row, east side of the frost-twinkling tracks, Tommy and Merry Gay now at the commissary could hear the stilted laughter of the school children, could hear The Other Twin shaking her black iron hand bell for end of recess. Same bell she used for fire drills and knocking heads, leaving little ears ringing while a lump formed on their skulls like the ringing trying to break out. 
Really the girls had been feeling a bit sated on sugar and hooky before they heard that bell.
The commissary was closed; Hoss was sleeping late again. The other men had gone to the woods or to the hooper shed, next door to the schoolhouse. Heart of the community during winter, when the stubborn pine gum refused to run, the open shingle shed was gathering place for most of the workers. There, they cleaned their tools and mended the gum barrels, rapping out a musical beat with their hammers as they nailed loose metal hoops to the staves; then slathered to the gaps between staves, a fat-smelling glue rendered from cowhides and other waste saved from beef slaughtering. All done to the timed rhythm of the head man, the hooper at his wheel, whose job it was to man the wheel, turning, shaping and shimming new barrels from hoops and staves. A merry bright heat radiated from a constant fire with the sounds of rapping, talking and laughing. Nobody could resist stopping by to watch, at least once a day. At the shed, Merry Gay and Tommy whiled away countless hours of their belittled lives. 
Out in the woods yesterday they had wandered up on a runty Negro boy with a shovel standing atop a tar hill, five feet high at its topmost. He was wearing handed-down britches and oversized unlaced boots. The wind was whipping at his pants legs and flattening the seeps of smoke rising from the dirt that covered the smoldering layered wood below. Every so often he would pack the dirt with the shovel over the pips of rising smoke to keep the pine tar in the process of being rendered from erupting into flames. Then he stood again on lookout from his tar hill. 
At the foot of the hill, offside from the wind, a big yellow dog lay sprawled on his belly in the warm dirt.  
The girls had been warned never to climb up a tar hill, because it might cave in and they would be burned alive. Now it didn't matter. The big yellow dog came alive with barking as Tommy and Merry Gay scampered up the hot mound, for no good reason but for the risk and to get warm.
Holding the shovel handle like a gun, the boy called out in a whiney monotonous voice, "Y'all go on now, heah? Go on, fore the bossman take a stick to me." He sounded like a tired old man.
 They ignored him, standing next to him and gazing out at the green but wintering pinewoods, warm on the cooking dirt but for the north wind pushing at their backs. Shovel now on the ground where Merry Gay had slung it at the dog, growling and shining his teeth and digging out the dirt along the south end of the hill, trying to climb up after them. 
"Ain't no girls 'lowed up heah," the boy said. "Sides, Mammy say y'all ain't no normal whites and I ain't belong to be sociating with you- all. Now git!" He stomped one oversized unlaced boot and smoke seeped from the dirt like his shoe sole was on fire. 
"Now look what y'all gone and did," he said, skiing down the slope of the mound to retrieve his shovel. "This my hill; ain't you-alls'."
Still quarreling, while the dog barked and snarled and dug, he began packing the dirt along the south end of the hill, smoke furling around his wiry bent body and the dog.
By the time he got back to the hilltop, the girls were gone. Hidden in the woods, they watched him packing dirt over the ruptures of smoke with his bare hands. Then he stood again, on lookout, serving at least some lowly purpose on earth. 
From the commissary steps, where the girls now sat, they could hear the commotion at the hooper shed. Only the women were about, sweeping porches, hanging out wash, drawing water from the steaming wells. Smells of breakfast bacon clung on the still, cold air. Slow smoke rose and spread from the chimneys of the stained shingle roofs across the tracks. One chimney at the far south end of the lane was puffing smoke higher, blacker, growing from a plume to a settling heap. Waiting for Hoss to open up for their candy breakfast, the girls watched the smoke begin to pour around the stately white house with brick columns gracing the wide front porch.
"Looks like Miss Ellie's put too much lightered in the stove again," Merry Gay said.
"She's gonna set that poor ole boy of hers on fire one of these days." Tommy, tiny in big tartan plaid britches, leaped up and ran down the doorsteps. "Let's go."
Usually, on the sly, they called Miss Ellie's boy "Booger Boy"-all the town's children did-because of his elephantine features and zombie-like motions, but by all accounts he should have been a man. Only on odd occasions when Miss Ellie led him out on the porch while her sister was away teaching school did anybody see him. Till The Other Twin got wind of it, Miss Ellie used to walk Booger Boy along the lane some mornings. Him clumsy and huge in carefully-tied brogans, dogs surrounding, baying as they would a great bear lumbered up out of the woods. And Miss Ellie, head nigh halfway up to his wall-chest, holding his hand in one of hers, reaching up, while the other hand swung a stick at the spooked dogs holding their ground. 
Booger Boy was just there, inside the roomy fine house, known about but unknown, and on dark nights the children of the town would dare each other to walk up on the porch and peep through the window.  They said Booger Boy wandered the Crossing on moonlit nights. They said he slogged house to house on those slow size-twelve feet looking for children to snort up through his trunk.
Merry Gay took off running with Tommy across the commissary yard, hopping the icy railroad tracks. They could still hear their used-to-be classmates on the playground and The Other Twin shouting for them to line up after figuring they were having too much fun. When the girls reached the end of the lane, they could see the smoke billowing from the off-set white kitchen connected to the house by a dogtrot with a water-shelf and a black bell hung from a tall wood pole. Evergreen shrubs hugging the body of the house had smudged the white walls, as if Booger Boy had been scribbling with green ink.  
Almost to the kitchen, they could hear Miss Ellie's small side-button shoes stomping on the floor and her laughing between coughs and saying, "My mercy! My mercy!" Squirrels barked and scamped up the smoky pecan trees outside the open door, smoke bunching and creating a screen that turned the yellow sun to rose.
Through the kitchen door they could see Miss Ellie waltzing from the doorway to the pulsing red glow of the west wall, fanning smoke with her white apron, her dull red hair freed from its bun and flying like a kite tail. One minute she was there and the next she was gone into the smoke, bogged in smoke. "Mercy me! Mercy me!" Coughing, gagging, and laughing.
"Miss Ellie?" Merry Gay shouted from the bottom doorstep, covering her mouth and nose with her hands.
Tommy, beside her, was gagging, ducking, her eyes blinking back the bitter smoke.
Miss Ellie was still fanning her apron, lost in the roar of the fire that seemed to be burning beyond the west wall but showing only in seething red peeps. Now and then gusts of smoke poured from the open door and Miss Ellie was lost again. Only her white apron fanning smoke, she was no longer laughing, only gagging and coughing.
"I'm gonna go ring the bell for help," Merry Gay shouted, hopping from the doorstep beside Tommy and racing toward the breezeway. Like Miss Ellie vanished in smoke. 
When Miss Ellie showed again, a specter in a white apron, she was nearer the doorway and Tommy ran up the remaining doorsteps and grabbed her by her skirt-tail. Miss Ellie was bent over; when Tommy touched her she stood straight and yanked away and began fanning at the smoke again. Struggling to breathe, Tommy ran to the doorway and ducked out and filled her lungs with fresh air, then headed back into the rosy-lit smoke and heat of the kitchen, calling, "Miss Ellie, get out. Get out!"
"Honey, help me," she shouted. "Help me put out this fire."
The bell in the breezeway between the house and the kitchen clanged, a shrill punctuation in the bass roaring of the fire.  
"Miss Ellie," Tommy screamed through the smothering smoke. "Come on."
Miss Ellie turned her back on her, fanning at the rosy black flames breaking through the west wall and the thick slumped figure of the ageless man beating at the flames with his huge stiff hands and bellowing like a gator. Tommy caught Miss Ellie around the waist from behind, dragging, inching her toward the doorway. Every step, Miss Ellie was stamping on Tommy's toes, coughing, shouting, reaching toward the fire and Booger Boy only feet away from the burning wall.
"Merry Gay! Merry Gay! Hurry. Booger Boy's in here," Tommy shouted from the doorway.
Then she stepped back, tumbling from the top doorstep, with Miss Ellie landing on top of her-hard bones and heavier than she looked, but soft as dough. 
Merry Gay shot past them as Tommy rolled free and jumped to her feet, heading up the steps and into the fire-streaked smoke and meeting Booger Boy, grunting, coughing, feeling ahead like a blind man, with Merry Gay shoving him from behind toward the doorway. Walking backwards, Tommy grabbed both of his hands and guided him through the smoke toward the sunlight raying around her.
Suddenly the men from the hooper shed came hopping over the picket fence and two of them helped lead Booger Boy down the doorsteps, while the others began drawing water from the brick well by the pecan tree and passing it up into the burning kitchen. All were shouting, rushing and roaring as if born of the fire. 
Coughing, half-blind, Merry Gay and Tommy on the ground next to the doorsteps began dragging Miss Ellie by the arms toward the pecan tree and the stunned overgrown boy sitting on the dirt with his legs stretched out before him.
He was wearing a soot-smudged and scorched white nightshirt with some soft-looking, loose-fitting gray pants, which made him look neither male nor female. His wispy brown hair, growing in one clump from his crown, was singed and he gave off a bitter scorch smell.
 Miss Ellie jerked away from the girls and crawled on her knees to where the boy sat sprawled and dazed, only grunting, but low as if ashamed. His tremendous head was bowed, moss-gray eyes peering down at the leathery smoked hide of his hands spread on his legs. His crinkled face was the same shade, lusterless and absurd to the point of comical with its centerpiece elephant's snout and wide-spaced bumps of eyes. Cute the way an animal is cute.
Miss Ellie lifted one stuffed dirty hand and kissed it, crying, then bowed over him pressed one cheek to the up-held hand. The boy's eyes raised slowly as if he were shy, as if his lash-less lids were smithed of iron, and his eyes smiled in place of those thin rind lips hidden by his snout. He rolled his eyes toward the back gate and then the front and the hoards of people gathering in the yard, slow-blinking in the sunlight as a gopher peeking out from its hole.
Merry Gay, standing next to Tommy, whispered, "He's not so scary-looking to me."
"Not out in the open, he's not." Tommy's wiry hair looked melted.
The school children and The Other Twin, holding high her black iron hand bell, began filing through the back gate. 
"Sister, Sister!" Miss Ellie bawled, still on her knees and holding the boy's hand.  "Forgive me, forgive me. I almost burnt up your boy." She turned her head to look at Merry Gay and Tommy, then smiled. "Wadn't for these angels here we'd both be burned alive." 


CHAPTER 4

Yellow lights the dimness of about a single candlepower each had started to burn along the facing concave walls of the coach while Mary Gay's eyes had been shut. A muzzy light in which to rest after so many hours of brilliant sun and motion. In less than an hour, Merry Gay figured, they would be home and she looked forward to her own bed, to the lapse in the tilt and sway ingrained in her body and mind. The racket of the train had become static in her ears, and the closed stale car smell no longer seemed alien or even offensive. Like everything else from the long day, the train ride was shaping into Past, evolving as neither pleasant nor unpleasant memory, simply something done. She would sleep for the rest of ride, but first she had to go to the lavatory. She had to check on Buck and Charlie to be sure they were behaving.
Priss appeared asleep already, her head turned toward the aisle, pale hair covering the right side of her face. Merry Gay stood, sidling past her knees without touching. 
She looked up, sat up. "Where you going?"
"Where are you going?
Priss laughed at being corrected. She used to correct Merry Gay. Priss had a private tutor and Merry Gay had gone to the one-room schoolhouse in Moniac before the grades had phased out in her ninth year in school. She read and she knew how to speak, but her family's bad influence was greater: What they knew, they knew. And what they didn't know, they didn't need to know. Can you count your money, son? Can you sign your name, my daughter? If the answer was no, you weren't grown yet; if the answer was yes, you were ready to hit the woods and help eke out a living for your family.
Holding to the seat backs, stepping side to side along the aisle, she started for the closed door of the exit. She was out on the sliding grid beneath which the coaches coupled before she realized that Priss was behind her.
The train couplings clanked and the wheels howled, absorbing all thought and sound, like the merging night silence of insects, frogs and trains at Moniac Crossing, till Merry Gay pushed through the heavy spring-loaded door to the smoking car. There was a shock of racket and odors, an eruption of frenzied shadows, mad shouting and bumping about, trapped heat and tobacco smoke, whiskey, musk, urine, vomit. The coach was lit by the same dull yellow lights as in the ladies' coach but looked greasy through the smoke haze.
 The girls were suddenly caught up in the midst of the ball players gathered at the door they'd just entered, which was now closing with an eerie, final delayed action. Only one of the boys seemed to notice them, a husky blond fellow with bad teeth who pretended to be thrown into Merry Gay. He thrust his pelvis into hers, then grappled at her large breasts with fingers like iron hooks. She shoved him aside and reached back for Priss's hand. She looked scared for the first time since Merry Gay had known her. Her red face blanched in streaks. 
Free from the tangle of the strange ball players, the girls had no choice but to keep inching toward the other end of the smoking car and the familiar men from Moniac Crossing, a jumble of Negroes and whites, of Shirley's loyal men and the protestors from the St. Augustine depot.  
Open space was reduced to the arched ceiling, churning smoke and whiskey reek and the hellish flickering of the corrupted yellow light, deafening demon voices rising up as if seeking outlet. Halfway through the pitching, milling, shifting men, Merry Gay realized that at least two of them were fighting again. She saw Buck shove Wendell Shirley and another man sail astride Buck's back. No coat now, no vest, and Wendell's white shirttail was loose, the front unbuttoned, or ripped open maybe. Hemmed in good, the girls began fighting their way through the smothering heat and bodies, the rot stench and racket, toward the door that Merry Gay, in the lead, could only catch glimpses of, but never took her eyes off of-air, silence, freedom from the choking smoke and bumping bodies. Sulfurous light and darting shadows on the backs of men and peeps of wall and the ceiling above. Looked like bats darting at sundown. Sundown light, but the windows looked painted black.
All that was familiar to Merry Gay, in the press of men's flesh, was the grip of Priss's small soft hand. Merry Gay was large enough and strong enough to fend off the men's bodies, but they would crush and trample Priss if she didn't get through. Merry Gay shouted, pushing at them with her free hand, her throat on fire with screaming, her eardrums cringing to bursting point from all the noise. She looked around for Wendell, screaming his name, and saw Charlie ramming one of Shirley's men against the west wall, and Mike Ruis kneeling as if praying, maybe coddling a broken fist.
Like a family member lost his mind, Nate Shirley with his pistol out before him nudged right past the girls, looking over their heads and shouting, without seeing them. When he moved his huge hot bulk, blocking the way, Merry Gay could see the door leading out again. A dark seamed rectangle with dim yellow lights each side. She shoved hard against the bodies of men, in the senseless throes of attack, men charmed by hate beyond reasoning, twitching Priss along and feeling her hand stretch from her arm like rubber.
Screaming was pointless, so Merry Gay quit. She no longer felt even angry with Buck or Charlie or any of them, because she was almost out, almost free, and because it was pointless. 
So close to the door, but not as close as Merry Gay had thought and they were blocked again. A burly Negro man shot out from the crowd on the left side of the car and slammed his whole body against the door. It opened slowly, languidly, letting in a draft of cool air, as another Negro man popped up behind the man at the door and yelled, "You run from that man?"
"Will!" Merry Gay said but couldn't hear her own voice.
Will didn't seem to have heard her either, but he saw her, as he turned, ducked and dodged a man coming at him, almost flying from the midst of the shadowy wrangling men on the left side of the car. One swift motion and Buck appeared slung like a monkey and stuck on the back of the Negro man standing at the lazily closing door. Both buckled to the floor and somebody else piled on and when he moved Buck was gone, Will was gone, and the Negro man who'd tried to run rolled to one side, his throat gushing blood, his head loose as a tooth on a shred of flesh. 
Priss was beside Merry Gay now, encircling her body with both arms and crying, screaming soundlessly. Merry Gay gagged, smelling the warm sharp mold of raw blood that had spurted onto her dress.
Popping sounds all around and she knew it was gunfire. The smell of burnt gunpowder was proof. That, and Buck trying to get the hell out of there, appearing before the door again, skidding to a halt in the puddle of blood, dark hollow eyes peering behind for whoever was after him. Wendell Shirley strode up, like on his way out to the dining car, and throttled Buck in the jaw and both fell away, dissolving into shadows and mincing boots. 
Merry Gay charged forward with all her might, all her grit and strength forged from her determination to make it to the door and the cool tart air and the riveting motion and sound of the train, a rhythm that would set things right. 
She and Priss had to step over the Negro man with his throat cut, who was blocking the door. She cried, feeling his fingers smush like peach drupe under her right shoe heel. Then they were outside with the dusk-not even sundown yet!-and it seemed so long since Merry Gay had seen the after-sun, or thought she had, striking the treetops.
The smoking car door eased shut on the hellish sounds and smells, but the girls could hear each other screaming and crying. Merry Gay still listened with an ear pressed to the door while hugging Priss close.
Merry Gay had only sampled this kind of violence before, among the men in the quarters fighting, or Buck and Charlie drunk and at each other. Next morning they'd be buddy-buddy. Only the women remembering the terrifying fight. She tried to hold onto that morning-after calm.
Suddenly Priss broke free from Merry Gay and bolted toward the door, but Merry Gay caught her around the waist from behind. She was stronger than she looked but Merry Gay, nerved up and doped on adrenaline, pulled her away, both tripping and sprawling against the door of the coach leading to the lavatory.
"Listen! Listen!" Merry Gay shouted into Priss's blood-speckled face. "Honey, we can't go back through there."
"I wanta go back," she bawled. "I want my mama."
One of the men inside the smoking car slammed his body into the door; it whooshed open-smoke, blood and vomit smells, shouting and gunshots.
Merry Gay and Priss cowered, covering their heads with their arms, waiting for the impact of many bodies. The door whooshed shut again, and they were alone, unharmed, momentarily paralyzed.
"Get up," Merry Gay shouted, scrambling to her feet, practically lifting the slender girl who felt limp, but thankfully had forgotten her mama or was willing to follow Merry Gay instead.
Merry Gay shoved open the door to the dining car and began walking Priss into the silent hall stained ruby-red from the wine carpet and velvet curtains, either backlit with sun or the lights of the dining car. She opened the ladies' lavatory door easy, stepped inside with Priss and closed the door, latching it. Hugging each other they listened to the coach door whoosh open and shut.
