Heavy boots pounding but padded by the carpet, somebody was running along the hall, circling back before the lavatory door; then passing through the coach door again.
How long the girls stood there in the stench of old perfume and toilet chemicals they didn't know, they couldn't tell, but the tiny rectangular window over the miniature porcelain bowl showed smoky sun and streaming treetops.
"Priss, I have to go check on Buck and Charlie and them. I have to."
"No, don't leave me. If you leave me I'll die." She hugged Merry Gay tighter.
"No you won't die, Priss. Men do this sometimes and it doesn't amount to a hill of beans. Remember how they were fighting at the depot back there?"
Priss nodded, then let go, and they stood looking at the blood on the skirt of Merry Gay's dress. "Then why don't we just stay here till help comes?" 
A good point, but Merry Gay had never been able to hide her eyes. She had to look out a window during a thunderstorm. "I have to go. Now you latch the door behind me and don't you dare unlatch it till I come back for you. Understand?"
"Yes." Priss was biting her nails, spitting them out to the stained and matted wine rug. Her hands were shaking. Freckles of blood on her face, a brave smile.
Merry Gay unlatched the lavatory door and stepped out, staring up and down the empty corridor. The partition of wine curtains trembled as if somebody from the dining car was about to part them but turned out it was only the motion of the train. "Latch the door," she whispered to Priss.
"Be careful, Merry Gay."
"I will." Merry Gay stood listening to the metal latch slide into place. Then with her back pressed to the vibrating wall, she inched forward, eyes on the door leading out to fresh air and the next door, a removed point from which to listen to the goings on in the smoking car.
Just as she passed through the door of the dining car, Mike Ruis burst out the door of the smoking car and leaped over the railing on the west side of the train, compact body cannon-balling down a ditch of close dry grass and weeds, Buck right behind and then Charlie in his thick glasses misted with blood. Expecting more men behind them, Merry Gay fixed her eyes on the roaring crowd and festering yellow light of the smoking car, then the slowly closing door. The boys had looked straight at her, hadn't seen her. She dashed across the sliding quavering grate over the car couplings and holding to the railing each side of the steps she leaned out, expecting to see Buck and Charlie rolling down the ditch like Mike Ruis, but saw only pines streaming past with streaks of the low crimson sun like the woods were on fire.
Again the door of the smoking car opened, leaking curses and shouts, and Merry Gay rushed from the railing to a black closet-like canister, to the left of the door, peeping around to see Nate Shirley holding out what looked like a shotgun, crossing to the dining coach door, kicking it, then shoving it open with one hand and bawling as he passed through, "You son of a bitches, I know you're in here."  Only seconds later, it sounded like one of the flimsy lavatory doors opened and slammed shut, then loud gun fire, then low gunfire as the dining car door closed, muffling it.
Back to the wall, Merry Gay sidestepped out to the west railing again, wind whipping at her face and hair, smoke and cinders chuffing and streaming low from the engine up ahead. Above on the low roof extension was the decal warning her to watch her head. Leaning farther out this time, she scanned the fast moving dirt and trees, looking for Buck and Charlie but saw instead an old man with a donkey watching the train cars pass.
The door of the dining car signaled it was opening with the whooshing sound and she hurried back to her hiding place behind the canister. Cold now, freezing, with the heavy clackety-clack alive in her ears, she watched Nate Shirley emerge. Wild-eyed, face afire, he stepped out to the connector between cars, shouting, "I got em, I got those son of a bitches." The smoking car door opened. "Locked up in the ladies' lavatory, bunch of pussies. Shot em dead through the door."
Before the smoking- car door could close on the racket and stench, Merry Gay was passing through the door of the dining car, running, screaming, stopping at the door of the ladies' lavatory. The door was sprayed with jagged round holes about the size of a pea.
"Priss! Priss! Open the door. Priss! Open the door. Say something, Priss." She pressed her right cheek and ear to the door, looking down and listening with strained concentration; then sank to the wine carpet in the brighter wine blood she could see spreading from beneath the door. Same color as the carpet, so that you could almost believe it was water seeping. Almost.
"No! No, no. Oh, God no!" She knelt there crying, on her hands and knees, feeling the warm blood like silk. Like Priss's hair. Like Priss's hand stretched from her arm like rubber. Priss who had stood up on the yacht and boldly corrected the strange man who had made a joke of Merry Gay's name when he introduced her, "Her name is Merry Gay Rose."
The train began slowing, brakes squalling, rocking and halting. No bell and no whistle.
Again she ran out to the connector, past her hiding place, and leaned over the railing. About a quarter-mile up the tracks, she could see the white two-story commissary and depot at Moniac Crossing blending into the early twilight produced by the background thicket of vine-bound black-gums, bays, oaks and brush. Then she saw Charlie crawling out from underneath one of the cars up the line, rising to his feet without looking behind or breaking motion, and running-that clumsy shambling he did in a hurry or caught out in the rain-running out toward the west woods, where Buck was chasing the sun finally going down.
From her hiding place, Merry Gay observed the WATCH YOUR HEAD on her left and the Negroes running past, scattering out into the woods where Buck and Charlie had gone. She observed the WATCH YOUR HEAD on her right and the women and children from the ladies' coach being ushered along the east side of the train toward Moniac Crossing by the tall gaunt-faced ticket-taker, who had that morning given directions to the lavatory to her and Priss.  Though it was dusk and the women seemed to fuse into one another, Merry Gay could see their stunned faces. In spite of the talking and shouting in the smoking car and up and down the tracks, she could hear them crying.  She heard her name on her mother's lips, calling her, and Miss Moxie saying, "They said not to look. But I'm a woman and I was curious. Bodies were stacked like cordwood and blood was running out the door."
"Ladies, move along!" somebody behind them yelled. "Go to your houses and stay there."
The children were bawling. A woman called out, "Priss? Priss?"
"Go on now, we'll find her," said the man. It was Deputy Sheriff Threet, who everybody hated. Dingy eyewhites and short round body-double-tongued Threet.
"My husband and daughter," Priss's mother shouted. "Where are they?"
Merry Gay felt stuck to the wall, stuck in her corner. Numb. She couldn't go out yet, she couldn't tell about Priss.
WATCH YOUR HEAD.
There was a regular parade of train officials and men passing rapidly through the breezeway-like connector between the dining car and the smoking car but the women were gone. No shouting now. Lots of commotion in the smoking car, but no shouting now. One voice rose above it all-Nate Shirley's. He didn't know yet that he'd just killed his favorite grandchild. Merry Gay wanted to tell him. She felt at peace and redeemed knowing she would tell him, at some point. That morning-after calm settled over her; the women's voices, talking, crying, calling, were dwindling out.
A door slammed in one of the houses up the way and across the tracks. Somebody screamed. Gunfire. Then every dinner bell at Moniac Crossing commenced with cumulative iron donging, donging, a sound Merry Gay was accustomed to hearing for occasional alerts of bad weather, and house fires, and daily to signal dinnertime to the men in the woods. But rarely all at once. 
Merry Gay sank to the floor with her knees raised, placing her head on her crossed arms. The voices of the men in the smoking car rose and pitched; somebody was running along the track on her side of the train, not seeing her, going on, shoes brattling gravel and brattling out to nothing.
More shots.  She smelled woodsmoke. The bells were still being rung, but with fewer dongs now.
The smoking car was alive again with muddled voices, shouting.  Deputy Sheriff Threet was yelling somewhere along the tracks.
The steam engine chuffed, the train rocked a few feet forward and stopped again as if in warning. She had to get off. She ran to the train steps, squatted and jumped, tripping on the bloody hem of her skirt on the slope of gravel and weeds, running out toward the west woods where Buck, Charlie and the Negroes had gone. She could see the commissary up ahead again and people white and colored running across the tracks. A ring-straked goat trotted among them. No lamps lit in any of the houses facing the tracks, but a huge fire burned from what looked like the midsection of the third row eastside of the tracks. 
Merry Gay slunk low, waiting, watching. 
Within minutes, the train was pulling out, full steam ahead. Behind, Nate and Wendell Shirley and his men were gathered around a couple dozen bodies laid out on the other side of the track, some still, some moving. White shirts or bandages flickered. Maybe Priss's white dress.
Nate Shirley moved out of the crowd of men and stepped onto the tracks with the shotgun he'd used on the train, stock tucked under his left arm and finger on the trigger. His right arm hung loose and dangling by his side as if unhinged at the elbow. He aimed the barrel up, sighting the train between the rails of the track, using only that one good arm, and began firing at the red caboose tagging in front of the commissary now, halfway along the facing houses of Moniac Crossing now.
 "Scared son of a bitches!" He fired off another shot. The shots rang out with the fading rumble of the train, seeking cover of the woods from which it came and taking Priss with it, for all Merry Gay knew.
Crouching behind a stand of fan palmettos, she had to know. Before dark set in she had to know. Could she simply step out from hiding and walk down the tracks without Nate Shirley shooting her? Before, she had thought she was beyond his reach, but from what she could gather now, it seemed that there were two sides, two factions-the Shirleys and their men against the old families of Moniac, including the Negroes. One thing she knew, Buck and Charlie were on the bad side of Shirley and his men; she'd seen Buck and Wendell fighting in the smoking car. Why Buck had sailed onto that Negro man's back she didn't know. Had the fight broken out because of the drunken baseball player fondling her? No. But Nate Shirley had to have been looking for Buck, Charlie and Mike Ruis when he shot up the door of the lavatory and Priss in it. Which made Merry Gay the enemy not only by heritage of place but by blood.
The eastern sky was bloated with fast moving gray clouds and the wind was picking up, rattling the palmettos and leaves of the hardwoods, limber pines rubbing their upper trunks together and creaking like the timbers of a ship. Hot but damp wind rendered into sweat on Merry Gay's face. Definitely hurricane weather, and she thought it might put an end to the craziness, give the men a different crisis to put their energy to. Besides, the rain would douse the fires she could see rising two rows over behind her own house and higher than the pecan trees. Spume of sparks driving through the clouds.
An iron bell rang out, then another, as if calling, pleading, for each other. 
 Keeping low and back behind the trees and myrtle bushes, she crept south along the tracks. Already the turpentine hands had begun raking and weeding a three-foot clearance around the pines to keep the controlled burns they would set in fall from damaging them. Something orderly about the clean raked circles; something hopeful in the promise that what had been done before would be done again. Between two of the trees, stacks of new clay Herty cups to be hung during the winter promised that something never done before would indeed be done. From where she walked she could see scraps of shred raw cotton along the track and she could smell the heated soot and oil absorbed into the sharp gray rocks of the rail beds and the creosote of the crossties carried on the east wind. Likewise bringing the men's riled voices, but not clear enough for her to make out what they were saying.  She was almost directly across the tracks from them before she could see good the dead bodies laid out in a row, a dozen or more of mixed Negro and white, not covered yet, and buzzed by blowflies in the warm wind. Some of the injured sat around or wandered, fanning flies as if a sign of their end also, or else gazing at their wounds with intrigue. Others moaned and lay back, knees up and elbows out, blood wicking into indigo and hickory stripe clothes, Sunday shirts and trousers.  She couldn't recognize any of the dead or injured from where she was squatted behind a cluster of gall-berry bushes. But neither did she recognize a slender figure she could make into Priss in white. 
Her body not being there meant that Nate Shirley couldn't know that she was dead, that he had shot her. Merry Gay had to resist the urge to charge from the woods and across the tracks and shout it out that it was Priss in the lavatory-shouldn't be too hard to check the story out-and not Buck and Charlie he had shot. But not yet. 

***

The great Hoss Rose had died in August of 1903 as pain-free and simply as he had lived. In the three-year span of the new century, he had seen come to pass the inauguration of a new U.S. president, Mr. Theodore Roosevelt; he had seen come to pass the rumored invention of a humidity-control machine, already in use in a New York printing plant; he had heard of the miracles of traveling voices and music machines. But he had missed by only a matter of months the one invention that may have ignited his interest in the outside world: the first flight of a piloted flying machine, in Kitty Hawk, North Carolina. Though it flew only 120 feet, he would have figured its potential for pertaining to the backwoods of Moniac Crossing. He would have liked talking about the flying machine with his buddies there for his funeral. 
For days after his funeral, they lingered underfoot in Bernice's parlor and kitchen and yard. Merry Gay would be glad when they were gone but sorry too; her daddy would be gone for good then, his booming voice, his easy ways, his big-bear-shambling throughout the house.
She had lately figured he would die first, freeing Bernice, leaving her, making of her a widow before her time. At least she seemed that way, being healthy and bossy and of sound mind. Really, she became old the moment he died and made of her a widow, stripped of title, at the mercy of the church and prey for every drummer and man dropping by to court a cook. She had to keep moving, she had to keep smiling, and she had to keep order.
Left to lean on in case she should quit smiling, were Merry Gay and Priss and Tommy, newly married. The boys were at their mischief, drinking, excused and absolved of all wrongdoing because their own daddy had just died-you know how it goes. Bernice didn't expect anything from them and didn't get it, but in a crisis they were generally there, ready to whip up on whomever had caused it. Pure and simple as their daddy, but not half the man. Nobody knew what had gone wrong to make them into rascals; it was easy enough to blame on Nate Shirley. But they were precious rascals, roosters being roosters, as far as Bernice was concerned.
After Hoss's funeral, the boys, Buck and Charlie, started drinking, long abstinence of two days and they were at it again, them and Mike Ruis. Bernice had to shoo them out of the house, and when they came again the next morning, sobered up and saying their sorrys to Mama, Ruis's mess of brown hair was bald in patches like the back of a dog with mange and smelling worse: pure essence of body odor and twice-tasted whiskey. One of their gang had talked Ruis into letting him cut his hair with his pocketknife. Buck and Charlie brought him home to Mama for a decent trim--Mama could fix it. That's when she started hollering and waving her arms, sounded like she was yodeling; that's when she took a broom to them and ordered everybody else out of her kitchen, out of her house, out of her own hair.
After the last one had gone, and the house was quiet, Merry Gay, Tommy and Priss began making themselves scarce; they'd never seen Bernice go to pieces like that. She stayed in the kitchen. She was still smiling, ever-smiling, elbow-deep in ropy dishwater, washing the soiled plates, pots and pans and coffee cups stained by flapping lips, already figuring on how to make amends without sacrificing her pride by admitting she was human, feverish, worn out and grieving. 
Around four o'clock when the heat had reached its peak in the overturned parlor, where Tommy, Priss and Merry Gay were trying to set it right again, Priss waltzed out and into the kitchen. Bernice's back was trembling like palsy and she had sweated so that her blue-striped dress was glued to her body, her blond hair matted to her neck. 
Playing a risky game of slip up-and-grab-you-unaware, Priss locked her arms around Bernice's waist and pressed her cheek to her back. "I know you miss Papa Hoss twice as much as I do and I miss him bad." 
Bernice stopping washing dishes, got still, then slowly removed her right hand from the dishpan and covered the small boneless hands lapped on her waist. Then she cried. Open and long and not covering her face, Bernice cried. "If I had it to do over with I'd do it different."
Merry Gay and Tommy in the parlor heard her say that and wondered whether she meant her marriage, her outburst, her life. They set down the ticking-striped upholstered chair they were carrying to the corner where it and only it belonged and sat, one on each chair arm, sobbing low. Tobacco smoke still fanned in the sun through the windows and the dust specks of the mourners rode the heat. Bernice's crying was a new sound in the house, one rarely if ever heard, and they could picture her smiling which made her crying all the worse. 
"I hate Buck and Charlie," Tommy said low, "don't you?"
"I hate somebody but I don't know who."
"You girls in there," Bernice bellowed out in a strangled voice, "get back to work. What you think this is, some ho-tel?"
They stood, lifting the chair again, eyeing each other. Their mama had gone crazy!
"Listen," Bernice bellowed again, "my Granmama Iris nearlybout disowned everlast one of her younguns after Papa Pete died. She had to blame somebody, she had to hate somebody, get it?"
Priss piped up in a small teary voice. "You know what they said when poor old Mrs. Elton was put away?"
Nobody answered, so she did. "The twins like to clawed each other's eyes out over who was gone get their mama's pine buffet. And them living together in the same house!" 
That night when the house was clean and ordered and the lamps lit to illuminate their tear-stung faces, they were sitting around the kitchen table eating fried ham and grits with red-eye gravy and buttered biscuits. Clean smell of lye from the scrubbed-white pine floorboards. The kitchen was made hotter by the wood burning down to ashes in the black and white cook stove cattycorner to the one window over the wood counter and the dishpan. Behind the stove black iron skillets and pans hung on the wall like holes cut in the wood. Like most houses the kitchen was set off from the main body of the house by a small walkway, so that if the stove should catch fire the whole house wouldn't burn. Set off from the kitchen was a pantry with shelves of jarred pears, peaches, blackberries, tomatoes, okra, corn, squash, peas and beans, colors muted by the blue glass of Mason jars. If it grew from the dirt you could jar it, providing it wasn't poisonous, and really it hadn't been all that long since tomatoes were considered poisonous. 
 A butter-yellow cat flowed among their legs under the table, its scudding tail measuring the hems of their skirts, then lifting the corners of the blue and white checked oilcloth their forearms were stuck to. They were like bees listening for the humming of the queen. Nobody talked, only sniveling, shuddering. Now what?
Priss spoke first. "Mama Bee, I don't know if you know it but you're fresh out of cornmeal. Tomorrow we better go on over to the grist mill."
Everybody eyeballed Priss-couldn't she see that Bernice was over-worked as it was and on the brink of a nervous breakdown?
Then Merry Gay remembered. She imagined that sad-eyed Tommy across the table had forgotten what Bernice had said about the gristmill: "When I get caught up one of these days I'm gonna go out there to the gristmill just to listen to the mourning doves and rest." It had made no sense to them before and didn't now. Didn't have to.
It was after noon the next day when the three girls and Bernice got started up the white clay road leading out of Moniac, bound for the gristmill. But this time the trip was the object and not the shell-corn in the wood barrel on back of the wagon with Priss and Merry Gay. Bernice was driving the wagon and Tommy, being an adult by merit of having married, sat on the other side of the bench. No talking, listening to the wheels jolting over ruts washed by the last heavy summer rain; listening to the song of crickets in the dried cornfields and to the peepers announcing a vine-tented branch or bottom coming up. They could hear the whine of way-off sawmills growing shrill as the blades struck wood; they could hear locusts humming in the oaks touching branches over the clean sand road. But they didn't hear a single dove purling anywhere.
The blue sky was faded with autumn, bleached by the long summer sun. It generated cross-tones of lemon and peach light and a haze like peering through tulle. But the women's world under the pastel dome smelled of parched sagey weeds and corn dust, with maybe a hint of melon, split citron, and dwindling tree sap. A lone frazzled water turkey stood on a clay gall in the middle of a shrinking pond.
Merry Gay was worried about Bernice being "caught up" enough to go to the gristmill and listen to the mourning doves. Being caught up could go either way, she reasoned, like a farmer building his last barn and knowing it's his last barn, that he has finally caught up on his earthly work and can pass on to his heavenly reward. And too she was sad not only because her daddy had just died, but because her mama expected so little, required so little; and what Merry Gay herself wanted couldn't even be bought, and worse, she didn't even know what it was she did want. She didn't want to be married like Tommy; she was pretty sure of that. Tommy always seemed to be granted permission by Will to do something not especially fun or selfish but for somebody else, like staying with Bernice for a couple more days. Really, his permission wasn't all that different from Nate Shirley's permission to take a couple more days off. Guess who was considered to have made the greatest sacrifice? Will and the bossman.
Out of all of them Priss seemed to know what she wanted and how to get it. She was her own boss. To stay with the Roses for a few days, she'd told her grandfather she was going and he'd told her parents where she was gone. But that seemed somehow wrong, sad too. Because with that level of unearned independence, at her age, where could she go from there?
Priss was drinking from the crock, cannonball-shaped buggy jug, water dribbling down her smooth fair chin. She handed it to Merry Gay. She shook it. They would have to get more water from the spring at the mill before starting home.
Dust chased the lazy wagon as if to catch the girls' dangling bare feet. Priss's toes were even and tanned; Merry Gay's were sloped and crooked. She had inherited her mama's tendency toward bunions but somehow she would avoid it, her gait would never go bad. How, she didn't know. It was just one more thing that was out-there, far enough away to hold promise.
At a Crossing they had to wait for a timber train to pass, the bright toot and whistle, long flat cars loaded with stacked logs snaked out of the woods to the tramcars by oxen and mules. Hot wind fanned the oily creosote and pine tar. A young woman in a blue bonnet with blue eyes was seated on the bench of the open car, facing the wagon. She watched them and they watched her till she was out of sight, going west with the train toward the moving sun. Where was she going? Merry Gay imagined the woman to be adventurous, worthy of a song.  Yes, her daddy would have written a song about the blue-eyed girl in a blue bonnet, but now he was gone.
Hot and dusty after an hour or so on the road, Bernice turned the wagon down a three-path road through the woods and along the winding creek. Cool vapors breathed from the green leaves and black water, and the smothery dust smell changed to crushed dill dog fennel where another horse-drawn wagon had drifted off track.
Soon they could hear the turning of the water wheel at the gristmill around the long curve, spellbinding as the crickets and locusts. But still they could hear no doves.
At the short plank bridge, a few yards from the shady cove of the gristmill, Priss took off her shoes and hopped down from the wagon. Pink-gingham skirt hiked up to her bare knees, she loped down the bank, through rank grass and weeds, and stepped into the clear, narrow stream. Cattails waved along the edge as she giggled and splashed, following the creek to where it fed into the dam that powered the grist mill.
The miller, a tall wiry man with a black beard, in indigo overalls, was walking the barrel of corn off the wagon when Priss climbed up out of the pooling black water of the dam. She looked pink and sweet as always but the ferns, reeds and trees on the mirror of dark water gave her skin a greenish cast. Even the large wooden wheel appeared to have taken on the green of the amazingly lush plants; but in the dappling of the sun, scum of drab nappy moss grew visible, a copper-tarnish smell you could see.
	The first time Priss had gone with them to the mill she had asked whether the wheel was built first or the elevated, off-set building above it. The rilling water scooped up by the rotating wheel would vanish inside the wood shed, charging the columnar dual stone grinders there; then dump back into the creek on the other side of the level spillway, yet the scoops always returned with what looked like the same water. The wheel reminded Merry Gay of a timber cart trying to roll up out of the water, always failing, but never giving up. 
Bernice, with her string purse on her wrist, but barely enough money to clutch in one fist, followed the miller on into the shed while Tommy and Merry Gay walked over to the dam to sit on the bank with Priss, wringing water out of her skirt tail.
Inside, the stone grinders were starting up with a hollow meshing racket. The miller spoke loud over the racket as he scooped the dry corn from the barrel into the funnel above the grinders. Then the rattling of the corn in the funnel, sifting bits of corncob and trash, and the corn shaking down into the interlacing notches of the twin stones, one stationery and the other pitching up and grinding its way down, coarse ground grits pouring into the wood hopper below.   
Corn dust was on the air, even a fine film on the water and in the girls' hair. They sat listening to the water dripping from the scoops of the wheel, coveting the white lilies afloat of the green pads. Then they heard the first dove purling out over the woods. Inside, Bernice was politely but loudly dickering over the price of milling corn. Said she'd be willing for him to mill it on halves if that suited him.
"She missed it," Priss said.
"She'll hear one when she comes out," Tommy said. "I'll shoot that damn bird if he quits cooing." She drew her feet from the pool and kneeled, then stood, heading for the wagon to get the buggy jug and refill it with the green water.
Merry Gay laughed. Laughter bouncing about the pool and returning in echo.
"Run about half of that back through in cornmeal, will you?" Bernice said to the miller. "Fine as you can get it."
The gears on the grinders switched to a low whine, then a nerve-racking grating sound. 
Tommy and Merry Gay had seen it all before and didn't care to see it again, but Priss did.
She was inside when the miller scooped the ground corn from the hopper into the funnel again, having adjusted the gauge from coarse to fine for the cornmeal. When she came out, cradling a cloth bag of grits in her arms, she was coated in corn meal dust, white from head to toe.
 Bernice with her string bag on her wrist followed with another bag of precious cornmeal. She stood a moment, looking all about and listening to the water spilling from the scoops of the wheel, back to her old self and smiling, mourning doves nothing but a whim. She had to get home and cook supper.
On the ride home Priss sat on back of the wagon again, bare feet swinging, sallow in the shade and gold shining through white in the sheen of autumn sun.


CHAPTER 5

Firelight heaped on the west side of the tracks now, building to a fast blaze at one of the Negro shanties beyond the commissary and the white sand road, the main road into and out of Moniac, which forked off at the various lanes both sides of the railroad tracks. The tracks and the main road formed a "T", with four parallel lanes each side of the tracks joining the main road, or top of the "T," and the site of the commissary in the right jut backed by woods. The fire grew brighter as the night grew darker. In the shifting wind, fire on both sides of the tracks was spreading fence to fence, house to house.
Everywhere dogs were barking and people were shouting. Fighting fire, fighting each other? Merry Gay, still hiding in the woods on the west side of the tracks, south of the commissary and the main road, tried to make out her own house, four houses down from the big white Shirley house on the north end of the row. She wanted to go home, to rest, to feed the chickens and hogs and eat supper and sit out on the porch, then go to bed-the usual. 
But first she had to go to the Shirley house, let her mother and Tommy know that she was alive, that Buck and Charlie were alive. Will? She didn't know about Tommy's Will. She did know about Priss and she would have to tell Priss's mother what had happened to her. 
Creeping closer to the white sand road and the strip of woods screening the commissary, battling mosquitoes in the thicket, she had no fear of the many poisonous snakes there. The real danger was south along the tracks at the makeshift morgue and hospital where Shirley and his men had gathered. They had set fire to a smoky fat-lightered stump, pacing in the light of blowing flames like sinners in hell.
Earlier Merry Gay had seen several of Shirley's men pass along the railroad right of way, shouting, running toward Moniac Crossing; some returning half an hour or so later on horseback and with wagons. Dogs frolicked along the tracks, one minute shying away from the shouts and gunfire, and next minute barking at Merry Gay and the others in hiding as if changing sides. Lanterns were lit at the camp now, encircling the dead and injured, like ghosts in a graveyard, or some alien voodoo ritual.
She was almost to the glowing sand road running south of the commissary and across the tracks to the east side when she saw fire break out at another of the houses somewhere behind her own house.
Then gunfire.
Smoke was sweeping east to west, combining, eerie in the phantom darkness of low clouds, snarling wind, dogs barking, and the shouts of people seeking either safety or each other. Where was safe and who was the enemy? 
She ran for cover across the sand road, ducking into the woods, that sweet green bay behind the commissary. She had to stay hid, as everybody else was doing, if she was any judge, till she could get close enough to cross the tracks without being spied by the Shirleys and their men, sure enemies. Children crying, in the woods behind the quarters and white side of the tracks, were being shushed by mothers. When she got to the opening between the commissary yard and the first Negro row shanty-the place where she had seen Will and Tommy kiss-she could see beyond the tracks and slightly to the left of the commissary-lookout her own green and turquoise house with brown ceilings, firelight and dusk meeting up the middle of the wide open hall, otherwise dark and still and untouched by fire or fury. It looked peaceful as any other nigh-fall evening after chores had been done and supper was on the table. Except for the red bricks of the well, left corner of the picket fence, and they blazed with reflected firelight, lazy spiraling of shadows like water-lights on the soap-rock banks of the creek. Still, the place seemed to Merry Gay like a church on sacred grounds.
Squinting into the dark, Merry Gay saw a man who had to be Travis Miller, their neighbor two houses up the tracks, squatting down left side of the doorsteps and holding a lit lantern into the recessed darkness beneath his front porch. Lantern in one hand, and a crying baby cradled in his other arm. Their spotted hound was tied to a wind-wrung chinaberry tree in the front yard. He lifted his pointed muzzle and howled and barked in that deep hoarse voice.
 Travis's wife Roberta scooted under the house on her belly with her two little boys crawling after her. The tall bony dog lunged at the rope, it snapped, and he took up his barking and lunging at the edge of the porch. Travis scolded him then handed Roberta the baby, mumbling something. The dog waggled, switching Travis's pants leg with his hard tail. On his feet again, Travis picked up his rifle from the porch floor and shot the dog in the head. The dog yelped and dropped to the ground, legs scissoring then overlapping, closing. The boys cried out, the baby cried, Roberta said, "How come you to do that, Travis?"
"What! And have him standing there barking at y'all for Shirley and them to butcher like they done on the train?" That said, he walked off with the rifle and his lantern swinging, along the side-yard between his house and the house next door to the Rose's.  
Just as Merry Gay was getting set to make a run for the tracks, she heard horse hooves clopping along the tree-bound lanes separating the four rows of houses on the white eastside. Deputy Threet seemed to be everywhere at once, roaring for everybody to stay in their houses, commanding the men to lay down their guns and show themselves. Merry Gay didn't have to wonder whose side he was on. He was on the side of the man with the most money and power; he was on the side of the man with the gun.
Feverish with fear, Merry Gay finally crept out of the woods, staying close along the outside stairs of the commissary that led up to the quarters of Shirley's hired single men. A man's shaving brush and mustache cup had been left on the middle stair, and there were mud splashes on the lapped white siding of the wall below where pans of bath water had been dashed. Sour mud odor in spite of the drought. Tall brick pillars underneath, and between them strips of white lattice filled the gaps. The wood scaffold under the window near the front was loaded with fresh split firewood that smelled tart; teardrops of clear resin hardened on the wall below. The oak in the front yard of the commissary was shedding its leaves to the wind like scraps of paper sack. Large nicked leaves scuttled along the packed gumbo to the white sand road leading into Moniac Crossing. Usually every evening the cows would come up and congregate under the oak, next to the wood-framed brick well, but this evening only their tracks and piled manure splats remained. Thistles of chewed sugar cane spat by children, and old men's bitter juice of tobacco. Oak gum barrels stood along the south end of the yard in a cluster of wild plum trees. An iron anvil bolted to a tree stump looked like a water turkey.  On the north end of the porch were a half-dozen huge bales of dingy Sea Island cotton, dense with seeds; only a few people grew cotton around Moniac anymore. Timber was king. 
She started out across the sandy lane ruffled with cow tracks and packed with foot tracks, the lane running parallel to the railroad tracks and end to end of the row shanties belonging to the Negroes, a like lane running before the houses on the other side belonging to the whites. Or what used to belong to them before the Shirleys came to live there.
From the rise of the tracks, she could see down the line stacked firewood to be used as fuel by the trains, then the circle of lanterns and the campfire. They appeared closer, like looking through Nate Shirley's spy glasses, as she and Priss had done. Nate Shirley who was so friendly you felt surely Travis and the others were wrong, that you yourself had to be wrong. You had to get into a certain position to view him straight-on, to see. And even then, you weren't sure.
The wind was gusting, blowing smoke and rattling loose tin on rooftops, cribs and barns. A door or shutter somewhere nearby was flapping and creaking in the wind like in a ghost town. The hectic cackling of chickens was everywhere, and a rooster crowed and crowed. A woman was pleading, crying. Or was it the wind moaning in a chimney? There were so many noises all around that Merry Gay had trouble identifying them. But the bells were no longer ringing.
She was in her own yard and it felt good, so good to be home. On her right, inside the front gate, an oak-stave barrel held a bouquet of rose bush switches that only the week before Bernice had stuck down inside to root. Now they were withered from lack of water but that seemed unimportant, something that had mattered yesterday but not today.  She knew her mother wasn't inside the long house with its open hall. She knew it because she knew the old house and all its signs of being occupied or vacant, because she knew her mother. Bernice would be right out in the open trying to round up her family, trying to fix things. She and Tommy both would. Yes, Bernice would go after Tommy first to help her look for the others, or to rescue her from the Shirleys if she herself considered them the enemy now. Did the battle include the women? Or was it only between the men who'd been drinking and would regret it tomorrow?
For some reason, it comforted Merry Gay to think it was only the men involved. She would rest in her daddy's rocker on the south end of the wide, shallow porch, then go on to the Shirleys. But then she'd have to tell about Priss, and she dreaded that.
Next door, at Jean and Anse's little unpainted shotgun house somebody was hammering on wood; double raps that sounded like a mighty Lord God woodpecker rapping on a dead tree. There was always some racket over there: Jean screaming at her baby boy and him squalling, her drubbing on a pan or a tub in stumbling beats, drubbing just to be drubbing, as if to drown out the baby's awful crying. Half the time either Roberta, Jean's neighbor on the right, or Bernice and Merry Gay, her neighbors on the left, had to rescue the baby from his mother, who'd suffered what Bernice called a nervous breakdown shortly after the baby was born. Not only did they care for the baby, they cooked for Jean's husband, Travis, when he came home, if he came home. He had been coming home less and less here lately, and as Bernice said, who could blame him, what with a crazy wife and a crying baby. Daily, it seemed, Jean's neighbors at the Crossing were giving up trying to help her, drifting away. And even Tommy and Merry Gay, who had taken up for Jean, were wearing down.  
Traveling voices up the lane along the railroad tracks and the sounds of gunfire behind her house, plus Jean's unsettling shrieks and the baby's squalling with the wind, drove Merry Gay inside, to the parlor at the front on the left side of the long, wide hall. Inside with the soothing vanilla and air-cured curtains and wood smells, undercut by camphor, the clock ticking on the mantel in the soft but not absolute dark. Home. But she couldn't stay, she couldn't stand not seeing what was coming at her, what would be her doom. 
She started to lie down on the oak bed in the right corner by the door. But the scrap-work quilt smoothed over the lumpy cotton mattress reminded her of Bernice saying she'd be glad when the sandspurs dried in so she could scatter them over the quilt and keep everybody from lying down on it in the middle of the day. 
Her quilting frame was standing before the brick fireplace. On the mantelshelf above stood the source of the camphor smell, a bottle of camphorated oil that stoppered or not mysteriously leaked the eye-burning fumes. Next to it was a cardboard cigar box. She picked it up, opened it and breathed in the familiar, piercing smell of resin from her daddy's sample cubes-a variety of shades ranging from clear topaz, like the gem itself, to muddy gray. 
Stretched side to side of the frame was another quilt, a picture puzzle of Merry Gay and her family's old dresses and shirts, and it was accented by the after-light of sundown through the tall west windows over the porch. Fine silver needles glinted, stuck in the quilt top where the trails of tiny even stitches stopped like ants. The rest of the room was in shadow, dusky corners and looming dark spelled by kinks of light.
Going out into the hall, out of habit she looked for the dainty platform rocker with ruffled leather trim, recalling that Tommy had moved it across the tracks when she moved. Her daddy had bought it for Tommy when she turned twelve and took to sitting in the hallway, either sewing or shelling butterbeans and peas. Here, she could catch the breeze when one came. Getting out of working in the garden, that's what Tommy had been doing. It had made Merry Gay so mad that her daddy had encouraged Tommy by buying the chair for her to sit and look pretty in while she and Bernice had to sweat in the garden picking butterbeans on their knees. And what had he bought for them? Running butterbean seeds. 
 On the porch again, Merry Gay sat to rest a moment in her daddy's retirement rocker behind the lattice trellis of sleeping morning glories. Still, she could see through the lattice the Negro row shanties and the commissary to the south. It should have been dark out; instead it looked like prolonged sundown from the fire-glow of burning houses east and west sides of the tracks. The sky was churning smoke and clouds, and down the tracks the circle of lanterns and the centerpiece stump with its flowering fire. She could even see the American Flag blowing from the peak of the commissary roof; she could hear it snapping like laundry hung to dry in March wind. She longed to stay, watching from her latticed viewpoint; she longed to wash the blood from her hands and change her bloody dress and shoes, clean and safe at home. 
The rapping noise next door went on. Stopped and started again. Hammer to nail, nail to wood. Closer, just beyond the north corner of the porch and over the picket fence that separated them from Jean and Anse and her insane goings on. Inside the house Jean railed, raging front to rear of the house, following the rapping outside. 
Then Anse appeared on the fire-bright side of his house facing the Rose house, a stack of short boards under his muscled left arm and a hammer in his right hand. At the first window, he stopped, dropping the boards clattering to the ground. The copper-negative camera image of a woman's face flashed at the window before Anse slammed the single shutter and latched it with the short wood bar that turned on a nail. While he hammered a board across, she hammered against the shutter with her fist, like an echo but for her harridan shrieking. Ignoring her, he took a nail from his shirt pocket and, using his left elbow to brace another board, he set hammer to nail and began driving the nail into the end of the board at the top of the shutter. And again, placing boards and setting his hammer to nails, all the way to the back of his house and the blowing shadows of flickering firelight and teetering tree shadows like stencils.  
Merry Gay crept from the porch, easy over weak squawking floorboards and down the short steps. In the wind rushing around the northwest side of the house, she made a beeline for the fence jamb overlooking the lane and the bent pear tree, limbs broken from the weight of the solid pendulous pears they had shared with Anse and Jean only weeks before, the gritty, tart fruit cobbled into sweet doughy pies for them or sometimes simply baked whole with cinnamon and sweet butter from the Rose's milk cow. Merry Gay could hear other cows bellowing in the east woods, beyond the burning fat-pine houses of her back-door neighbors whose buckets she could hear too, clanging hollow down the wells, then drawn up full, bumping solid against the brick walls. 
The natural neighborly thing to do would be to go help put out the fires, but she was too scared and confused. In her own yard, on her own place, she didn't have to confront what she didn't know. But she was scared there too. Her heart was beating too hard and her head felt light and hot.
Jean was now cursing Anse and the baby too, stamping her feet on the floor and beating on the walls; sounded like she was banging her head, Lord help her.
First of summer, on a dewy morning, Jean had waded out into the dark tilled dirt of her vegetable garden spot carrying with her Anse's straight razor. She sat on the soft cool ground and without her usual preamble began slicing the veins of her wrists, calmly, intently, as if tatting lace or smocking a baby dress, the rich red dye from her own handiwork spilling onto her lap and down into the soil. Finally she had made something, had done something of value and not to be ignored, so she was quiet then. Bernice had found her when she went out to check on a setting of eggs in the hen house-old chicken snake had eaten the setting hen's last eggs. Jean was at that point rubbing dirt into the bloody bracelets on her wrists, pale and clammy and calm. Bernice had stanched the bleeding by applying pressure to the severed arteries, then sealed the cuts with spider webs. Slowly she built up Jean's blood with beets and beef liver, and Roberta took Jean's baby boy to wet nurse with her own. 
Having boarded up all the windows and doors, Anse dropped the hammer to the clean ground of the yard butted up to the Rose yard, took up his lantern and a shotgun from the end of the front porch, then strode out the paling gate and through the live oaks along the lane near the tracks. The wind was shredding the branches, and leaves and clumps of moss tumbled like rocks along the lane.
"Anse?" Jean called from inside. The baby was crying, the wind was howling. "Anse, where are you? You get back here. What if the house catches afire?"
"Stay there! For once in your life just behave yourself." Not looking behind, he walked off south along the tracks. 
The Rose fence along the front and sides was draped in the huge heart-shaped leaves of gourd vines. It looked like a curtain of ruffled, emerald taffeta but for a few gourds still hanging, drying in to shades of mingled pottery. She ducked down behind the vines, listening and waiting. 
Voice increasing in volume, Jean shouted, "You come BACK HERE RIGHT NOW OR I'M GONE KILL MYSELF AND THIS BABY HERE BOTH, HEAR ME?" Yes, and he'd heard it before.
Anse and Jean were born and raised in Baxter, Florida, over the state line, a hop and a skip west of Moniac Crossing, but like many of the others he had turned into a Shirley man. Merry Gay would have to remind herself time and again that night that she didn't know if there actually were two sides, the Shirleys and the townspeople, as she thought, but assuming there were sides, and this was indeed war, she had no way of knowing who belonged to which. She had no idea who to hide from and who to run to. 
Jean's madness seemed suddenly less isolated and odd, under the circumstances, her raging no more remarkable than anybody else's at Moniac Crossing.
Merry Gay started to step out from behind the pear tree and the gourd vines, to follow the paling fence to the back gate that opened into her neighbor's yard. Get the hammer dropped on the ground and rip the boards from Jean's door and set her free. She would fit in fine on this stormy night with the crazy lot of them. But suddenly some motion up the lane along the tracks caught her eye; some woman in a white blouse and dark skirt walking fast on the other side of the live-oak row, long bushy hair fanning in the wind and arms swinging with grace and purpose. Not at all like the mindless motion of the others Merry Gay had been put upon to watch since she and Priss had entered the smoking car that evening. And yet there was about her posture and bearing a kind of sad but triumphant apathy: since I don't matter, I have nothing to fear.
The blowing firelight across the tracks at one of the houses burned down to level flaming ceils and beams ignited the woman's broad pale face. 
"Tommy," Merry Gay called low.
Tommy stopped, body and breath on hold like a spied rabbit.
"Tommy, it's me, Merry Gay. Over here."
"Who's out there?" Jean called inside the house. The baby who had paused from crying, previously unnoticed by Merry Gay, picked up crying again. Crying to be crying, like the men shooting to be shooting. Jean told him in a gentle voice to shut up. Then loud, "Shut up, I say!" Then in a nasal voice clogged with breathing she began singing what sounded like a church hymn, a funeral lullaby that seemed to fit with the mayhem of burning, shouting and crying.
Tommy walked slowly, hesitantly, from the lane to the fence jamb, in burning shadows, as if unable to believe her eyes or trust her ears. So much racket all around, and overhead the blowing moss hanging down from an extended branch of the huge oak looked orange and eerie, as if caught fire too.
"Merry Gay? Merry Gay. You're alive? They said?" She reached over the fence, over the gourd vines, embracing Merry Gay, rocking and laughing low. She felt soft and boneless, her brittle hair smelled like hay. Then she stepped back, peering down with her heavy eyebrows crooked. "Why are you all bloody? Your skirt, your hands?" She was whispering, hissing. "Where's Will? Where's Buck and Charlie? Why isn't Priss with you?"
Merry Gay shook her head, unable to speak about Priss yet. "Is Mama okay? Does she think I'm dead?"
"She said no. Miss Moxie said yes, you and Priss both. Mama says, 'When trouble breaks out my Merry Gay, she heads for a hiding place. Won't come out till it's over.' She said you and Priss would be on home soon as the shooting stops."
"Priss is dead, Tommy. Priss is gone." Merry Gay's voice broke, she sobbed with the wailing of the baby inside the boarded up house.
"Who's out there?" Jean called. "Please don't set my house afire, please."
Tommy was shaking her head; her square white face in the firelight was like a target saying "here." Too much face, too much hair, not at all lovely up close, but doll-like from a distance. "Maybe she's not dead."
"She is. Nate Shirley shot her."
"Mr. Nate? Why?"
"He thought it was Buck and Charlie hiding in the lavatory. He shot through the door."
"My God! Is that her blood?" She held Merry Gay's bloody hands by the wrists, staring from them to her skirt. "What about the boys? Where were they?" 
They began walking toward the front gate, pacing alongside each other, talking low over the fence. 
"They rode the rods to Moniac Crossing, then bailed off the train and took to the woods." Merry Gay opened the gate for Tommy to pass through.
"Please don't set this house afire," Jean called. "Please."
"Anse boarded up the windows and doors and took off," Merry Gay explained to Tommy. She almost envied Jean her childlike circumstances: locked up where nobody could get to her, safe and blameless, everybody else in charge and nothing expected of her but to stay put. 
"Maybe he did right. Her like she is, crazy and all." Tommy looked pensive, watching Jean's house, the firelight playing over the slanted boards and shutters. "But she's right too, about fire and her boarded up in there. The women in the quarters took to the woods soon as they heard."
"From the train too. Before it stopped good, the men were bailing off and running fast as their feet would carry them."
"Will's in the woods too, I guess?" 
"I don't know, Tommy. I saw him earlier, but now I don't know." Even if she had the time, Merry Gay couldn't have brought herself to tell where and under what circumstances she'd last seen Will. Recalling what she'd witnessed of his involvement in the fighting on the train, she figured he was either dead or wounded at the camp south along the tracks. But she didn't say that either. 
"Is Mama at the big house?" Merry Gay asked.
"She was, but she left, looking for you. Told me to stay and wait in case you showed up there. Then I left to look for you and Will. This storm?" She looked up at the sky, black frothy hair back-blowing around her face. 
"I know. They said in St. Augustine there's a hurricane moving in off the coast." Merry Gay gazed up with her, red-stained clouds churning. "Tommy, what is everybody saying happened? I can't make sense of this."
"Some kind of uprising against the Shirleys, is what they say.  Mr. Nate sent word for everybody to lock up good and look out. Somebody, I think Miss Moxie, said they hadn't oughta let the niggers load up on the train first. Mostly they're just scared as the rest of us; Muffin's in labor and Mr. Nate's got a hurt arm. Mr. Jay went missing, don't nobody know where. But it's Priss they're mostly looking for?and you?said you was s'posed to be looking out for her."
Gunfire erupted closer, across the tracks, sending Merry Gay and Tommy running for cover in the shadows along the north side of the Rose house. They hid behind the chimney, leaning into the cool clay bricks, waiting, remarking to each other with their eyes. When the shooting quit, they stood a while longer listening to the wind and the katydids and maybe the last whippoorwill of summer, seeking out other normal night sounds and smells. Merry Gay recalled the smells of wood smoke and supper cooking and the sounds of forks on plates, pots and pans clattering and stove wood dumped on kitchen floors. Children would be laughing and people would be talking low on their front porches. All that remained now of the way it was before was the smell of wood smoke from the houses on fire.
Just as Tommy and Merry Gay were going through the back gate, to go after the hammer that Anse had dropped in the yard and rip the boards loose, freeing Jean, they heard a mob of men on horseback and saw their torches bobbing up the lane along the tracks. Quickly they ran back to the Rose house and scrambled underneath the kitchen floor next to the back porch. 
"Gone get rid of all them damn Shirleys," one of the men yelled.
 "Yeah! Troublemakers," said another.
 "Land thieves."
 "And murderers?don't forget that."
Merry Gay wondered if Tommy was remembering their last time in the same spot under the house, when they were children, wondering what the town would do to them. It seemed simpler then, knowing now the outcome. But Tommy seemed just as vulnerable, as much object and target of their whimsy as before. Only this time there were no adults standing over them, watching over them, nobody to run to inside. Tommy and Merry Gay were the grownups now. And this was more than talk. 
"Let's head out the back way, see if we can find Mama," Merry Gay said. "She probably doubled back, looking for me to show up at the Shirley's, me and Priss." Jean's baby inside the house next door was crying steady and strong. "Providing they don't set her house afire, Jean's better off in there."
"But the baby?" Tommy grabbed Merry Gay's arm. "What if she hurts the baby?"
Merry Gay turned at the gate, whispering. "We can't get him out without her. Even if we could, his crying would draw too much attention to us." She walked on through the gate. "Besides, I'm not so sure that Jean's any crazier than the rest of us."
"You mean them, right?" Tommy was following close. 
"I'm not sure who them is, Tommy." Merry Gay vanished into the shadow of the hellish house next door. "I'm not even sure about myself."
Nearing the backyard belonging to Roberta and Travis, on the other side of Jean's, they could hear in the east and across the tracks the crackle and lashing of burning houses, and behind them the baby crying and Jean yelling her head off, and from the Shirley yard the whooping mob. Light everywhere it seemed except where they were walking, that safety of silence and darkness. Then close-by a woman humming a tune, sucking in her breath and going quiet as she spied Merry Gay and Tommy.
"Who's there?" she called out. "I got a gun! Ain't scared to use it neither."
"Roberta," Tommy said, "it's me and Merry Gay. Don't shoot." The burning house next row over suddenly flared in the wind parting the bushes and trees and they could see each other.
Roberta was seated against the wall on the small back porch near the open door, draped in quilts and children. Her baby was nursing at her breast, one small hand spread flat on her soft creamy flesh possessively. Nothing soft about Roberta but those milk-plumped breasts. She was stocky, hard and strong as any man. Her massive shoulders put them to shame, and she took pleasure in her strength. When she was a little girl, her goal had been to get strong enough to tip and roll a barrel of gum up the pole runners at her daddy's turpentine still. "I'm yet to get the straight of what-all's going on. Y'all?" Even her voice was husky.
She was talking too loud. Merry Gay moved closer, whispering what she knew, keeping it brief; while Tommy stood behind her, listening and watching.
In lieu of comment, Roberta began rearranging the baby at her breast. He whimpered and she hushed him by shoving a nipple into his mouth. "Y'all go get me that boy of Jean's fore she does him harm." 
 A rooster crowed from a wooden post on the northeast corner of her yard, and they could see his feathers in the light of the fire like ruffled soot in a chimney.
"Anse's boarded up the windows and doors, Roberta," Merry Gay said.
"I figured as much," Roberta said, "all that hammering. Looks like he could've brung the baby to me." 
 "We're out looking for Mama," Merry Gay said, in part to distract her. "Have you seen her?" 
"I still don't see how y'all two got split up on the train." Roberta stopped talking to listen to an outburst of whoops from the direction of the Shirley house. "Seen somebody come by a while ago but couldn't tell who it was. Didn't walk like Bernice though."
"I thought I saw Travis putting you and the children up under the house earlier." Merry Gay was on the doorsteps now, and Tommy was still standing in the yard, waiting, making herself invisible. 
"Heck! I ain't staying scooched up under no house with all these younguns. They come shooting at me, I'll shoot back, what I say. Whole bunch is gone crazy as that rooster out there. They been just itching for the chance to jump on each other," she said. "How come me not to go on that train ride, me and Travis both."  She hummed a laugh, then coughed as a gust of windborne smoke banked and curled over her and the children against the wall.
Tommy and Merry Gay could count on her being armed with the pistol Travis got for her. She always had it with her to shoot snakes or mad foxes, coons and dogs, or even normal dogs into mischief; her neighbors could look out for their dogs to go missing if Roberta caught them near her chicken yard. Her old granddaddy had taught her to shoot. Said aim for the stomach, if it's a man, and don't let him get close enough to take the pistol and use it on you. Even with an animal, one shot may be all you get so make it count. It was a golden rule with him, and passed down to Roberta: the point is to be predator, not prey; the point is to live another day.
 "That blood on you?" she asked Merry Gay, when the smoke had cleared.
The sleepy-headed boys each side of her sat up. The baby was making mewling sounds.
"We've gotta go," Tommy said. "When we come back by we'll go get Jean's baby for you. If you see Mama, tell her Merry Gay's all right."
Tommy didn't take up any time with Roberta, never had. She figured Roberta, being born white, was her born enemy. She figured Roberta thought she was lazy because she was part-Negro-all colored people were lazy in Roberta's book-plus weak because she was small. But closer to the truth was the fact that Tommy moved slow, paced herself, and refused to do chores at odds with her nature. Like her sitting in the breezeway shelling butterbeans and peas while Merry Gay and Bernice picked them in the garden out back. Bernice had threatened to take a switch to Tommy at first, and Merry Gay was jealous. Her daddy pointed out that somebody had to cook and keep house and didn't Tommy look fine sitting so. For a fact, she would sew on buttons, mend pants, bake cakes; just don't let her get dirty or sweaty. Besides, who knew when and under what circumstances she might make a misstep and the town would come down on her again?
"Listen out for Jean," Merry Gay said to Roberta. "See they don't set her house afire."
The mob was already inside the white picket fence of the Shirley's grassed front yard. No, two mobs: one there with Deputy Threet to protect the Shirleys and the other hellbent on getting rid of them. The Shirley men were posted on the right side of the yard, and the Moniac protestors on the left. A single lamp burned in back of the house, the kitchen area, with its many mullioned windows. The split louvered shutters were open, in spite of the coming storm, but the filmy white curtains were drawn.
Deputy Threet with his shotgun drawn was seated on the right baluster of the front doorsteps facing the tracks. "Now I don't give a damn for Shirley and his boys neither, but I ain't gone let them women and babies in there suffer for what that old coot and his boys done. He's bunged up his arm and the doc's in there with him. Got a sick baby too and another one bout to be born. Soon as Shirley's able, I'll throw him out here to y'all. Hear? Then I'm gone take the doc down the tracks there to see to them shot-up men."
Grumbling, roaring, and shouting from the crowd. Torches twirled fire and scattered it to the wind. Still a good stiff wind but an outcropping of stars overhead was shining between layers of roving clouds. The horses beyond the front gate neighed and pranced and sidestepped in the fusing shadows and firelight. But Merry Gay knew that Deputy Threet had said the right thing, the right thing to stay on both sides. The good people of Moniac wouldn't want him not doing his duty by the women and children, even the Shirley women and children, who they had no gripe with. They wouldn't respect him either for throwing a hurt man to the wolves. He was a bastard, but he was theirs.
Tommy and Merry Gay were hiding behind the conical cedar trees lining the outside of the white picket fence, south side of the winged white house, the finest house in all of Moniac, the only fine house in all of Moniac. A fine house by reputation and historical bearing more than design, though everybody knew it had been erected of only the choicest heart pine, that the windows and doors and moldings and such had been imported from England by the grandfather of Travis Miller. 
Old Man Miller had made it big in Charlton County by buying up timberland and operating sawmills. He had died many years before and Travis's father had taken over the house and the business, but he was never the businessman his own father was; Nate Shirley, who would have been a match for the wily old man, had a nose for sniffing out the weak and soon came by the Miller house and land. Travis was maybe sixteen then, a boy with all the inherent shrewdness and grit of the Old Man developing within him, body, soul, and brain. He couldn't prove it but he knew in that same soul that Nathan Shirley had waylaid, or one of his men had waylaid, his daddy at the cemetery behind the big house while he was squatting at the grave of his wife, shot him dead on the spot where he would be buried next to her.
 Because of timber mismanagement practices, and an assortment of bad trades, Travis's daddy was well on his way to losing the old home place, along with Old Man Miller's other numerous investments, before he got waylaid in the cemetery. By the end of the year the place would have been auctioned by the bank in Folkston anyway. What really galled Travis most, according to his wife Roberta, was the fact that Shirley had treated Travis like the helpless boy he was at the time, that he'd killed or had killed a man for the house he'd tried to buy not a month before, that he'd killed a man for the land the very cemetery was on where he shot him. That it was simpler like that, to shoot and be done with Travis's daddy, was the unpardonable sin against Nate Shirley.
"You think Mama's in there?" Tommy peeked around one of the close-set cedar trees. Across the pruned-grass strip of yard were four inset curtained mullions in a line. The lamp was still lit but there were no sounds, not even a whisper. "She could be delivering Muffin's baby."
"I thought the doctor was in there."
"I doubt Mr. Nate and them's gone spare him right now just to deliver a lil ole baby."
"Will you go in there, Tommy? I can't. I can't face up to Priss's mother just yet. So, don't tell them I'm out here, just get word to Mama I'm okay. Tell her Buck and Charlie are okay."
Tommy and Merry Gay ducked down and ran for the rectangular grapevine arbor silhouetted by the fire behind the Shirley house. Leaning into the arbor struts, barely breathing in the rotted grape and smoke smells. Then Tommy, keeping in the shadows, dropped low behind the picket fence all the way to the back gate in darkness. Minutes later, Merry Gay saw her suddenly appear on the doorsteps and as suddenly slip through the door like a cat.
Listening to the overlapping voices of the mob, Merry Gay thought she recognized the amplified timid voice of Tynce Withers, who lived with his big family two rows behind the Rose house. Maybe this was a prank, she thought, even knowing better. Men at the Crossing were always playing pranks on one another, especially Hoss and his buddies.
Hoss had butchered a beef for the commissary, first of last winter, which usually brought in customers by the droves, as he said. Three days and the beef would go bad, so it was a must that he sell what he could as soon as he could and give the rest away. He had waited on the porch.
The usual straggled in: little old ladies come for their meal, flour and cheese, some sewing thread, what have you.
"I reckon you ladies'll be wanting some this sweet tender beef," he'd say.
"Not today, Hoss. But thank you all the same."
The turpentine hands would drop by after work, plus two or three of Hoss's buddies to jaw.
The beef was going on its second day, still cool between its covers of fresh pine branches, in the wooden box on the north end of the porch. The north wind was blowing like air off ice. Like a hawker at a fair, Hoss would lift the branches of layered green needles to show the bloody fat-marbled beef, the marbling now fading from yellow to white-not a good sign.
When they left, Hoss sat in his chair on the porch, cold with the sun going down, scratching his head under his hat. It was the beatingest thing why nobody came after his beef.
Only thing left to do was give it away.
He tried that, driving his wagon with the box of beef on back, door to door, east and even west of the tracks. But people would grin and shake their heads-"No thanks."
Last house, two rows behind his own, he stopped at the little shotgun house of Tynce Withers. He was a stropping red-headed hog farmer with a fat wife and a house full of younguns. Took all he could do to keep them fed and in shoes. Not that Tynce was one to complain. Couple sides of beef wouldn't be nothing for the wife by herself to put away.
Light inside and all kinds of racket. Smoke of bacon frying and oak-smoke from a fire when Tynce opened the door to greet Hoss. Ugliest children you ever did see flocked in the doorway peeping round their daddy's long legs. All red-headed and freckle-faced, rotted teeth and snot.
"Got you a box of beef out here in the wagon," said Hoss, adding, "for free." He reached out to pat one of the little girls on the head. She dodged behind her daddy. "Cute lil ole booger," said Hoss. Even the old brindle dog on the porch was none too friendly. Just lay in a curl with his long tail wrapped around like a coiled snake. Course it was cold, so who could blame the dog?
"Y'all get on back now, you younguns." Tynce stepped out to the porch, closing the door behind him. "You ain't sold that beef to yet?"
"It's the dangdest thang," said Hoss. "Can't give it away, don't look like." 
Tynce was barefooted, in overalls and no coat; Hoss was bundled up, shaking.
"Let's set down on the doorsteps here," Tynce said and stepped across the narrow sloped porch and sat, long white toes curling under the edge of the third step. Hands latched between his spread legs.
Eager to go, to warm by the stove in his own kitchen while he ate some of the fried steak Bernice would have waiting, Hoss sat down next to Tynce, watching the wagon in the glow of his lantern on the bench. A few dogs wandered around the wheels and the mule snorted, blowing white smoke.
"Tell you like it is, Hoss," said Tynce.
"I'd prechate it." Hoss hung his head. What the hell!
Tynce looked at him then with those warm green speckled eyes. "Word's got around that yo' beef ain't no good."
A cold bluish backdrop of light silhouetted the wagon and the mule like a calendar picture in the month of February.
"No good!" Hoss sat straight up, warm suddenly in spite of his frosty breath.
 "You recollect that old cow got hit by the train week or so ago?"
"I do." 
"All fevered up and limping around?"
"Somebody ought've put her out of her misery," said Hoss.
"Well, word is, you did."

***

Maybe Merry Gay shouldn't have sent Tommy inside the Shirley house, she thought. Maybe Tommy was afraid of the Shirleys now. Her working for them had seemed a legitimate excuse for her passing in and out-the back door, the back door only. 
A few more minutes and she stepped out the door alone and floated in her long dark skirt and white blouse into the shadows of the yard and without a sound was standing next to Merry Gay under the grapevine arbor. 
"She's not here. Muffin's in labor and Miss Moxie's bout to have a heart attack. Mr. Nate and the rest of them are walking the floor over Priss. But he says long as she's with you she's likely okay."
"I shouldn't have left her, Tommy." Merry Gay leaned against one of the stout arbor posts. "She begged me not to. She was so scared, she looked so little and weak."
Tommy stepped over and put an arm around Merry Gay's shoulders. She was shaking. "I can't picture her scared, you know that? Her white and rich, I can't picture that."
Breathing in the smoke filtered through the green leaves of the grapevine, shoes resoled with sticky grape pulp, they watched the light from the torches of the mobs and listened to them quarreling with each other, with Deputy Threet. 
"I tell you what I can picture now, Tommy. I can picture Nate Shirley shooting Travis's daddy in the cemetery back there." Merry Gay nodded northeast, that section of dark woods where the cemetery contained their dead families, her own daddy included. "Mama didn't do us any favors trying to keep peace with the Shirleys. None of us were fooled."
"It wadn't about peace," Tommy said and took her arm away. "It was about living, making a living. Same as me."
"Let's go." Merry Gay started walking, ducking low, toward the south end of the arbor. "Mama's probably over at Miss Tilly's, staying with her till this is over."
From there, in the dark and fusing firelight, they could pick out only the general direction of Miss Tilly's house, two rows east and not a single light on that end of the row. 
"We'll only scare Miss Tilly to death if she's holed up, hiding," Tommy said.
"Scary as she is, she's liable to shoot us for one of the Shirleys."
 "What if that mob mistakes us for some of the Shirleys?"
"Or the other way around," said Merry Gay. "Who's really against who here, do you know?" Her throat was raw from singing and screaming.
"I'll bet Mama circled back looking for you and she's at the house by now," Tommy said. "Maybe Will's home by now." Tommy's clean neat bungalow was on the second row, west-side of the tracks, a straight shot from the front of the Shirley house. All was dark on that end of the quarters but the two fired houses in the middle were spreading smoke and flames. Stark chimney's standing like monuments to the dead.
"Tommy, I don't think Will's home. I think he's for sure hiding out somewhere like Buck and Charlie and them." Then Merry Gay told about the fight in the smoking car, but not about sweet humble Will as caught up in the bloodletting as the boldest of them. Really, she suspected that he was one of the dead or wounded at the camp south along the tracks.
The wind tore at their hair and skirts and there was a sprinkle of rain, no stars now, only curdled smoke and clouds. Weary from walking and holding their breath and dodging gunfire and yesterday's friends happening up out of the shadows as enemies, they held hands to keep from getting separated and for comfort, creeping through the backyard gardens, around trees and through gates, toward the house where they'd both been raised. It would be so dark at times that they would stumble into walls of wells and fences, or almost hang themselves on clotheslines, and then again they would be out in the open, exposed as in daylight by the spreading house and outbuilding fires along the three lanes, east across the fence. Hogs were snorting and squealing, scuttling through the dark, dogs barking, cattle lowing and chickens cackling.
 Just before they reached Roberta's yard, south along the back fence line of the first row of houses, they passed by the camp barn and lot. A dark horse ripped through the burning door of the barn, a hellish explosion of whinnying and splintering wood and fire. It sprang through the vine-draped tree limbs, topping the fence, almost trampling them. 
Arms shielding her head, too scared to scream but screaming inside, Merry Gay clung to Tommy's hand. They were shaking, both of them shaking. Up ahead they could hear Jean's baby wailing, and behind, the mobs' swollen voices, and Merry Gay longed to go back to the seeming safety of the Shirley house, like Deputy Threet switching to the winning side. But more than that she wanted her mother, like Priss wanting her own mother earlier on the train, cut off from her by a mere nightmare only a blink of the eye away from over.
Tommy was thinking something else. "At least they ain't set fire to Jean's house yet." But thinking about Bernice too. "Mama'll fix it."
Roberta was still sitting on her porch, singing to the whimpering children. All pale in the faint glow of firelight through the pecan trees. Her nasal kenting was sweet as slow rain on the roof in the swoon before sleep.
"Roberta," Merry Gay called low, "it's just us again, me and Tommy."
"What they got going up there?at the Shirley house?" She sounded sick or scared, one.
 "They're fussing and griping with that old deputy," Tommy said.
"You see Travis anywhere?"
"No," said Merry Gay, "we couldn't make out anybody from where we were hiding. Have you seen Mama yet?"
"I ain't, honey." Roberta shifted her clutch of sleeping children. "I seen a big man following right in behind y'all. Looked like that Elton boy, so y'all be on the lookout. He about got loose during all the commotion. Or could be the twins is dead and ain't nobody left to keep him in." She paused and then wearily went on talking. "Why don't y'all go get Jean's boy and then come set with me up here and wait on Bernice. She'll be along by and by, and won't be long before they kill each other off or call it quits." 
Merry Gay decided that Roberta was scared, and that made her more scared; not of the Elton boy-she and Tommy both had long ago overed that. But yes Merry Gay would like to do nothing but sit with Roberta and the children, maybe close her eyes. Roberta's house seemed safe and removed enough from the main action and the fires; it seemed a good place to look out for Bernice.  Jean's beating on the walls and doors and the baby's crying didn't even seem so unnerving here with Roberta in charge.
She started up the doorsteps, but Tommy yanked her back. "We'll get that baby to you soon as we can, Roberta," she said. 
"I've nussed him so long, seems like my own. But I reckon he won't starve, and Jean's a sight better off shut up than running loose. I got enough on my hands as it is." She looked down at her sprawled sleeping boys and the baby at her breast, no longer sucking, but sleeping, pacified by her nipple. 
Thunder sounded off in the east, followed close by lightening, illuminating a mountain-like ridge of iron clouds. The rooty smell of hogs in the parlor off the swamp carried on the stiff smoky wind.
"Storm oughta sober em up," Roberta said. 
Merry Gay laughed. Tommy didn't; she was rearing to go and didn't want to get Roberta started on the subject of men.
 Before Tommy married Will, Roberta had a serious little talk with her. She said Negro men are built like stallions, so be sure he mounts you from behind. Had Tommy so scared she started not to get married, and none of Bernice's serious little talks did a bit of good as antidote. The image had been struck on both Tommy's and Merry Gay's imagination, and maybe even Bernice's. That entire week before the wedding it was like a herd of stallions were stampeding the house, cunning, menacing. None realized how enchanted the other was till the morning after the wedding and Tommy walked into the kitchen and said, "It ain't so." And nobody even asked what ain't so.
But this time Merry Gay knew that Roberta was only trying to make light of something serious. The riff between the men may have started out with bullish behavior and alcohol, but it had gone beyond that.  
"You want to come with us?" Merry Gay asked Roberta, hoping she'd say no. All those scared children would only delay their search and make them easy targets for Shirley's men. Suddenly Merry Gay wondered why, who. Why a target and who would want to kill them? Their men, all men, their protectors turned savage? Nothing made sense if not the theory of a drunken fight evolving into war between the Shirley men and the old Moniac families. "We'll be back to check on you." 
"Do. I'll keep a lookout for fire and bust Jean out at the first sign."
The kitchen door off the hall of the Rose house was open, flung wide. The room was dim with refracted firelight and felt warm coming in out of the wind, and again the ticking clock on the mantel in the parlor beyond the connecting dining room wall soothed Merry Gay.
