But it unnerved her too. She wanted to know what time it was, how much time had passed, how soon the morning.
"Mama," she called low. Then Tommy called. Both their voices trembling, them seeking in the dark as they passed from the kitchen to the dining room and the pie safe with its nutmeg smell.
"This is crazy," Tommy said. "If she was here she'd answer." 
"If she could," Merry Gay said. "Let's check the bedrooms. See if she's been here or something." She could be near-dead from a gunshot wound, she could be dead on her feet, literally.
They separated, checking the three lofty dark bedrooms, across the hall, moving among the old beds and dressers, chairs and tables charted in memory. Bernice never changed the placement of the furniture like most women. Things were where they belonged and they belonged to her.
In the fire-bright hallway, together again, Merry Gay said, "She's not here." 
"She's been here though," said Tommy, holding something up. "Miss Moxie's hateful ole ugly shoes."
 Merry Gay laughed. "So she has been here!" She quit laughing. "But where is she now?"
"She's all right, Merry Gay, I know she is." Tommy was holding one shoe in each hand, swinging them. "Yeah, she's just gone looking for you and Priss. I say we stay here awhile, wait for her to come to us."
They sat on the long bench in the hallway, between the parlor and dining room, in the confusing flicker of firelight and the on-again, off-again gunfire and shouts. West side of the tracks, then east side, at the commissary they could see if only they had the nerve to step out on the front porch. It was that bright out, from the fires. 
Merry Gay was shaking, chilled in the wind streaming through the hallway. Shoulder to shoulder with Tommy, so close her hair tickled Merry Gay's face and made it itch. She felt itchy all over from need of sleep. Both sat with their backs resting against the dull green wall that looked greener in the firelight, sheen they'd never noticed before to the brown bead-board ceiling. Every shadow on the floor from the wavering fire took on the shape of Bernice gimping along; every creak of the house resisting the force of the wind took on the sound of Bernice gimping along the hall. They'd heard those heavy rocking footsteps so many times.
Merry Gay sat forward, her over-sized shadow looming up the hall. "Let's go in the parlor. My back is killing me." 
Tommy only sat there. In a daze, dozing? Suddenly she stood, maybe ashamed of falling asleep or maybe toughened by her new harder life across the tracks more than Merry Gay had realized. Fights often broke out on the west side, always had. Hoss Rose, bossman before Shirley, would be sent for to settle the fights, to scold them like children and send them off to their own beds. But Merry Gay wondered how often Tommy had fretted over just such an eruption to her peace when somebody new to Moniac Crossing figured her for white, a white woman shacking up with a colored man. Or married, which was worse. Had she gone to work keeping house for the Shirleys to gain Nate Shirley's protection? "The man" who knew the facts, that the blood running through her veins was close enough to black and would defend her and Will both from the red-blooded newcomers he hired.

***

Angels again, following Miss Ellie's chimney fire and her blurted announcement of who for the record had birthed Booger Boy, Merry Gay and Tommy were back in school, back in church, the Rose household was back to normal.
A few Easters later, Merry Gay in a white robe was singing at Sunrise service. All area churches had come together in one accord to celebrate a risen Savior. On the second stanza of "He Arose," facing out at the solemn morning faces of the town, against a backdrop of rosy sunlit windows, she saw for the first time up close Nathan Shirley and his little granddaughter Priss.
He stepped through the door, hat in hand, and stood waiting for Priss to enter behind him. She had on a pink billed hat tied under her soft chin and looked shy. He closed the door easy and remained standing there with her, fixated on the girl in the white robe singing in the pulpit. In his fine gray and chalk-striped suit, he looked too important for the tiny white church with its tall steeple; his look of reverence and awe were too much, meant to sway attention toward him. But he could have been the risen Christ standing in the doorway with those downcast humble eyes. 
Melted wax from the lit white candles on the pulpit table had produced a soft fragrant smoke, like incense, mingling with the Easter lilies in baskets each side of the door. 
When Merry Gay was done singing, he nodded, then led Priss to the middle pew on the right and sat down beside her. All eyes on him and knowing it.
Tommy, in a white robe too, her wild hair tamed into a ponytail, floated up into the pulpit and began leading the benediction from the back of her maroon hymnal. Face as pure as the light, she enunciated each word and kept her eyes down, staring at the book, while the congregation read their response. That she'd been changed by her encounter with the town, her sudden knowing that she was obliquely unique, was masked by her very presence in the all-white church who had accepted her as white, so white that a few years later they would condemn her marriage to a Negro man. 
To Merry Gay Tommy had seemed torn in two, after their near-rape at the creek and Tommy's rape by the town. She didn't know where she belonged, so at times she belonged only to herself. Going barefoot in winter and wandering off alone, sullen and gnawing on her lips; eating on the back porch of the Rose house instead of with the family in the kitchen. Then Tommy would seem to forget and be one of them again, laughing, playing-Merry Gay's playmate, beloved sister and daughter.
After the sunrise service, outside in the blooming green and fuchsia light of azaleas and pines, Shirley strutted up from the crowd in their homemade Easter garb to speak to Hoss.
Smiling slyly with his hands caught behind, he said, "Amazing. Simply amazing. Has she had any formal training?"
"Who?" Hoss knew; he was just sucking up the compliment as modestly as he knew how.
"Your daughter, Merry Gay."
"She was born with it." Hoss watched his girls carrying baskets full of Easter lilies down the church steps. 
"I hear you play a mean fiddle; I play the violin." Shirley watched them too.
Hoss only stood there, maybe expecting an invitation to pair up, or more likely a showdown.
 "I've decided that the more I know about you folks here, the less I know." 
Hoss crossed his arms over his broad chest. "How's that?"
Speaking low and under his breath and turning to look behind, for effect as much as anything, it seemed, Shirley said, "That girl, Tommy. She's Negro, right?"
"What's your point, Shirley?" Suddenly Hoss looked puffed up. "You wouldn't be asking if you didn't know."
"Just curious," he said. "Just curious. Generally in the South folks are more picky about their dealings with Negroes."
"I hear you!"
Shirley laughed, leaned closer than was acceptable at Moniac Crossing. He whispered, "Tell me about this Nancy McCall, your other girl's mother?Tommy, I believe her name is. Why is Nancy McCall's name still used to describe a cheaper grade of gum?"
"Well, you know how some things just stick in your craw," Hoss said. The girls were ready to go; Hoss had been ready to go. "A fine woman, Nancy McCall. No finer."
"Man to man, I'd like to know how things work around here." Shirley caught hold of Hoss's elbow as he started to go. "Know what I mean?"
"I'm beginning to."
"Nigger in the woodpile, reversed. Right? Or is that girl there just your own personal poontang?"
Hoss glared at him, then made as if to walk off, but faced him square. "You being new here, and it Easter Sunday and all, I'm gonna let that slide for now. Next time, I'm gonna dirty up that fine suit for you and make a frown out of that smile."
That evening on the Rose's front porch, at sunset, Hoss in his rocker called out for Merry Gay inside the house to come listen.
She stepped out on the porch and listened. Some strange instrument up the lane was playing music. Up and down the tracks, white blooms of dogwoods shouted from yards and woods.
"What is it?" she asked her daddy, sidling over to his chair.
"A violin. A violin at Moniac Crossing!"
"Who?"
"Nate Shirley. Sounds like he's on his front porch or in the yard."
 The music was beautiful but eerie, in the guttering evening light, in plain old Moniac Crossing. The closest she'd ever come to such a sound was the long, plaintive notes of the train whistles. She felt annoyed by this music, almost, but drawn to it at the same time. "Let's go see," she said.
"No." Hoss who never said simply no without explanation, repeated it again. "No."
She listened closer, waiting for Hoss to explain. Not sparing her innocent ears a single word, he told her what Nate Shirley had said about Tommy that morning. And told her to see to it that Tommy never went near the scoundrel if she could help it.
Shocked by her daddy telling her that, she only stood there.  Then she recognized the melody. "`He Arose,'" she said.
"I wouldn't be surprised," Hoss said, "I wouldn't be surprised."

***

On the davenport in the parlor, Merry Gay and Tommy listened to the clock tick in the creeping camphor fumes and watched the firelight from across the tracks cut figures on the white curtains and the wide pine floorboards. They were too filthy to be sitting on the tapestry davenport but it seemed not to matter then; what mattered was what they could see and hear and hope for, not heaven exactly but not this hell either. They could hear the baby crying next door, but Jean had quit pounding on the walls. Now and then a pistol shot and the coarse raucous voice of Deputy Threet were carried on the wind, dogs barking and tin shrieking only background racket for the action.
Merry Gay said, "We have to find Mama." The smell of dried blood sickened her in the close room. "I know she's out looking for us now."
"You know what I think?" Tommy's voice sounded too loud, too high-pitched but that didn't matter either.
"That she's like Will, hiding out till it's over?"
Tommy paused and then spoke lower. "If Will was in the woods with the others he'd get word to me, he'd come after me."
Merry Gay wished she hadn't mentioned Will; she knew Tommy was right. "Maybe he has been looking, like Mama's been looking. Maybe we should just stay here till morning or go to Roberta's." She tried to make out the ticking clock on the mantel, to see what time it was. But really she was afraid to look, afraid to know. The wind seemed as endless as the day. "I'll be glad when it starts raining," she said. "At least it'll be something; at least it'll be a change."
"What about everybody out there though?the people in the woods? I wonder will they come back home anyway when it starts raining."
"God, Tommy! I can't sit here." Merry Gay stood and walked to the window, looking out at the fire behind Roberta's house, bare rafters strung with blowing fire. Another fire had cropped up on the west side of the tracks, fire racing along the fat pine fence-rails like rats. She didn't tell Tommy.
"If I know Mama," Tommy said, sighing with her head back, "she's stopping off house to house, helping out. What I guess we oughta be doing."
"She was too tired last I saw of her, Tommy. But maybe she would after all." Merry Gay leaned into the wall by the window, looking out at the pale flickering firelight on the bricks of the well. Nothing was changing.
"You remember that time before I got married and we went swimming in the creek and got ticks? How Mama stripped down and made us strip down and we checked each other head to foot?"
"What does that have to do with anything now?" Merry Gay could hear her breathing like the breathing of the chimney, the downdraft of wind.
"I guess it don't. I was just remembering how fun and funny she was. I was thinking how I looked at her poor old saggy, warped body and couldn't believe that could ever happen to mine."
"Me too. I guess I thought that." Merry Gay yawned.
"But now it doesn't seem like that. I mean, it does, that I can wear out and give out and die even."
"Priss was so young, Tommy. So sweet and tough." Merry Gay started crying again.
"What will Mr. Nate do when he finds out he killed her."
"How will he find out?"
"You'll tell him. That's how. I don't know when or how, but you'll tell him."
"You mean, to get back at him for killing Priss and my brothers too if he catches up with them. For swindling us and everybody else out of their land? Is that why all this happened, do you think?"
"That, and for rubbing our noses in it. Among other things too many to count."
"I want my mama," Merry Gay said in Priss's voice, though propped up and ready to go out searching for Bernice again.
If Hoss Rose had been alive, Merry Gay knew he would have brought order. And it was not only the thinking of a spoiled daughter. The entire community used to listen when he spoke: they trusted him because he had proven they could trust him. During court, when he served on the jury, along with Nate Shirley-usually the two most powerful men in the community were called to jury duty-it had been Hoss Rose who swayed the remaining ten jurors with his common sense. Nate Shirley was as loud and better educated but his loudness came off as preachment, or worse, the speech of a tree-stump politician. He didn't understand them, so he couldn't speak for them. Hoss Rose could. 
For instance, on a case involving two little-time cattlemen claiming the same cow, Nate Shirley had reasoned that the cow in question had in fact been grazing on the range of woods claimed by the first farmer; but as Hoss pointed out to the jury the second farmer could call the cow by name and it would hitch its ears and look up.  Cows are dumb by nature, Nate Shirley said, any cow might do that, looking for an ear of corn or looking just to be looking (what he really said was, Bovine nature is to respond to stimuli, be it racket or rations). Yeah, said Hoss Rose, but who save for the farmer who had pulled the calf at birth would know about a dot of white on its underbelly?


CHAPTER 6

Easing out the parlor door to the hall, they stopped, holding their breath. Seeing first the long spread-legged shadow of a man laid flat as a paper doll on the hall floor, they looked out at the front porch and saw him in silhouette, small by comparison. Standing there, considering them. A pistol in one hand hadn't shown in the shadow on the floor because the man was holding it out before him, aiming it at them.
"Wherebouts is Buck and Charlie at?" he asked.
Merry Gay felt rooted to the floor. She didn't recognize the voice. She didn't recognize the shape of the man with the firelight across the tracks at his back.
"We don't know," Tommy said. "We don't know where anybody is." 
"Y'all just here all by your lonesome, huh?" He sounded friendlier suddenly, or devilish. Up to no good.
"No. Our mama'll be here any minute now." Merry Gay tried to calm her heart, to steady her voice. It was no use. 
He stepped closer, his shadow moved up the hall. Merry Gay holding to Tommy's waist stepped left as she stepped. She stopped as his shadow stopped.
"So, you're that gal they say sings so pretty, huh?"
Even if Merry Gay could have she wouldn't have answered.
"Kind of shy, ain't y'all?" He laughed. "Me new around here and all, looks like y'all'd be more neighborly."
So far he had revealed that he knew Buck and Charlie were not behind the shot-up lavatory door on the train. Did he know that Priss was behind the door? Was that why he hadn't asked where the bossman's missing granddaughter was at? 
Merry Gay shoved at Tommy's back. She inched forward. He followed. The shadow showed an arm on the right, a hand and the pistol motioning right. 
"You girls," he said in a serious curdled voice, "let's just step on in that room over there. Ain't nobody gone get hurt if y'all do like I say do. Hear?"
Through the hall, out back, Merry Gay could see firelight, opening, space to run if she could trust Tommy to run too. But Tommy felt stuck, frozen in inching motion. Would Bernice be able to save them as she had from the rolling-store man at the creek so long ago? No. If she showed up now with the firelight at her back at the end of the hall he would probably shoot her. So much shooting going on at the Crossing, one more shot would go unnoticed. Besides, who was there to save them? The wind was howling around the house and gusting along the hall, almost blowing them forward.
"You got trouble with my brothers, go after them," Merry Gay said. "But leave us alone."
"Well, y'all just a sight prettier than them ole boys and ain't no need in us not getting to know one another while I'm here. Tomorrow, ain't nobody gone know the difference." He laughed again, then quit, pistol shadow waving on the floor. "Now, y'all better just get on in there and get it over with."
"Run, Tommy!" Merry Gay shoved her ahead and Tommy's feet seemed already in running motion, Merry Gay close behind her. End of the hall, hopping off the porch and running again, into the blowing shadows and against the wind, as their feet hit the ground.
Side by side, they reached the back gate at the same time. Beyond the gate, Jean's boarded up house came alive with her kicking and stomping. Shouting, "I know what you're up to, don't think I don't." A muffled pistol shot as if from inside. "I'll kill you sure, you mess with me."
Merry Gay looked back to see the man from the hall stumbling along the edge of the porch, gaining his footing, then running from the shadows at the southeast corner of the porch and into the fire-bright yard, north side of the house. At the northwest corner of the fence he leaped up on the brace, looked around, and hopped over, running through the blowing shadows of the oak and up the rail bed to the tracks, heading south.
"He's gone." Merry Gay had hold of the sharp gate pickets, bracing herself.
"Who was he?" Tommy had picked up a loose fence rail she'd found on the ground. Her breath was coming forced and hard.
"Said he was new around here. Had to be one of Shirley's men." 
"Probably some of that convict labor he's always bringing in on us."
The old settlers of Moniac had been enraged when they learned that Shirley was bringing in convict labor, leased from the state, who he could work cheap in the pinewoods. For a while every cow wrangled or random shot fired was blamed on the convicts. But Nate Shirley never quit bringing them in, and soon the old settlers wearied of trying to sort who was good from who was bad among all the newcomers. Shirley and his sons were proven criminals and problem enough. 
Next door Jean was still raging. Banging two pot lids together, sounded like. The baby screaming and her mocking him. 
"Sounded like she fired off a pistol in there," Tommy said.
"It could have been Roberta at her house."
"I think it was Jean's hollering scared him off. He's likely heard about her if he's heard about you."
"No," said Merry Gay, staring beyond the shadow-streaked back yard to the south side of the house and a gigantic stooped man accented by the firelight from across the tracks. 
"Booger Boy." Tommy stood the point of the fence rail into the dirt at her feet and leaned on it, watching him step heavy side to side then vanish into the blocky shadow of the next house.
"Booger Boy scared him off." Merry Gay kept watching for him to show again in another streak of firelight, maybe heading for the Elton house at the south end of the row. 
Tommy must have been thinking the same. "Was he following us?" she whispered. "Trying to save us like we once saved him? Roberta was right-it was Booger Boy behind us when we went up to the Shirley house."
"Could be," Merry Gay sighed. "Could be he's just taking advantage of this craziness to get out for a change."
They were still standing there at the gate, stuck as they'd been in the hall. "What now?"
Jean answered the question. "Hey, let me out!"
"I guess we should let her out."
"Not yet." Merry Gay started for the back porch of the Rose house. "Bring that fence rail with you," she said. "If he comes back we'll at least give him a headache."
"Booger Boy?"
"No. Not Booger Boy." 
"What's his real name anyway?" 
"Samson."
"How did you know that?" 
"I just named him."
From the back porch, they could see another house flaring up with fresh whitish fire on the next row, only one house up from Roberta's, it appeared, just over her back fence.
Jean who had calmed down for a spell began pounding on walls, doors and windows. Kicking even. The baby was crying, a sound as reassuring as the disturbing sound of Jean's attack on the walls. So far he was still alive. Soon they would have to let her go. They would have to put up with and protect her. One more reason for finding good ole dependable Bernice, who was the inadvertent expert on handling crazy people, drunks, and daughters with coward hearts.
Gunshots went off with the sparks and twirling flames, or maybe some ammunition had exploded inside another burning house, apparently caught from the one next door to it. The crowd gathered at the house was shouting for help; Deputy Threet was shouting orders for everybody to get back and stay back before somebody got bad burned. The wind was whisking through the pine needles and howling around the resistance of tree trunks, children were crying, men cursing, dogs barking.
"Not another fire," Merry Gay said with an intake of breath. "At this rate all of Moniac will be burned up by morning."
"Or blown away, if this wind gets any stronger. Maybe Mama's over there, helping fight fire."
"Where we oughta be, right?" Merry Gay said.
They were sitting along the back-porch wall by the water-shelf, fence rail leaned on the bench between them.
Tommy didn't answer, seemed to be considering the right answer. She got up and went down the hall, toward the front, and when she came back she was carrying the shoes belonging to Miss Moxie. 
"What are you doing with those shoes?" 
"I don't know yet." She sat on the top porch step, placing the shoes to one side, and watched the fire bloom with the lightning in the murky sky. "What'll it take to stop them?" she mused.
Standing behind her, Merry Gay watched. "Tommy, look!" She pointed southeast at a large wheel of light framed with four burning posts and a pitched roof.
"The old hooper shed," said Tommy.
Nothing more said as they watched the fire-lit wheel and frame of the shed crumble to a heap of flames, a terrible crash and rumble and then a neat trail of fire spreading left and igniting the foundation and walls of the one-room schoolhouse.
Tommy sucked in her breath. "I wonder if Booger Boy?I mean, Samson's?still out there, watching over us."
"I hate to say it, but he still scares me. The way he looks is part of it and I won't lie."
"In a bad fix, I bet we'd both be glad to see that elephant face."
Merry Gay walked over to the water-shelf on her left, lit by the house fires on the west side of the tracks. Two shacks burning side by side but like one big house. She lifted the metal dipper from the bucket of light-twinkling water and drank. Her throat was so raw she could hardly swallow, but the water tasted of nourishing minerals, was soft and cool when it did go down. She was hungry suddenly; she was either feeling that after-morning calm or growing accustomed to the mayhem she felt born to.
"I don't get this, do you?" Tommy looked behind her, up the hall, as if she expected the strange man, should he return, to be standing in the same place as before. 
Merry Gay had been doing the same thing. Now, she searched the shadows of the yard for his long shadow-the only image she had of him. She took the fence rail to Tommy. "Here, if he comes up the doorsteps, you lam him over the head." 
But he didn't come and neither did Bernice. And if Booger Boy, renamed Samson, was out there, they didn't see him.
Merry Gay longed to tell Tommy about singing on the boat, to tell about the wasted symbolism of the song she sang, about her revenge by depriving everybody of a second song. But it all seemed petty now. 
Dipping water from the bucket to the wash pan, washing her hands, she sketched in for Tommy only the most important details of the day, what had happened between the men at the depot and on the train as she understood it, how all of this trouble had come about. 
In daylight the colors of the prince's feathers growing from the sour mud of the water-shelf runoff would be crimson, but in the firelight the burry flower heads were burnished as pumpkins. The large iron dinner-bell stood dumb on its tall pole, center of the mud-hole flower bed, its thin hemp rope trailing from the hinged bail to a nail driven into the sturdy pine plank supporting the left end of the water-shelf.  The wind whipped the rope, causing it to flap against the inset wall at the corner of the porch. Merry Gay recognized it as one of the minor noises she had been hearing and was glad she could identify at least one of the sounds in the mishmash of racket. What if she should ring the bell? Would Bernice hear it? Would she come running?
Smelling the soap sweetening the bitter smoke, Merry Gay said, "You up to walking aways?"
"Long as I don't have to wear these old shoes." Tommy laughed low and looked down at the shoes next to her. "Where we going?" 
"I think I know where we might find Mama. Maybe even Will."
"Where?"
They had closed the kitchen door to the hall and a sudden gust blew it open and slapping against the wall behind. A calendar scraped the wall above the stove. Merry Gay went down the hall and forced the door closed against the wind.
"Down the tracks where they've camped with the dead and wounded," Merry Gay said as she stepped onto the porch again. She could imagine it now, all very clearly. If her mother wanted to find her she would check first at home, then among the wounded and dead. And Priss? Surely her family had checked there first, and not finding her there, or Merry Gay, they would assume the two were terrified and hiding somewhere till it was safe to come out, as Bernice had said. She thought about Nate Shirley talking about her grit and common sense rubbing off on Priss. Well, look where it had got her. 
Later, Merry Gay would hash over her guilt, but for now she had things to do. Tommy would need her if Will was dead; if he was wounded, he would need them both. A Negro man wouldn't be at the top of the list when the doctor got there.
In the stretch of woods, east side of the track, between the last house and the firelight of the makeshift morgue and hospital a mile or so south, Merry Gay and Tommy had to tread mud in a stand of cypress, afraid to step out to the railway but not sure why exactly. Only that behind and ahead were angry shouts and the crackle of house-fires and gunfire. Dozens of dogs were yipping and barking, ganging with the crowd their masters were ganging with, or maybe thronging in the thick of the excitement for an all-night romp. But in the cypress swamp, the bullfrogs and peepers blocked all other sounds and the smell of swamp mud cleared their heads of smoke.
Still, they held hands, stepping high with their skirt tails hiked. Not once had Tommy mentioned the word snake, and Merry Gay knew why. Like every human threat, danger was relative. And it seemed to Merry Gay in this new life of her longest night that her plight was really simple: stay one step ahead and out of the way. Do what you have to do and let the rest wait.
Mud sucked at their shoes, and their calves cramped from jacking their knees. Several times they stepped down on stobs and cypress knees, and finally had to drop hands and take off their shoes and carry them. Alone, together, in the swampy dark. 
"I hope he's following us," Merry Gay said.
"Me too."
Of course they meant Samson, but the name was too new to say with feeling. Besides, Merry Gay feared that the sight of the stumbling giant would ignite some new terror to make their hearts race faster. After all, they couldn't know for sure whether he meant them harm or was protecting them. Still, the notion that somebody stronger was looking out for them made them feel safer. Less at the mercy of the night and strange men who might turn out to be familiar, men they saw everyday and never knew to fear. Like those strange familiar men gone mad in the smoking car.
Live branches, pine needles and cones rained down in the wind. Slow going with their eyes fixed on the light down the tracks that seemed to split with the trees blocking their vision. The light seemed to be moving away like the voices downwind. 
When at last they reached the site of the wounded, the circle of lanterns and the burning stump revealed only ghosts of men: some sitting, some standing, pacing or lying down. Loud frantic babble and groans. Without stepping out from the cover of woods, the girls were too far away to tell if Bernice or Will was among them. The camp was at least twenty or thirty yards away, but on the rise of the rail bed, the men were in full view but faceless. 
"Isn't that his car?" Tommy whispered, hiding behind a stout pine trunk with Merry Gay hanging to her right shoulder.
"Nate Shirley's? I don't see it." Then squinting through the dark, she thought she did see it, a dark block parked diagonally to the right of the camp, front wheels almost on the tracks, but if she looked at it a certain way it became only an illusion, darker dark. "He must have slipped out the backway with the doctor and driven past while we were in the swamp."
"Don't look like the mobs followed him, does it?" asked Tommy.
"Not that I can tell. Maybe they followed Deputy Threet to the house that just caught fire, expecting Shirley would still be at his house when they got back." Merry Gay leaned into the tree trunk and tugged on her shoes, leaving them unlaced, her toes squishing mud. "Can you make out Mama or Will from here?" 
"No?I don't know."
The wind was whistling in the pine top overhead and rocking the limber groaning trunk.
"We've got to get out from under these trees. This wind's apt to blow a limb down on us. Let's go."
"I can't go out there yet, Tommy. I can't tell about Priss yet, and besides I don't know where I fit in all this because of Buck and Charlie." 
"I don't reckon anybody would have it in for me and me working for the Shirleys, do you?
"I wouldn't think so, no."
"I'm the one went after the doc in Fargo. Wadn't for me I doubt he'd be here right now, don't you?"
 Really Merry Gay thought it possible that Tommy might be considered the enemy too because of Will and also her association with the Roses, her family. "I don't want you to go. I don't see Will or Mama anywhere anyway."
"You don't think??"
"I can't think, Tommy." Merry Gay stepped away from the tree toward the total dark of the swamp; her mama could have been there and left or she could have been shot down on the spot. Will was likely dead there before them. She couldn't let Tommy find him like that, she couldn't risk what her sister might say or do. 
"Wait!" Tommy hissed. "Do you hear something coming down the tracks?"
Merry Gay stepped over to the tree where Tommy was standing again, tuning her ears in that direction and picking up a clacking, ratcheting sound. A pumping sound, like a blacksmith firing the coals with his bellow.
They stepped out from the tree line as far as they dared, seeing up the track what looked like two people floating toward the circle of lanterns, one thick the other thin, backlit by the fires of the burning houses at the Crossing. Both bending at the waist and straightening up, moving fast up the tracks like spirits. 
"It's a railroad handcart, I think," Merry Gay whispered and stepped a few more feet toward the rail bed.
"Somebody coming to help, thank God." Tommy followed her, hanging close and smiling.
Several men at the camp, having heard the cart too, and possibly seen it, moved out toward the tracks with their railroad lanterns, calling out to the men on the cart.
"Oh no," Merry Gay said, holding out her arms to stop Tommy, "It's Charlie and Buck!"
"I thought you said they were gone."
"They must have hiked up to the switching tracks and got the cart to come back and look for Mike Ruis or?I don't know."
The men appeared to have recognized the Rose boys too. Shouting at each other they ran back toward the camp and the wounded men, lanterns swinging, and began fumbling weapons from the ground and running again toward the tracks. Buck and Charlie on the handcart picked up speed, passing right by them.
Shotguns and pistols popped and boomed. Merry Gay and Tommy ran toward the woods, holding their ears. Suddenly tired, so tired her eyes showed red sparkles in the dark, Merry Gay sat in the briars and weeds and cried. Sobbing loud, because what did it matter? Tommy knelt beside her. "Get up," she said. "Let's go. It'll be morning soon."
"No, don't you believe that. Don't say that." Merry Gay had been waiting so long for morning to come, and with it, peace, that she no longer believed in morning and didn't know what she did believe in but whatever, it verged on blasphemy. She could no longer pray.
The men who had shot at Buck and Charlie on the handcart were chasing south along the tracks, war whoops claiming the sounds of the wind and the ratcheting of the handcart.  
Tommy braced Merry Gay with one arm and helped her to stand, walking her out to the clearing just off the railroad right of way and toward the mean light of the blazing houses at Moniac Crossing. 
"I'm not going back to the house yet," Merry Gay said.
They were approaching the Elton house on the south end of the Crossing's tracks. A dark house, not a sound. Even the dog was gone.
"Don't look now," Tommy whispered, looking back, "but I think I saw Samson. I think he's for sure following us." Her arm was still around Merry Gay's waist as if to help her walk and her full coarse hair was blowing in Merry Gay's face.
"Thank God."
"Thank Samson."
"Don't. You'll scare him off." Merry Gay suddenly switched back to the problem at hand. "Buck and Charlie are probably dead. We've got to find Mama now."
Creeping yard to yard, along the back fence rows of wind-wrung pear, fig, and pecan trees for cover, Merry Gay and Tommy kept walking when they got to the Rose's back yard. Glancing back now, they could see no trace of the big man, their protector, who looked like an elephant. Obviously, this time he had stayed at his house. They could hear out front along the lane by the tracks men on horses and men on foot, loud talk overlapping. One of them coughed, somebody said, "Listen!" Gunfire sounded to the left, across the tracks. 
"At the commissary?" Tommy whispered.
"Sounds like it, I can't tell."
The men crossed the tracks, in the light of the new fire on that side, heading toward the commissary. One's hat blew off and skirled along the tracks like a dark bird.
Merry Gay and Tommy were almost level with the backyard of Jean's house. The baby was crying fitfully, louder than before, more open, closer. The porch looked dark in the chutes of firelight blazing each side of the yard from across the tracks.  
"Does it look to you like somebody's ripped the boards loose from the backdoor?" Tommy said.
"Maybe. Come on."
Jean's porch was always cluttered with old furniture, mattresses and clothes. Underneath the kitchen window was a wood kindling box and that was where the crying seemed to be coming from, which at first didn't make sense. Then their eyes adjusted to a faint glow from the open kitchen door and a lit lamp or candle somewhere inside growing in the darkness as they stepped from the light of the fire across the tracks. The glow had been there all along but unnoticed before because of the brighter firelight.  
Merry Gay and Tommy stopped at the porch steps, each calling low for Jean. Eyes on the dumpy flailing baby dressed only in a sheet diaper, lying inside the wood box under the kitchen window.
"Jean?" Merry Gay called, going up on the porch. 
The wind had picked up, gusting, and an aluminum bucket or pot rolled under the porch, bail rattling. Pecans pelted the tin roof of a shed beyond the back fence. 
 Tommy, following close, whispered, "She's not here. She's left the baby," then "Jean?" she called to be sure.  No answer, only the wind knocking the open door against the wall of the kitchen. Heat from inside oozing out around the in-rush of wind, so hot it seemed that surely the meager lamp or candle glow might erupt into house-fire.
 "Where would she be?" Tommy asked.
"Crazy as she is, no telling." Merry Gay had heard Jean refer to Samson as a freak; she wondered if he had heard her say that and had taken revenge on her in some way. Was he capable of such thinking, she didn't know?
 She knelt and picked up the baby. He was wet and his skin was hot, flesh knotted up from the strain of crying. Somebody had made him a bed of bunched flannel sheeting, like a bird might build a nest, atop the firewood. She placed the baby on her shoulder shushing him, patting his marble-smooth back. He was heavy, solid weight. Husky and not sweet like some babies. He smelled of burped milk and pee. The wind took his breath and he stopped crying and shuddered, then began crying again. Holding the baby made her feel hopeful and renewed, at peace after witnessing her brothers being shot at and likely killed. He was a miracle, proof of survival. Everything would be all right; the night would pass with the storm. Tomorrow would come after all.
 "Moses in the bullrushes," Merry Gay said. "Remember that story from Sunday School, Tommy?"
"Let's take him to Roberta," said Tommy, peering out in the dark, "see if she's seen Jean or Mama."
Shushing the baby, they walked fast, headed for the sagging gate leading to Roberta's yard. The house fire across the hedge of bushes and trees was burning low now but whipping and snapping in the wind. Roberta was sleeping, slumped over, her back to the wall, the two boys snuggled close, wrapped in quilts. The wind tore at the quilts and the boys' fine brown hair. Roberta's baby was cradled in her arms, a neat bundle swaddled in a pieced quilt.
Hearing Jean's baby crying, Roberta sat up straight.
"It's us Roberta," Merry Gay whispered. "Have you seen Jean?"
She shook her head and pulled her own baby close. Then laid him flat on her lap and reached out. "Hand him here," she whispered. 
"Her baby was on the porch but she was gone."
"He's wet and hungry," Roberta said. 
Suddenly the girls realized that Roberta was sobbing, sobbing low, not really a woman to cry and she sounded like she was choking. She held Jean's baby in her arms a minute, looking down, "Lil' ole fellow's fevered as me," she said. Then holding her left nipple in her right hand she guided it into his mouth, stifling his crying. "When will it all stop?" she whispered. "What will it take to make it stop?"
"Has Mama been by here yet?" Merry Gay whispered, easy and at peace with the absence of the crying. All was relative.
"I don't know if I dreamed I seen her or I seen her," Roberta said in a squeezed voice. "Either way, she was walking by, gimping like she'll do, and says for me to come help her get Jean out before she hurts herself and that baby. I says, 'I'm sick as a dog.' It just come over me-that old low blood I reckon-and here I got all these babies? I'm trying to get up and next thing, I hear boards ripping off Jean's door, seems like, and Bernice hollers out, 'It's me, Jean, don't shoot.' She shoots anyhow and then lets out a holler and shoots again, and ain't nare nother sound but that baby crying. I must of dreamed it because it was light outside then."
"Tommy?" Merry Gay whirled. But Tommy was gone, passing through the south chute of firelight in Roberta's yard, through the gate and running across Jean's yard, almost to her porch. "We'll be back, Roberta, I promise." 
Merry Gay stepped down off the porch, walking, swinging her arms, hearing the squawking of the swinging gate's hinges between Roberta's and Jean's yards. She tried to make out Tommy on the blind side of the light, on Jean's porch. The tiny glow from inside the house transferring to her white blouse. 
Gunshots from south along the tracks, the quarters burning good now, wind puffing smoke like bellows in a beehive, but none of it pertained to Merry Gay, none of it mattered, not even the two odd drops of rain that drizzled down her cheeks before the tears started. 
She could hear Tommy's skirt swishing and her shoes skimming the grass belatedly. She thought she could, but feared she had grown so used to the racket of gunfire and shouts she noticed only hints of sound, sound logged in as memory. Like Roberta's low ashamed sobbing, her soul sensing that her dreaming wasn't dreaming after all.
The firelight from a corn crib burning next row over, across the back fence, set off Jean's porch like daylight, sundown light. Merry Gay could see Tommy to the left of the open door, standing over what they'd earlier thought was a heap of old clothes. One pale clothed hump and one dark. "It's Mama, Merry Gay," she said. "Mama and Jean."
Merry Gay ran, thinking how loud Tommy had said it. How probably that meant that both women were dead. Then Tommy wouldn't care who heard.
"They're dead," Tommy said. "Both of them."
"Dead." Merry Gay stopped, clasping both hands over her racing heart. "Tommy, no, not Mama too."
Tommy was bending with her hands on her knees, head down and sobbing, coughing. "All that time?" She didn't finish what she was going to say, only kept sobbing.
On the porch, next to Tommy, Merry Gay leaned over her mother, seeing a gunshot wound like a small carefully bored hole, a punch-out in her weather-bronzed, wrinkled forehead. Jean's mouth was blown away, blood seeping and clotting like the oversized painted lips of a harlot, pistol in her right hand. 
Suddenly Tommy quit crying and walked over to the edge of the porch, looking out at the burning crib. "She's my mama, my real mama," she said. "I can't believe Anse leaving Jean a pistol."
Merry Gay was the one crying now, low and almost crooning, in her singing voice. Tommy walked over, standing beside her; they hugged, shaking, crying together, a melody that grew to howling like something wild, like the wind. Each blotting tears on the other's face. Then they broke free, standing apart and wiping their eyes and noses on their muddy dress tails. 
Merry Gay kneeled before her mother and held her rough right hand, the hand that had changed her clothes and brushed her hair, patted her on the back when she was good and paddled her bottom when she was bad. "I doubt Anse left her the pistol; she probably found where he'd hid it." The hand was filthy, stuffed-feeling. She kissed it anyway. 

***

Tommy was no longer crying, no longer hiding, but marching out toward the fire-lit lane along the railroad tracks, marching north with her head high and Miss Moxie's shoes and Jean's pistol down by her sides and swinging.
"Tommy, no," Merry Gay called, following, finding only a whisper of a voice even trying to shout. But she was thinking loud. "Wait!" she called out, running to catch up. "What are you doing?"
"I'm taking Miss Moxie her shoes."
Collected rags, moss and papers and even some woman's sunbonnet had blown and banked along the railroad embankment. The thick branches of the old live oaks on their right were giving up to the wind green leaves and brittle twigs and now and then whole limbs that had looked solid enough before; limbs that looked dead were holding. That's how bright it was from the fire across the track, and but for the east wind Merry Gay was sure she could feel the heat. 
"I don't think we ought to," she called after Tommy. "I don't think?"
"Go back if you want to, scardy-cat," she yelled without turning, marching on. "I'm not scared anymore."
Merry Gay could see that the fire across the tracks had spread all the way to the northernmost shanty on the edge of the woods and was eating its way through a chicken coop and shed behind the house, to Tommy's house. She knew Tommy had seen it too, so she said nothing. The mobs in front of the Shirley house seemed to have gathered for the newest fire, shouting, running, shooting. 
A man shouted, "One of them Shirley boys, get him!" 
Shots rang out, the fire snapped and lashed and sang with the wind in the pines of the bordering woods.
"Will," Merry Gay said, "maybe Will?"
"Will's gone," Tommy said, marching on up the lane.
At the south end of the Shirley's picket fence, Tommy kicked open the side gate and marched through and on toward the rear of the house, Merry Gay close behind her. When they reached the kitchen with the light burning behind the white curtains, Tommy backed off and tossed one shoe and then the other to the low sloped cypress-shingle roof, two loud sliding thuds but they stuck there. Then she fired the pistol up in the air. "Hey, Miss Moxie," she shouted, "come get your shoes. They're broke in good now."
Again, she fired the pistol and the backdoor opened a peep. Miss Moxie in her long flannel gown was standing, holding a lantern before her.
Her lips parted to a mixture of teeth, long, yellow and overlapping as if rattling around loose in her mouth. "What y'all mean by that?"
"Mama's dead," Merry Gay said. "I brought your shoes back." Her voice worked this time; better she be the culprit than Tommy.
Miss Moxie covered her mouth with one hand and a different hand on her shoulder pulled her inside. Then Priss's mother Sara stepped out into the doorway. She was wearing a dark fringed shawl about her shoulders, her right ringed hand holding it closed at the throat and the wind played the fringe like piano keys. 
"Where's Priss, Merry Gay?" she said calmly. 
"I don't know, Miss Sara."
"When did you last see her?"
"At the lavatory on the train. We got separated."
Her calm was cracking; her smooth tilted face was cracking. "How?"
"I don't know."
"You're lying." Suddenly Wanda was by Sara's side. She was a small woman with red hair and milk-glass skin, Nate's second wife, almost as young as Muffin. The skirt of her white cut-lace gown ballooned in the wind.
"Y'all go on now," Wanda said. "We don't want anymore trouble with you people."
"We don't want anymore trouble with you people either," Tommy piped up and stepped forward.
"Tommy, wait! Let's don't," Merry Gay said. "I need to tell them about Priss now."
Across the tracks, the fire sounded like a storm; the wind sounded like fire.
The door opened wider and all the Shirley women except Muffin were standing, scared as any other women that night, just as vulnerable and weary and common as poor Roberta even.
"Priss is dead."
Her mother gasped, then sank away from the door.
"She got shot on the way to the lavatory. I had to hide or be shot my ownself. Her body's still on the train, I think."
One by one the women moved away from the door. It closed easy as the door of a tomb and they began wailing and sobbing.
"Why didn't you tell them who shot her?" Tommy said.
"Not yet, I can't yet. I've got my reasons so don't ask again."
"Let's go," Tommy said.
"Where? Why?"
"I'm going to watch my house burn, for one thing. Then I don't know. I don't know what it'll take to stop them either, but we're not hiding anymore. Mama didn't raise us to be cowards."



CHAPTER 7

The raised railroad tracks seemed to Merry Gay like a stone wall they would have to climb over, at that point. They simply stood there looking.  Looking to the dark woods up the tracks on the right, and ahead, above the tops of the Negro row shanties, then left to the circle of lanterns at the camp down the tracks for the wounded and dead. Looking, for one thing, because they'd been taught that the sound and speed of the trains could be deceiving while standing directly on the tracks.
 Once the tiny girls had been playing there, making mountains of the sharp warm rocks, and had looked up to see a black locomotive moving almost silently through the heat-warp, shimmering, coming on, glittery-black iron, but with the grace and stealth of a snake gliding along barn rafters. 
When Tommy married Will Bernice told Merry Gay, "Now she's free to live with her own people across the tracks." She said it as if they had raised a bird that seemed happy enough in its cage but would be happier freed. The tracks had never seemed like a dividing line before though to either of the girls. The trains were part of their life, belching smoke and ruining window curtains and the clothes fresh washed and hung on the line to dry. Their powerful rude rumbling seldom even woke them at night. And the tracks were where they spread their tea sets and dressed their baby dolls, having to move their playhouse four times a day or have them ground into hints by the polished wheels of the trains. 
Still, it was a fact that the Negroes and whites worked together but didn't go to church or school together. None except for Tommy, till she was almost ten and got kicked out of school. She had grown up white, then changed-up to Negro. She was two people with two lives; she had a before and after that had seemed to make her timid but instead had made her tough. Still, to Merry Gay, Tommy was like cold ash maintaining its unburned form until touched.
Standing close on the tracks that night, in the rain, they watched Tommy's house burn to the ground. They watched the walls melt away and the fire shine on the dresser mirror and eat up the bed Bernice had given to Tommy and dynamite the tomatoes and beans and blackberries she had put up in jars that summer. They exploded like fireworks-phaft phaft phaft! Even the small barrel-shaped heater looked hot on its platform of flat-laid bricks.
 The black ruffled-leather padded ladies' platform rocker that their daddy had bought for Tommy when she turned twelve was revealed in the front room, Tommy's parlor, as the walls came down like gold-flame curtains. The chair looked smaller than Merry Gay remembered, like Tommy herself, somewhat overdone-too dainty, too prissy for the color black. Even Bernice had suggested reupholstering the petal-shaped patch of leather on the inside back with calico or some other cotton print. Oak wings each side of the padded leather petal made it look like some scary insect; had made Tommy when she sat in it look ready for flight. Now the three engraved fingers at the tip of the short narrow chair arms were nibbled by fire, like the charred claws of a coon trapped in wildfire. 
The soft spots of gray ash where the other houses had burned looked purged of their owners' belongings, their ratty bedding and clothes. Tommy and Will's house looked new and bright on-fire.
In the gutted shadows and light-spokes of fire, from their bold lookout, they found not a single Negro across the tracks. It was as if they'd long ago moved away. The wind had sulled or taken to the woods with the Negroes-that's how it felt. The white mobs they had seen earlier at the Shirley house had moved on toward the commissary with their lightered-knot torches and guns and protesting horses, taking with them their racket and danger.
"Do you really think the Negroes are all in the woods?" Merry Gay shivered, hugging herself.
"Where else would they go?" Tommy's bristly black hair had sprung loose from the clasp in back and fanned wild about her pale face. She was still carrying the pistol, but it hung by her side in the folds of her dark skirt-black, gray, indigo? It simply looked dark, drab with mud and filth. "They know these woods like the back of their hand," she said and her eyes shone like river stones. 
True, they turpentined those woods every weekday; weekends they fished the streams and slews, hunted squirrel, possum, bird and bear. Deer, quail and doves were white men's fare, white men's sport. But Merry Gay and Tommy knew how spooked the Negroes could be by ghosts and snakes, how miserable the rain. 
"Looks like this rain would drive them out of the woods," Merry Gay said, "don't you think?"
"All depends."
"You mean it depends on how scared they are of what's waiting on them if they come home?"
"Likely they're huddled up under the weather shelters in the gum woods."
"Those old lean-tos," said Merry Gay, "not much cover to them."
A rooster crowed from the east woods. 
"It's almost over," Merry Gay said. "Let's go to Mama's. Let's sleep. Tomorrow we'll be getting everybody ready to bury." If she tried to name them all, those she suspected were dead and those she knew were dead, she couldn't bear it. 
But they'd no sooner stepped from the railroad tracks than they were on the odd-spaced crossties and sharp ballast again, running toward the commissary and the sounds of gunfire and angry shouts, not away from them this time.
Tommy in the lead leaped crosstie to crosstie in flickering shadows of the dying house fires along that section of tracks, the pistol she'd taken from Jean's hand in her own hand and her shouting back at Merry Gay, "They were hiding upstairs in the white hands' quarters. Come on!"
"Who?" Merry Gay was running because Tommy was running, out of breath but intent on catching up, on stopping her. The rain stung her face and she could hear it smacking on the crossties and rock and hissing in the fires on her right.  
"Will and them. They're hiding in the commissary not the woods. Hurry!"
It made sense to Merry Gay, though she didn't really believe Will was among the Negroes who were hiding. She believed he was dead. Looking back on the scene in the smoking car, she could see clearly that he had been in the thick of the pivotal fight, one of the blood-lusting men who'd set off the whole community of Moniac against each other, class against class, race against race, or worse, daytime friends against nighttime enemies.
In the commissary yard, Tommy and Merry Gay mixed among the mob, some on foot, some on horseback, to see Negroes sprawled prone on the stairs and on the ground to the right of the commissary. The smell of gun and wood smoke seemed to lay peace over the weary, wet, swollen crowd, and even the lit torches were still as fires in lamp chimneys.
The American flag on its pole above the railed second-story porch stirred in the rain-driven breeze. Clouds of smoke hid the storm clouds surely rolling in. The leaves of the old white oak were soggy and looked like burlap scraps, its white bark peeling in papery scrolls. 
Several white men with torches were walking among the bodies of the Negroes. Merry Gay counted eight, bloodied and broken, indistinguishable death-faces in the torchlight.
"Y'all go on home to your families now." Nate Shirley on his chestnut gelding appeared out of the rainy dark at the rear of the commissary, coursing through the dead men and the joined mobs. His right arm was hoisted in a white sling and looped around his vein-strutted neck, but still the reins lay easy across three upturned fingers. 
A few men shouted at him. One said in a trembling voice, "You the cause of all this. You!"
With his left hand Shirley reached inside his brown field jacket pocket and pulled out a pistol and fired it in the air. "These boys here were holed up in there ready to waylay us, who knows what-all. They figured to hide up there with only one door to the outside and a lookout over the top porch. What we got's a real rebellion here-us against the coloreds. Now you can side with me or you can side with them; ain't no other side to it."
"That's not what all this started over," a stocky older man yelled. "These colored boys ain't done nothing to you."
"Yeah!" another shouted. "We got a bone to pick, it's with you." Several men roared, shouting at once.
"Listen," Shirley said, holding up his gun and left hand with his right, "listen!" They seemed willing to but not eager. Pacing, muttering. Recognizing his cracker-talk for what it was-mockery. "Any man here's got a problem with his dealings with me, let him step up now. You take a notion to buy your land back, come up with the cash money."
They got loud again, drowning him out, then low again. One shouted, "What cash!" Another yelled out, "You made me a loan on my place then collected on it the first time I run late paying." Somebody else suggested they tar and feather the whole bunch of them, Shirley and his boys.
Shirley fired his gun in the air again. "If you're not willing to reason with me, then get on out from here. You're outnumbered, in case you hadn't noticed." He seemed to catch on to his own contradiction; he seemed to detect the falsetto of his own voice. "Get on out from here, the whole lot of you. Leave my boys to their work, clearing out the bodies, so they can go home too."  Dismissing the old Moniac clan he turned his horse prancing through the other men and their shadows.
About a dozen men headed for the tracks, leaving twice that many in the commissary yard-two mobs again. Not all of them, either side, because down the tracks the others were still wandering with their lanterns, and along one of the lanes east of the tracks Deputy Threet's flat voice hallowed and Wendell Shirley answered, then gunfire. Anse was one of the men dragging bodies from the dark stairs, hard thumps of heads hitting the stairs.
"He's not here, Tommy, Will's not. Let's go." Merry Gay took hold of her right elbow to guide her toward the tracks. 
Tommy's right hand flew up with the pistol as she yanked away. Her face was luminous as tin. "These are my people, mine!" 
Merry Gay had never known anything to separate them so: my people. 
"You go on," Tommy said, stepping forward on the tramped gray mud of the commissary yard. Cow tracks were filled with water, the cows themselves still in the woods, but their warm-ripe odor remained, sharpened by the wet. Clear dimpling puddles spread like maps on the packed sand yard. 
Merry Gay started after her. Suddenly she realized that to her knowledge Tommy, part-white or not, was the only declared Negro either alive or left in Moniac. She was the one who might further raise the ire of Shirley and his men.
Shirley, sitting astride the horse and gazing down at his arm in the white sling, gritted his teeth and shot his gaze through slashes of rain in Tommy's direction. "Why aren't you hiding, nigger gal? I would if I was you after pestering my family and shooting a pistol on my premises." 
Tommy met his eyes, then looked past him at the Negro men still being dragged from the shadows of the stairs to the light of the torches and lanterns near the commissary front. Merry Gay could tell he took it as a slight, that Tommy hadn't even flinched. 
	"She wasn't trying to shoot anybody," Merry Gay spoke up.
He switched the head of his horse around, facing them both, as if to run them over for Tommy's ignoring him-among other things. The gelding champed at his bits and slung his head. "That Priss's blood on your dress, cracker gal?"
Merry Gay looked down at her strange yellow dress, bloodstains almost purple and hem trailing mud. It seemed so long since she'd put it on; it seemed so long since putting it on had been important.
"Yes." She was all set to tell him that he had shot Priss, his favorite grandchild. But something stopped her. Not pity, not compassion, but power in knowing, in not telling-yet. The sure knowledge that at some point she would use what she alone-no, she and Tommy-might need at some point for leverage. To break this puffed-up man, who she longed to break this very minute. 
"My mama's dead." She couldn't bring herself to say Jean was dead, not with Anse within earshot. Out of pity, she wouldn't tell him. "I don't know who-all else is dead."
No change of expression on Shirley's face, only pain-personal, physical, selfish pain from his hurting arm. "I hate that," he said. "It couldn't be helped."
"It could have been helped."
"Then blame your brothers for starting it."
"You shot them too, didn't you?"
He laughed out and tipped his brown plantation hat, daring the rain collected in the brim to run down his back. "I did my dead-level best," he said. "It's like they say, the poor are hard to get rid of."
"I think that's 'the poor are always with us.'"
He laughed again, wiggling his upturned fingers with the reins at rest there. "What a waste." He clucked his tongue, nodding his head. "What a waste."
Tommy, still staring at the bodies being dragged down the stairs, walked off, wandering among them and peering down.
"If you mean me," said Merry Gay, "I don't consider my life wasted."
"I would have sent you to all the best schools; I would have helped you get out." He fanned his left hand toward the road-out hidden by the commissary and trees: one road, according to Shirley, kept his turpentine labor from leaving while owing the bossman. He was always on the lookout for strange horses and wagons, suspecting another local turpentine man of sneaking in and trying to lure away his labor. On the up and up, the other man could pay off their debts to Shirley and he was welcome to them. 
"You're the one who should get out," said Merry Gay, "you're the outcast here."
"Didn't you learn anything from me?"
"More than you know."
He laughed. "I get your meaning, cracker gal."
"I do thank you for making me read "Heart of Darkness." 
He glared at her, shifting his weight in the saddle, his trim bulk, to indicate she'd struck a nerve and he was about to strike back. "Tell me about Priss, who shot her, where her body was last time you saw her." 
"I don't know." She walked past him to get to Tommy, now standing over a dead body and crying with her right arm over her eyes. Merry Gay could smell the sweat of Shirley's horse and the clean of his bandages; she could hear the creaking of his leather saddle as he rose in the stirrups, shouting, "Take your nigger sister and the both of you get on out from here before I shoot you down like dogs. Like them boys there." He pointed with his pistol at a man being dragged down the stairs by his heels. It was Will in his filthy white shirt and black gallowses, limp and sliding, head bumping and nodding down the stairs. 
Tommy seemed to have been expecting him, expecting that. She walked over to his body and dropped to her knees, releasing the pistol by his right side. Merry Gay knelt beside her, hugging her with her left arm while her right hand scooped up the pistol and hid it in the draping of her bloody yellow skirt.
Anse, stocky and bright-eyed, said, "Awright now," and bent with his guinea legs spread to make room for his little-man's paunch and caught Will's ankles, the uppers of his shined black boots, to drag him away to the edge of the woods and the wagon being loaded with the other dead Negroes.
 Tommy leaped up and sailed on Anse's back, hands on his shoulders and legs locked around, screaming, biting his neck. He reached behind and grabbed her by the hair and slung her to the ground like a sack.
"Shitfire!" He cooped one hand over the bite on his neck like a precious butterfly, then began wobbling madly, mindlessly. "I ain't never!"
Tommy lay there on her side, as she had landed, like the corn stalks in autumn dried into blowing poses, like in the game of Sling the Biscuits she and Merry Gay used to play. Waiting to move, to have to move, or in this case, waiting to be moved. Her wan face in the shadows took on angles and hollows. She didn't blink. 
Two of the newer white hands Shirley had lately hired came up and one kicked at Tommy on the ground, missing as she rolled away facing the dark woods. The other held a pistol on her, aiming for her head now turned to see the bullet that would fire from the barrel next breath, last breath. 
"Let's have some fun with this nigger gal first," said the one with the pistol.
The other laughed, looking up at Shirley for his okay. 
Merry Gay rushed into the midst of them, hearing Shirley's gelding snuffling behind her. She threw herself on top of Tommy, covering her body with her own, while at the same time aiming the pistol in her right hand straight as she could aim it at the man pointing his pistol
"Take me, take me too, I dare you." She switched the gun on Shirley astride the horse. "Kill me and you'll never know the truth about Priss."
"Hey, girl," Anse yelled, stumbling over to where the other men were gathered in the near-dark, "that's Jean's pistol! How'd you get it?"
The rain was falling at a slant in the light of the lanterns set on the ground beyond the plank stairs. Hardened crystals of rain. The wind blew and it came harder from the woods and the dripping leaves and vines sounded like streams rushing.
"She shot my mama in the head, mistaking her for one of you. You! Then she shot her ownself." Merry Gay was screaming her news, misplaced revenge she wished on Shirley. Anse would do. "You gave a crazy woman a pistol and boarded her up in the house!"
"What the hell!" Anse said and backed away.
Then Merry Gay saw Samson, addled-looking and stiff with his arms bowed by his side, appearing like a wet sea monster in a dream between the men and the girls. He began grunting, trying to speak, in a language only he understood, though his menacing tone was unmistakable, while slowly lifting both arms as if to block the men from harming the girls.
Somebody breached a rifle.
"Don't shoot." Merry Gay knelt, then sat back on her heels and lowered the pistol in her hand. "Don't shoot him."
The men began mumbling among themselves, some gaping at the ugly aggrieved mammoth while others looked away.
"It's that old Elton boy's what it is."
"Somebody oughta take care of that crazy," one said.
"Let em go," Nate Shirley said from high up on his horse. "For now," he added. "And you boy"-he swung round, facing Samson with his arms still raised like a tree or a wall, as if he'd practiced before a mirror and filling the mirror decided to convert his scary image to power and try it out on the world-"you git on home now, you hear?"
That said Shirley rode off toward the tracks, appearing to vanish into the dawning sky.

***

The great Hoss Rose was great in the eyes of his daughters and all who knew him, except maybe Bernice, and there he'd lost standing only by demerit of minor flaws, petty irritations; for example, while reading The Folkston Recorder he might come across a familiar old name in the obituaries and, knowing it wasn't the same person they both knew, he'd say to Bernice something like, 'Well, looks like old Walter Lee finally gave up the ghost,' as if the name Walter Lee had been assigned to one man forever. God, that was corny! He would tell the same stories over and over to her and the children, little masters at daydreaming to the drone of their daddy's strong but diminished voice. But Bernice would give him his due for having been greater overall than the petty irritations caused him to seem in late-life, after he'd lost all and had retired to the house for her to wait on. She'd had to hang on to the big deeds and there were many.
 As a young man serving in the Georgia State Militia, during the War Between the States, he had slipped off from his regiment stationed in Savannah with two of his buddies, one a fool for trouble, and the other a coward. Both were out for themselves and no one else, to sum it up. Their destination had been home, but when they reached Fernandina, Florida, they were close enough to home to feel brave, suddenly intuited with the need for daring after so long holed up waiting for the delayed enemy battalions marching eastward. The South Georgia farm boys had been attracted to the war only for purposes of secession and even that had lost its luster after a while. They longed for action, and if not action, home, home and peace; the soughing winter ocean promised both.
 It was early March, early morning, and the surf along the seashore of Fernandina Beach was higher than their heads, the murky in-slush sweeping in magnificent curls and dragging the sand for yesterday's deposits of clam, conch shells, jelly fish and crabs. The surf was as powerful as the wind swooping over the Appalachian mountain chain, curling under and over to form tornadoes. They had been there too. The slight man with something to prove to his buddies told them to dare him to swim out-he wasn't afraid. The other man reminded him that they were not where they were supposed to be and he could get them all in trouble if he swam out. Kingly bald, even then, Hoss Rose pointed out that the undertow would likely grab him and carry him out to sea.
 They were standing on the ragged plinth of granite placed along the tide line to prevent washing of the coarse sand and sea oats. A scattering of distant ships and shrimp boats bobbed like toys on the ripping tidewater. Nobody out on the beach because the wind was whipping oily gray grit like soot that stuck to the skin; the sky was churning clouds of mud.
 The buddy threatening to swim out suddenly took off his patched boots and worn navy pants and ran toward the wave dragging the sand out to sea and kept running till he was waist deep, then chest deep, hooting, laughing, shouting that the water was like ice, calling the other two men "girls" for not swimming out with him. He was still shouting when he went under, shouting again when he popped up like a buoy. 
"Undertow," Hoss Rose said, stripping off his shoes and pants, keeping his eyes on the buddy about to be towed out to sea, out to where the tiny ships skimmed and ducked stem to stern.
 "Don't," said the other man, standing next to Hoss and watching too. "Let him alone, let's go. We tried to warn him. If you save him he's liable to get us in trouble for leaving the regiment."
 Hoss ran to the edge of the water, high-stepping out, then diving into the next monster wave approaching. Winded and strangling on the freezing water turning with sand like salt, when he got to where the other man was maybe making his last showing, he began treading water, shouting to the man not to fight him. "Don't fight me and I'll save you."
 The man reached for him, gulping water into the red maw of his bruised and furious face, target for Hoss's huge fist. Before the lolling head could sink again, Hoss gripped him by his left arm and slung him backwards onto his back, balancing the man while swimming toward shore.

***

With the dawn and the rain on Monday morning came the sheriff and about a dozen deputized men from the Charlton County seat of Folkston, some twenty-odd miles northeast of Moniac, headed up by Buck and Charlie.
The wind was spreading sheets of rain west and off the Atlantic coast when Buck and Charlie blew into the hall of the house they called home. Stomping mud from their boots, and just stomping to be stomping.
Merry Gay and Tommy were huddled in skimming sleep under a quilt in the rear bedroom where their dead mother had slept only two nights before. Tommy in her filthy white blouse and dark skirt and Merry Gay in her blood-stiffened and reeking yellow dress.
No curtains on the windows, only pull-down parchment shades, two four-paned window-lights set in the south and east bead-board walls. The iron bedstead was rusty and the walnut dresser held the only mirror in the house. The mirror in which all the Rose children had first seen themselves and later on judged whether they were pretty or ugly, and later still, judged whether they were pretty or ugly enough. Then like Bernice, merely passing, glancing at the shells of their feeling bodies, illuminated ghosts of themselves. Not a single one of them was vain, yet they liked what they saw in the mirror. Even stumpy, half-blind Charlie, muscular but fat. 
Wet and filthy, but sober, Buck and Charlie stood in the doorway eyeing the two girls, now wide awake and watching the boys, listening to the rain on the tin roof and the horse hooves along the lane by the tracks and the lane between the first and second house rows, behind the Rose house. Much shouting could be heard beyond Bernice's bedroom windows, but no gunfire. A donkey brayed. Was it over?
Merry Gay sat up. "Is it over?" she asked.
"Nah," said Charlie. "Won't be long though, now everybody knows the sheriff and them's here."
"Where's Mama?" Buck asked and wiped his wet face with his rolled blue shirtsleeve.
"Mama's dead." Merry Gay stared out the window at the dashing rain. "Jean mistook her for one of Shirley's men I guess and shot her."
"Crazy bitch!" Charlie's face blazed around his fiery beard and he whimpered. "And after all Mama done for her."
"Shot herself too," Merry Gay added to stop it there.
The butter yellow cat slipped through the door, brushing the boys' legs with its tail, each in turn, then flowed over to the bed and hopped up purring and curled close to Tommy, who was lying still on her left side with her knees drawn up.
After returning home from the commissary, Merry Gay and Tommy had cried and talked themselves to sleep. They'd talk Mama for a while, then Will, then Priss, then Samson's attempt to rescue them. Nothing new and nothing that would change anything. And now they were witness to Buck and Charlie doing much the same thing-blurting out, backing off for a good cry, meek, then mad as hell. "Last time I seen Mama?." "And she wadn't no old woman neither?." "Who'd a-thought??" Making it all up as they went till they just wore it out.
"Where's Will, Tommy?" Buck stepped into the rainy light of the room and sat on the foot of the bed, Tommy's side, near the east wall. He squeezed her muddy bare foot on top of the quilt. "Dead, I reckon," he added when she didn't move and didn't answer right away.
"Where's Mama at now?" Charlie crossed over to her dresser, leaning with his broad back reflected in the mirror. He stared up from beneath his thick glasses at the tongue and groove ceiling overhead. "Where's her body at?"
"On Jean's back porch," Merry Gay said.
"I'm sorry, sugar," Buck said to Tommy, crying low and sniffling. "That ole boy was fine as they come. Wadn't nobody I'd ruther mess around with and that's a fact."
Tommy had one hand on the cat, squeezing its green eyes, watching her and Buck. Tommy's eyes closed, white face whiter in the rainy light. Her long dark lashes looked blacker wet.
Then he told them about him and Charlie going to Folkston to get the sheriff, and how the sheriff had to force-deputize the men scattered on farms from Moniac Crossing to Folkston. "They were scared to death of getting involved. Wadn't nobody wanting to get mixed up in our fracas," he said and laughed low. His light brown hair was wet, strands clung, and his scalp shone.
"The sheriff and them'll have everything straightened out by dinnertime. Y'all get on up and we'll see to Mama and Will." Buck stood. "Man, I hate it about Mama."
Charlie stepped away from the dresser and stopped, head bowed, right hand down and turned out in a gesture of shamed satisfaction.
"They took Will, Buck," Tommy said. "Shirley's boys took him and we don't know where." She started sobbing, deep and long, and nobody moved, only waited while Merry Gay told the story about the shootout at the commissary. No mention of Priss because she seemed not to pertain there in the presence of Buck and Charlie; no mention of Shirley's threat because Merry Gay feared it would set them off again. Set them off like dynamite and night would start over again before noon even.
When Buck and Charlie were about ten and twelve, they found a dead dog on the railroad tracks, obliterated to bones and teeth. They took some firecrackers and set them in the jaw of the dog and lit the fuses to watch its teeth explode. If they'd had dynamite now they would have blown up the tracks, they would have blown up the whole town, themselves included. They still might.

***

To keep Buck and Charlie from seeing Merry Gay's bloody dress and demanding more information to tick them off, Tommy went out to heat water for her and her sister to wash up. Merry Gay could hear the teakle over the well squealing like a pig as the rope unreeled, feeding the bucket down into the water, then squealing again as Tommy reeled it up. Rain gushing from the eaves of the porch claimed the sweet pitter of raindrops on the roof. If not for the fervent hoof beats along the lanes this last storm of summer would be a blessed change from hot to cool, from green to gold, sapless leaves scuttling through the wet air and baring the branches of the maples and sweet-gums. 
In the kitchen Buck and Charlie were talking low, frying side-meat and brewing coffee, and Merry Gay could almost believe things were back to that morning-after normal she'd so longed for. But stripping off her blood-stiff yellow dress, bundling it up and stuffing it under the bed, she knew it wasn't over: her mother was dead, Will was dead, Priss was dead, and Shirley and the other men hadn't yet played out their vengeance.
Neither had she.
Tommy came through the door with a black iron kettle of steaming water and poured the white basin on the washstand about half full. Then she set the kettle on the floor and went out again and came again with a bucket full of cold water, a green smell mixing with the steam.
While she undressed, Merry Gay bathed with the sweet soap and warm water, staring out the window at the passing horses and riders along the lane behind the garden and fence of her own yard. She hadn't bothered to pull down the parchment shades because she figured nobody could see in through the screen of rain without the lamps lit. That, but also because she needed to see out.
When she'd done, when she had washed and dressed in her white blouse and navy striped skirt, she went out, leaving Tommy to bathe and dress in the hickory-stripe pinafore belonging to Merry Gay. Tommy could tie the sash snug about the waist and come close to fitting into her larger sister's dress.
But for the rest of the day, after breakfast, they would be slogging in heavy black rubber boots and the cumbersome brown ponchos and hats that their brothers wore to the gum-woods on rainy days. So what did dressing up matter anyway? The sounds of the rain and frogs peeping, blatting and croaking, on the woody fringes of Moniac Crossing finally seemed foreboding, they both agreed, rather than comforting. 
Earlier they had gone out with Buck and Charlie, in ponchos and boots but fresh clothes also. The air reeked of wet soot, bitter burn. At the back porch steps of Anse and Jean's little shotgun house, they had stood in the steady rain, staring at the quilt-wrapped bodies of their mother and Jean and listened to the rain pecking on their rubber hats and ponchos and on the tin roof of Jean's porch. 
A flock of big-bodied shrieking guineas scared up from underneath the porch, scooted like shadows across Jean's yard to her garden. The small house was balanced on great chunks of lime-rock. Staked tents of burlap cooped over the tomato hills in the garden, a few still red and green, but the twined vines of the butterbeans were withy, the brown seed pods too swollen and soppy to rattle in the breeze. Across the back fence and down the line on the other side of the lane, houses and barns that had stood yesterday were blackened and smoking rubble, or no rubble, they had burned so clean. Whole sections of pickets and rails gnawed away. Moss singed in the live oaks, its leathery leaves cooked to a well-done green. 
Nobody spoke, only stood there for a few minutes, as if in prayer or sober benediction.
Finally, Buck said, "I guess Anse ain't home, pore ole boy."
Tommy and Merry Gay eyed each other under their dripping hats. Merry Gay shook her head, signaling Tommy not to tell yet that Anse had gone over to Shirley's side. That he'd been bought with the power of the big man.
Tommy bowed her head, moving her pale lips.
Buck and Charlie stepped up on the porch, peeling the quilts from each body rather than ask which was which.
