They viewed, then stood, each on opposite ends of the porch, Charlie with his hands on his hips and gazing up, and Buck with his hips cocked and one booted foot forward to steady him. Charlie strode over and looked down. "Aye Lordy, shot her through the head!" Then hung his head, weeping silently, "Mama, I won't never drink no more."
"Get her feet, Charlie," Buck said. "We're taking her home till this rain lets up."
They lifted her easy, Charlie backing down the doorsteps and lifting up to balance Bernice as Buck began stepping down.
"Ain't gone let up to my way of thinking," Charlie said, seeming mad at the rain. "Say they's a herricun moving in off the east coast."
They carried the quilted bundle past the girls, who followed them on toward their house, through the narrow gate and up the steps of Bernice's own back porch. Growing from the base of the tall brick pillars under the house were Bernice's faded purple petunias. A lone tumblebug was rolling a marble of dung as if it mattered. Dust funnels of mythical ladybugs and dry dirt tracked with three-pronged chicken claws and the wallowing path of a great snake-the gopher snake they trusted to keep the rattlesnakes at bay and not to crawl out and scare the family. 
Horses were galloping along the lane by the tracks, men shouting at one another in the rain.
"Trying to get at the heart of it, I reckon," said Buck.
"Good luck to em," Charlie said.
They laid the body of their mother easy on the floor between the water-shelf and the walkway from the porch steps to the open hall. Each side of the hall stood oak benches with walls for backrests, and tin tubs hanging above them like de-silvered mirrors. Tommy's yellow cat was walking the porch banister, seeking sun and savoring the relative quiet. 
Backyard trees that last night had been only trees became plum, peach, fig, pomegranate, and so on; things taking on a different meaning in the daylight, especially the death of their mother. As long as Tommy and Merry Gay-and maybe even the boys were thinking the same-had been running, dodging and hiding, the edge of her death had been dulled. Now everywhere, everything, was Bernice, more Bernice that the body bundled in the damp quilt.
Between the porch and the tool shed was a black iron soap kettle, then a clothesline strung between cross-posts. The chicken yard next to the shed was empty, the chickens probably eaten by possums and owls or having taken to the woods or underneath the house. Mended pots and baking pans for watering the yard animals all about the yard were overflowing with rainwater. The brittle gray shed looked like always, nothing out of its place, not even a scorch spot on the cypress shingle roof. Wheelbarrow propped against the wall; and the new red trough-like hay baler ready to go to the hay field if there was any man industrious enough to take it since Hoss Rose died. Everything else was old, everything else was brown or gray, except for the porous white grinding-rock wheel, for sharpening knives and tools, and it was frog-green with moss and mold. The tin lean-to over Bernice's raised seedbed was doing its job of shedding rain from the eaves, so that the delicate green radish seedlings looked merely misted. But if one of Bernice's grown children didn't place the burlap screen over the bed soon somebody else's chickens would scratch the seedlings up.
"Too hot inside, what with the fire in the cookstove from breakfast." Buck said it to the two girls still on the porch steps as if to explain away his guilt for appearing heartless: Bernice couldn't even be laid out respectably on her own kitchen table where she'd laid out so many herself.
"You girls see to her," Buck said, stepping along the wall rather than over Bernice's body to get to the doorsteps. "Me and Charlie is got business to tend to."
And they were gone: men to their business, and women to their own.


CHAPTER 8

Behind a snapping wet white bed sheet hung from the rainy-day clothesline on the east edge of the porch, the girls washed and dressed their mother for burial.
Together they had chosen a light blue cotton dress from the rod of hopeful home-sewn clothes hung cattycorner of the gloomy bedroom where they'd slept the night before. Then they had placed a couple of wide pine planks seat to seat of two kitchen chairs, lifted Bernice up onto the makeshift table, stripped and washed and dressed her.
Beyond the hung sheet sounded like boys playing: here one minute, there the next; the sheriff and his men talking and shouting, begging, cajoling the neighbors into talking. Horses cantered along the lanes. The rain blew through, under, around and over the bed sheet, which the girls had hung for privacy. Mold, mildew, gloom. The floor and walls were wet, spongy as mushroom and mushroom-smelly. Behind the screen of white looked bright as white thunderheads with the setting sun beaming on it, morning light but abnormal. Smoke rising from chimneys, normal; bacon frying in the kitchens, normal; tart smell of pines and creosote of the crossties, normal too. But something was missing-the trains.  
"Ole Shoo Fly-coming through!" Hoss used to bellow, then stand propped up and proud on the commissary porch to watch the flat beds of cotton bales pass. 
Daily he flagged down passenger trains, picnic trains, and even timber trains for anybody needing or wanting to get from here to there, delighting when the engineer acknowledged him with double toots of the whistle before hissing to a halt. In an emergency, all of Moniac Crossing depended on the trains to taxi them to where they needed to go, to a hospital, say, when one of the Negro men got stabbed during a Skin game (you could look out for trouble when the men went to passing under the table cards stuck between their bare toes). Nightly, Hoss flagged the trains down with a burning lightered knot.
Merry Gay gently washed Bernices's left hand, stiff and pale as though her fingertips had been dipped in melted tallow. The damp rag had been rubbed with sweet soap, antidote for the death smell. Avoiding her mother's eyes peeping from beneath half-closed lids, her own eyes leaked tears. "The women always did this before, right Tommy?"
Tommy on the other side of Bernice was washing the right hand. "Kind of makes death seem realer, doesn't it?"
"I'm not ready to be one of those women yet." Merry Gay turned to the water-shelf to rinse and re-soap her rag.
"I'd rather be back changing sheets and sunning mattresses myself."
"I think we'll probably be doing this from here on out."
"Why?"
"Because we're doing it now."
"Let's just do it," Tommy said. "Let's don't think about it, okay?"
A triangle opened in the bed sheet, admitting gray rain and the head of Deputy Threet, dingy eye-whites and swarthy round face. He stood there holding up one corner of the sheet. "Don't tell me that's Miss Hoss y'all got there."
Merry Gay pulled the quilt up over her mother's face. "I expect you could tell us, Deputy Threet."
"Same's going on all over this morning." He turned and sat on the edge of the porch, peeping out around the sheet, blocky head and back to them and legs crossed like a lady's. "A crying shame's what it is."
Tommy and Merry Gay exchanged looks of disgust, waiting for him to leave. 
"Say Anse's wife is dead too," he said. "That right?"
Merry Gay tried to think of something bold to say, how Bernice would have handled him. No, her daddy. How he would tease a coward and send fools walking, exercising their brains. "You just passed by her porch there, I'd lay you ten to one," Merry Gay said. "You know Jean's dead."
"I say good riddance, what I say." He waved his hand out before him. "Woman like that?ain't no tellings."
"Yeah, she was pretty useless, poor old thing." Merry Gay was baiting him now. 
Tommy covered her mouth, black eyes wide with shock.
"That ain't the half of it from what I hear. Hear tell she didn't cook half the time, and beg your pardon, ladies, old Anse would have to slip up on her to have his way with her."
"Mama here's proof how dangerous Jean was."
He half-turned his head, then turned facing out again, as if fearing what he might see. What he might get caught up in having to do. 
"I wouldn't look just yet, Deputy Threet, if I was you." Merry Gay closed her eyes, praying her mother would forgive her irreverence, her Hoss-Rose bent for plying foolishness on fools. "Well, I guess it wouldn't matter now Mama's dead, and truth be told we could use some help. Jean over there, for one, you could?"
Tommy leaned against the wall at the head of the corpse, burying her face in her hickory stripe apron. Trembling all over, either laughing or crying. When her apron dropped from her face, Merry Gay saw that she was doing both, laughing and crying. Her ears were red, her face was flushed. Her eyes were wet with tears.
Deputy Threet stood on the ground, letting the corner of the sheet go. "Well, I'll be shoving off, ladies. Got the living to see to, you know? And looks like y'all managing just fine."
"I wish you'd check on Jean for us when you go back by-she keeps sitting up and her eyes fly open. Push her down and close her eyes. Oh, and you wouldn't happen to have an extra pair of handcuffs on you, would you?"
"Handcuffs, you say?" 
They could see his frozen silhouette behind the sheet. 
"Yeah. If you put em on Jean it'll keep us from having to slap her around while we?uh?fix her, you know."
He cackled hesitantly. "Y'all messing with me now."
"And our own mother laying here dead!"
"You got enough of Hoss Rose in you to make light of death, like he done with old Redbone the day we buried him over in Fargo. Or have you forgot?"
"That was an accident."
"That song you sung wadn't."
"I was a child, didn't know better."
"Well he did, he knowed better."
After his hunting buddy Redbone's funeral, Hoss Rose would never take death serious again-life, yes, but not death, which he had no control over. He and Merry Gay never played and sang at a funeral after Redbone's because they couldn't trust their tickle boxes not to turn over, extreme laughter akin to crying.  
"I hate it about your mama, I do. I hate it about Anse's wife too." Threet still had his back to them, dark round head wagging like it was loose. "Say she keeps sitting up, huh?"
"Don't mean nothing really." Merry Gay changed tones, standing along the wall next to Tommy, in fits of stifled laughter. "Give me a hand here, Tommy, will you. That is if you can let go of that for a minute." She changed tones again. "They say it's nothing but nerves, you know like when you kill a rattlesnake and it keeps shaking its rattles and squirming."
"Listen, I gotta get on down the tracks, see about them boys there. Say they got trains being stopped and robbed all over."
"You be careful now, you hear. Now you and Buck and Charlie are on the outs, you better sprout eyes in back of your head."
"Buck and Charlie? Why, we're buddies."
"Were?were buddies. They know you're in with Nate Shirley, heard you was one of them fellows knocking me and Tommy around at the commissary last night."  
"I wadn't on the place; Wendell Shirley'll vouch for that. We was both out trying to keep the peace. Tell em that."
"They'll be back in a minute and you can tell them yourself."
"I gotta go. Tell em I'll catch em later, swap a bottle with em maybe."
Merry Gay stepped to the edge of the porch and lifted the corner of the sheet, watching him walking fast toward the back gate with his right hand on the pistol in his holster.
"Check on Jean now, will you?" she called out. "And thanks for stopping by to pay your respects."
He kept walking, finger in the air, wagging-he had business to see to. 
Tommy and Merry Gay watched him from the water-shelf till he disappeared behind the line of peach trees in Roberta's yard.
"You scared him to death," Tommy said.
"He was born scared," Merry Gay said. "But I can't believe he really bought that about Jean sitting up and all."
 Tommy looked at Merry Gay, both sober and sad again. "I just learned something about men from him."
"You can't judge all men by him, Tommy."
"I know that. But I do think they've all got one thing in common. They're afraid of us women."
"I wouldn't go that far."
"No, they are. They're afraid of what we're made of, what's inside that makes them need us." Her cheeks grew flushed, roses of pink. And her head seemed to rise from the stem of her cream neck. "We can grow life inside us and that's something, that's something."
  When they were done they lapped Bernice's hands over her chest and brushed her heavy blondish hair, then covered her with a fresh quilt she'd pieced and stitched from scraps of the family's worn-to-a-frazz clothes.
Tommy walked over to the water-shelf, staring out through the rain at the house next door. "We have to lay Jean out, you know that."
"I know that." 
"And if Anse catches us??"
"We'll lay him out too." Merry Gay pulled the pistol from her skirt pocket. "While we're over there, I need to get some more ammunition. Then if you want to we'll go looking for Will."
"No, he's dead. And finding him won't?" She didn't finish. "What about Nate Shirley? Would you shoot him too?"
"I've got something better in mind for him."
Tommy faced Merry Gay, hands on the water-shelf behind her. Rain-drenched sheet sucking and popping in the wind. "You mean telling him he shot Priss. And what if he doesn't believe you?"
"He will. He knows it wasn't Buck and Charlie in the lavatory by now. He should know that Will was in the smoking car when Priss was killed in the lavatory; he was right there with him. And only that morning Wendell had warned the Negroes not to go to the lavatory in the dining car. Could be Priss has been found and he's figured that he's likely the one who shot her. Can you imagine him telling Wendell and Sara he killed their little girl?" Merry Gay started out the porch steps. "Let's go get Jean."
Sure enough that Anse was still out "on business," like Charlie and Buck, Merry Gay and Tommy stopped on the porch and moved Jean's body out of the blowing rain. She was light as a papier mache bunny but puffed up and her bloody lips were cracked and dry. They went on inside the nearly dark closed house smelling of the damp and cold soot from the cook-stove in the kitchen. Dirty diaper smells, soured milk, and stale chicory grounds.
Having only a single idea where Anse might keep his ammunition, they followed their instincts to the front bedroom, to the standing cedar armoire, generally used for storing arms, in the corner at the right foot of the iron bed.
No ammunition in the drawers, none on the shelves, but the sweet smell of cedar was pleasant while the girls searched. There was a warped black belt hanging from the door with a broken hinge (the belt looked six feet long, a mystery of the body's girth as perceived in length); every time the damaged door swung out at a tilt, it creaked and Merry Gay's nerve endings flared.
"Go watch for Anse," she whispered to Tommy. Merry Gay handed the pistol to her and began feeling inside the pockets of an old black coat but found only a dried-up buckeye fruit that Anse had carried around for luck and it made him seem less menacing, more boy-like and put upon. On the floor of the wardrobe someone had scattered dried chrysanthemums and marigolds to thwart the wool-eating moths. The thistles of once-blooming flowers haunted Merry Gay: who Jean was before, who she might have been again.
Anse had a sharp musk smell, a swampy, rotted-cabbage smell. Merry Gay couldn't smell it in his clothes she was pilfering through but it was absorbed into her memory, her brain. The smell reminded her of the people she'd sung for at a small country holiness church near Moniac Crossing. Poor people, good people, some of the kindest, friendliest she'd ever sung for, but also ignorant. She was fresh and cute and sang like an angel; that's what they said. They just had to hug her. They looked smoked, smelled of smoke, or snuff. Their unwashed bodies and clothes made the church house reek; she couldn't wait to get away from them. Later, when she was older, she realized that she had a bias against such people for no reason other than her nose. Not poverty-she liked poor people, she liked poor people's starchy food and she herself was poor. 
Finally, searching under the iron bed with the cotton and feather mattresses supported by spaced-out wooden slats, Merry Gay found a metal lock box, unlocked and floored with assorted gun shells, a cloth bag of small silver bullets that matched the ones left in the pistol Tommy was carrying back and forth, front to end of the long closed hall, front to back and front again.
Weighing the bag of bullets in her right hand, Merry Gay tiptoed out into the hall, holding them out for Tommy to feel. The boarded-up house had grown lighter since their eyes had adjusted and they could see daylight through the cracks and seams of the cured reddish pine. 
First, they loaded the pistol, sliding bullets into the chamber; then Merry Gay placed it in the pocket of her skirt again. They were set to head out through the kitchen when they heard footsteps on the porch, muffled by the rain.
Merry Gay and Tommy leaned into the hall wall. Pistol aimed at the open door from the kitchen to the hall. Somebody was standing on the porch, standing over Jean's body it seemed. Rancy lard smells hovered in the damp.
"What if it's Threet?" Tommy whispered, so close that Merry Gay could smell her. She was a harsh-soap girl, clean but never sweet. 
"You couldn't throw him within a half-mile of here now." Merry Gay scratched her nose. 
"Samson?"
"I don't think so, not and it daylight."
They waited a couple of minutes, then when the wind picked up howling around the corner of the kitchen, they ducked low and started for the bedroom to hide.
Outside men on horseback, sounded like a dozen or more, were passing along the lane by the tracks. Merry Gay thought she heard Buck and Charlie talking in the middle sweep of the horses' sucking hooves in mud. 
Slowly the kitchen door to the porch opened wide, a dim chute of light. Merry Gay and Tommy sidled along the hall wall and into the bedroom where they'd found the ammunition, slipped inside and stood peeping out at the hall.
"I hope you meant what you said about laying out Anse," Tommy whispered. 
Merry Gay raised the pistol, hearing footsteps coming up the hall. 
"What if it really is Jean?" Tommy giggled low. 
But the hair stood on Merry Gay's arms imagining it.
"Anse?" a woman called. "That you, Anse?"
"It's Roberta," Merry Gay said, opening the door. "It's Merry Gay, Roberta. Me and Tommy."
She was wearing a wet black knitted shawl around her broad shoulders. "Y'all scared the living daylights out of me."
Merry Gay started to ask how she was feeling but she looked hale as ever, toughened by having made it through the night. Roberta despised weakness, in a woman or a man, and had been known to put up a front when ill with what she called low blood.  
"Where's the baby?" Merry Gay asked. "What about the children?"
"Neighbor lady's got 'em. I didn't know what come of y'all so I decided to see to your mama. Word is Shirley's got it in for you. And Tommy. Specially Tommy."
"What about Travis?" Tommy asked. "Where is he?"
"He's riding with the sheriff and them. But as of about an hour ago, they hadn't made a bit of headway. Ain't nobody talking, looks like. Either they're in too deep or scared to talk, Travis says." She took off her wet shawl and scrubbed at her matted ash-blond hair.
"Buck and Charlie came in this morning and carried Mama to the house for us to see to," Merry Gay said. "We got her laid out on her back porch for now."
"We came to get Jean," Tommy added, "figuring Anse wouldn't?and her family doesn't know."
"She come from right over there at Baxter," Roberta said. "Ain't much to that bunch of people though."
"Tommy's Will got shot last night, Roberta."
Roberta didn't say a word, just stood there, staring at her feet in old work brogans. Then she walked over to Tommy standing next to Merry Gay and hugged her, rocking as she whispered. "Everylast one of em oughta be horsewhipped," she said. "Drinking and showing out. And me, strong as a horse generally.  I let her down. I let em all down."
Merry Gay didn't know whether the "her" Roberta made reference to was Bernice or Jean, maybe both. 
"I don't have a bit of use for them Shirleys, after the way they done Travis and me," Roberta said, "But I do hate it about that lil ole friendly gal getting killed. Used to she'd come over and play with the babies so I could get out in the woods some." 
So, Merry Gay thought, word about Priss had spread, through the neighbor lady or Travis maybe. She had never before heard Roberta carry a tale; she didn't talk and didn't like other people who talked. 
This time it was Merry Gay who didn't want to talk. "Let's go see to Jean," Merry Gay said. She wanted to hear what was being said about Priss but didn't want to say anything yet. She knew Tommy wouldn't talk about it.
They were losing light fast. It must have been three or four, after noon. The clock in the parlor had not been wound and had stopped sometime during the night. The rain was coming harder and the wind was flapping the flimsy sheet curtain of the makeshift morgue on Bernice's back porch, misting the twin bodies laid out side by side on planks. Roberta had insisted on doing most of the work of laying out Jean, because the girls had handled Bernice she said. The two washed and dressed bodies were covered in quilts and then brown rubber ponchos, which Roberta suggested, probably held in the heat. Not a good idea.
 Twice Roberta had gone to eat and nurse the babies, bringing back biscuits and fried ham for the girls and news from the all-knowing neighbor lady: one of the young Negro women had come looking for her husband who was killed last night at the commissary and had been raped and beaten to death; another Negro woman, one of the old gray ones, had sneaked out of the woods looking for food at her house and was shot standing addled in the ashes of her kitchen; a sniper had fired a shot at the sheriff and he had gone to Folkston to telegraph for the state Militia. 
They needed to have a funeral, bury Bernice and Jean and be done with their grief. Be done with the sight and waxy feel of the lifeless bodies beginning to look bruised and bloated, but smelling of soap, clean but soon to be filthy again. And too the swampy flatwoods would only get wetter and might float up the caskets; at least, the dead would have been buried, awful as it seemed.
The men hadn't come in but the women could hear them up and down the lanes in front of and behind the house, eerily calling out in the rain. The women discussed burying the dead without notifying the men. There were no caskets, and no time to build them; they would have to make do with the buckle steamer trunks inside the Rose house. A little bit of arranging and the bodies would fit. Bernice would be curled up as she had always slept, so that was okay. 

***

Good daylight and Hoss Rose had been driving the wagon, moving slow toward the old tram road through the woods to Fargo under a sheet of iron clouds threatening sleet. Little Merry Gay was bundled up in a sweet red wool coat that her mama had cut down and sewn from a coat that some woman had given her to wear herself, and on Merry Gay's lap a quilt with scraps from one of her old indigo dresses. She imagined she could smell pee on the quilt because one of the Negro women had told Bernice that the way to set the dye in cloth was to dip it in ammonia and if she didn't have any ammonia a chamber pot of pee would do the trick.
She dropped the quilt to the floor and kneeled on the bench, waving back at Bernice and Tommy watching from the yard across the tracks. Bernice looked worried. The Great Hoss Rose looked worried. But Merry Gay wasn't because she knew the pine box in back would hold up fine, her daddy had been two days building it with his own sturdy square hands and it would hold up fine.
As soon as they hit the tram road and the woods had closed the path created by logs snaked out by oxen and mules, she crawled over into the bed of the wagon and climbed up on the yellow pine box. It was long, man-sized, and smooth but cold, the perfect stage for her to sing on if not for Redbone needing it.
"You rip that pretty coat now," Hoss called back laughing, "and your mammy's gonna have both our hides." He always said that and sounded like he meant it, even though he would make two of Bernice and feared no man regardless of strength, temper or size.
Usually he drove the wagon loose, lead-lines in one hand and letting it roll, but this morning he was slumped forward driving with both hands. Rifle stood on the floor at his right side, he stared ahead through the woods as if searching for something he might shoot.
"I'm fixing to sing for you, Daddy," she said. "All the way to Fargo." 
"Well, ain't nothing I'd ruther hear and that's the truth, baby. But you better save it for old Redbone's funeral. You get the sorethroat, you won't be able to sing, hear?"
His voice boomed out over the frosty green pines and palmettos. In clearings of shed scrub trees and bushes, like smoke, drab green curds of reindeer moss put down shallow roots in the hard ground. They looked like sea sponge washed ashore and dried in the sun till brittle and stiff, out of place in the hardy woods but magical and mysterious for that very reason.
She sat swinging her legs and kicking the box with her the heels of her button-up black high-top shoes. His smoked brown banjo lay on the bed of the wagon at her feet. Between songs she asked, "You sad, Daddy?"
"I am, baby. I reckon I am. Me and old Redbone goes a long way back."
She sang in her shaky trilling tenor all the songs she knew. Sang 'Jingle Bells' twice because it was almost Christmas. Maybe on the way home they would find a Christmas tree.
"It'll hold up fine, Daddy," she said when she was done.
"I hope so, baby. I hope so."
Happening up out of the thick woods were sawdust piles like yellow mountains, the sawmill crews gone on to other locations for their saw lumber. They came upon a massive timber car so high above the tram rails that Merry Gay had to sit on her daddy's shoulders to see inside-tree bark and pine straw. Somebody had left a rectangular metal scrape bucket and a square-hooked iron and Hoss opined one of the hands had quit and moved on, having found the scabbed tar, the cheapest grade of resin, too stubborn and bothersome to scrape from the cat-faces of the pines. "You have to take the bad with the good," he told the little red-faced girl in the red coat.
 By the time they got to the Suwannee River, about a half mile south of Fargo, it was midday and the sky had brightened only slightly. Still those iron clouds but they had been forged into thick rods and stacked. A faint tang of tannic water and a hint of wood-smoke were in the air.
At the wooden bridge the horse's hooves changed from their familiar thudding on the ground to hollow clacking and echoes of clacking as if from the sudden void of dense woods, the open space of sliding pewter light, the vibration of the rushing black water below surging against the bridge pilings. Whorls sucking the light under and spewing it up as foam to collect with the sticks and dead leaves in the eddies along the banks, wringing willow and towering cypress trees with flat tops and rusty needles, their knees sticking up from the shallow patches of sand. Streamers of moss looked iced.
"Is that Fargo, Daddy?" From her perch on the box behind him, Merry Gay sat almost as tall as the Great Hoss Rose, high on the wagon moving toward the break in the road. 
"Far as you can go," he chuckled.
"Daddy!" She laughed.
"Well, trains heading east, once upon a time, would run out of track. How come it's called Fargo, they say. Far as you can go."
 "Far as heaven where Redbone is now?"
"Ain't no tellings, baby. Ain't no tellings. Me nor him neither one wadn't never much on religion."
"Mama says it's that old white deer haunting y'all."
"She's just jealous, baby. Mad cause I wouldn't let her dress me up in my one good suit."
"Why?"
"Redbone's people don't have no suits."
"No. Why is she jealous?"
"You ask more questions than Tommy."
"Are you talking like that because Redbone's people talk like that?"
"I'm talking like I always talk."
He wasn't. It was as if he were practicing for the funeral like they'd practiced the song. No, it was as if there were two sides to Hoss Rose, the smooth side for women and the rough side for men. She felt let in on something special, riding high behind him.
Up ahead were dogs and horses and wagons and people in head rags and bonnets and hats. They looked big in their padded coats, like they were wearing all they owned. All were moving slow in the cold from one store to the other, from the heat of one wood stove to the next. The same tall longleaf pines as in Moniac formed a fortress around the little town, same railroad tracks bringing the outside in but seldom taking the inside out. No money so no reason to go anywhere. Men like the Great Hoss Rose and Redbone wouldn't have left these parts if they'd been rich. Hell, they were rich!
"You wanta quit kicking that box for me?" he snapped.
She hadn't noticed her shoe heels drumming on the box. Somehow the sound had grown into the horse clopping and the wagon rattling. But she knew it was the beat of the crazy song playing out in her head.
She had hoped to pass among the people, show off her daddy's fine box; maybe go in one of the stores for some ribbons for Tommy's wild hair. But Hoss took the left fork running south along the railroad tracks, then right again along a row of facing run-down houses. Rooty hog smells and collard greens or cabbage and stringy boiled beef, grass-fed. Dogs sat scratching by the side of the lane and children played chase, bare pale ankles and legs shining like bone. Even the cats looked rough. Dead trees, dead-looking houses; except for the smoke spiriting up from the chimneys. All had congregated at the large roof-heavy house at the end of the lane. Women in bonnets were passing in and out the front porch with rag-tented pans and bowls. Men in drab hats and clothes, old clothes, like Hoss's, were backed up to a yard fire built from a great lightered stump, giving off scorch and soot smells, rolling black smoke tangling with red flames. 
"Ho!" Hoss hauled on the lead-lines, though the horse was barely moving then. A cautious horse retired from saddle riding when he grew too tender in the flanks and wary of bear, wary now of all the commotion or fusing smells or maybe the men rotating around the smutty fire who resembled bears, who smelled wild as bear.
Smoke spread over Merry Gay on the pine box and stung her eyes, so that she had to cover them.
Hoss swung down, springing the wagon. He strolled over to the fire and began shaking hands with each of the men, then hugging and slapping backs heartily. The Great Hoss Rose, the biggest bully, the one everybody came to fight from all over South Georgia and North Florida, trying to claim his title. Growing boys wanted to be like him and women wanted to catch his eye, but girls like Tommy and Merry Gay were scared to death that the sporting fights would turn mean and they'd be caught in the middle. 
She and Tommy had seen two grown women fight their way from the commissary porch to the yard, in pouring-down rain. Men on the porch and Hoss included, looking scared, hanging their heads, walking the floor, and mumbling. In the mud puddle between the porch steps and the tracks the women pulled hair and clawed and almost ripped each other's frocks off.
Bernice, from her front porch across the tracks, shook her head and said, "All that over some man!"
"Which man?" Tommy asked.
"You girls, go on back inside." Bernice had shooed them away with one hand. "And if I ever hear tell of either one of y'all cutting the fool like that, I'll take my tee-weed switch to you."
Merry Gay and Tommy, heading inside, thought at first she was mad with them. Then they heard her yelling out through her hands, "Hoss Rose, you started it, now you finish it."
Now, a fat woman on the wide sloping front porch of the lopsided house was crying into a handkerchief and a scrawny carrot-haired boy on the broken doorsteps was wheezing out a sad church tune on a harmonica. The men at the fire stepped in one accord to the end of the wagon while Hoss reached over the side and lifted Merry Gay down from the pine box he'd built for their mutual buddy. One man had flews under his chin like an old hound's. Another had a wen on his neck.
Next time she saw the box it was being loaded onto the wagon bed again and she had to sit on the bench next to her daddy. He was sweating in the cold from helping load it. She scooted as close as she could get to him without disturbing his rifle and leaned into his cold padded coat. She knew Redbone was in the box now because the six men from the fire had almost buckled under its weight while carrying it from the house to the wagon. While Hoss drove the wagon back along the row of houses, she had to be quiet and think the song she would sing without kicking her shoe heels on the bench wall.
At the little white church across the road they'd taken earlier at the fork, the people she'd seen eating at the house and wandering the yard and open hall were standing three-deep along the outside. The men who'd helped load and unload the pine box were riding in another wagon behind Hoss's wagon. They hopped off, straightening their coats and hats, wiping tobacco from the corners of their lips, and walked over to Hoss's wagon, pulled right up to the front of the church. He stepped down and lifted Merry Gay down and while he got his banjo from in back of the wagon she stood gazing up at all the big women and men and head-on with a girl about her own size. The little girl, Redbone's girl, was squinch-eyed, blue-eyed, clinging to her fat mama's dress tail.  
	Merry Gay wanted to tell her not to worry, that her daddy was in heaven, but she figured after what her own daddy had said about that there was no sense getting the girl's hopes up so that when she got to heaven someday and her daddy wasn't there she wouldn't be in for a disappointment. But really the girl didn't look like the kind to be wasting time in church or worrying. She was blond and puny but growny looking, and her thin pale lips twitched like she was smirking or up to something. It could have been the snuff.
Hoss took Merry Gay's hand in his right, holding to the neck of the banjo with his left, and they followed the six men struggling with the pine box and Redbone in it.
"Aye God, if he ain't heavy!" one whispered. He looked like a tadpole.
"Well, don't shift him over here on me," the man across from him whispered. "Hoss, catch hold of the foot will you?"
Hoss handed his banjo to Merry Gay and lay hold to the end of the casket. "Easy, boys, easy. Ain't no guarantees on this thing."
The banjo felt as heavy to Merry Gay as the pine box looked, but really it was awkward to handle without accidentally strumming the strings, which was happening right along every time she tried to brace it against her chest like her daddy. Merry Gay thought they were going inside the church where her hands might thaw and she could put the banjo down, but all the people straggled off around the south side of the church like they were in a hurry to be done with Redbone and get in out of the cold. The men with the pine box stumbled behind them, then Redbone's fat wife in faded black and his little girl with a lump of snuff in her nether lip and Merry Gay with the banjo. 
Lots of crying, sobbing, weeping; even a wail or two, out of respect maybe, but Merry Gay couldn't see who it was. She could see to her sides, plain headstones and mounded-dirt graves, one with a broken glass lamb and a rat tail of snuff spat by Redbone's girl drying in on the dirt, but she couldn't see ahead.
Redbone's fat wife and little girl stopped and Merry Gay stopped, but Hoss blocking the pine box from view kept walking forward like he was holding to the stocks of a plow being pulled by a mule. The gap he left soon closed in with people. Everybody smelled like smoke. Then the fat lady shoved through and Merry Gay and Redbone's girl followed to see the pallbearers setting the pine box on a waist-high wood scaffold with thick spaced lengths of rope draped and swagged like a hammock end to end and trailing along the piles of raw dirt on either side. Beneath the scaffold and box was a long deep hole carved from the marled white clay and gray dirt.
Redbone's wife with a mole like a dog tick on her chin was crying into a dingy white handkerchief as she sat in a kitchen chair a few yards from the scaffold. Merry Gay tried to get close to the woman to get warm. But her little girl snuggled on her right and some church-lady types with hair balled on back of their heads like Redbone's wife hovered on the other side. Most of the women were fat and looked self-satisfied. (Redbone always said that a woman puffed up on religion and food is right even when she's wrong. Said their type would mistake Jesus Christ for Satan even with nail scars in His hands.)
Merry Gay held the banjo by the neck, the body of it barely clear of the dirt, then she figured to rest it on the toe of her shoe and so let her arms relax.
An old old preacher in a black suit stepped out of the crowd and strode over to the head of the casket. He was carrying a black Bible with his yellowed thumb stuck inside between the pages to mark his place.  He opened the Bible with his thumb and mumbled a verse or two, then prayed, thumb in his Bible again. While he prayed the women hissed, "Yes, Jesus, thank you Jesus," over and over and Redbone's wife quit crying and joined in, filtering the chant through her handkerchief.
Hoss, standing with the men on the other side of the pine box on the scaffold, stepped around to where Merry Gay was standing. He lifted the banjo off her toe and took her small cold hand in his and they walked over in the loose new dirt to the head of the box and the preacher with his head bowed, thumb marking his place in the Bible. He smelled stale with age, like old lace.
Hoss strapped his banjo to his wall-like chest and strummed a few bars, whispering down at Merry Gay. "Now, sing it, baby, sing it for ole Redbone."
She was too cold. The song had frozen in her brain. Her teeth were chattering. He plucked the same bars over again-with his fingers and not a pick; it could have been any song she was about to sing. Everybody was watching her, even the preacher.
"How sweet," said one of the women standing next to Redbone's wife and little girl.
"Sing it, angel," another said in a tweety voice.
"Sing it, baby," Hoss said. "Sing it for ole Redbone."
"Shy little thing, ain't she?" one of the women hissed. "Sign of a modest woman later on."
She was shy but she didn't like people knowing it. She squeezed her eyes shut picturing herself back on the wagon, on the pine box, singing and kicking her heels, until she recalled the start of the song and trusted that the rest would follow.
"I'd as live die if ain't no deerwoods,
 whiskey and women left to try
I'd as live die if ain't no dogs to run
neath a starry sky

Lord, take me on the fast train to gloryland,
to gloryland, to glory land,
take me on the fast train to gloryland
and them deerwoods in the sky."

Nobody was smiling, nobody crying, except for the men from the yard fire standing on the other side of the box. The women next to Redbone's wife were crossing their arms and puffing up like toad-frogs, and the preacher's mouth gaped wide, showing a brown coffee tongue.
Hoss cut his eyes toward the pine box on the scaffold while he plucked the tune on the banjo. Behind her, as she sang, Merry Gay thought she heard one of the men fart, farting, but sounded more like a nail being yanked out of wood with a claw hammer.
She glanced at the box on her left while singing the chorus again and saw it shift a little to one side and kind of bob up at the head. Everybody was whispering, pointing her way, enough to break her heart. And then she saw what they were really looking at -Redbone's bare white feet and ankles hanging down over the hole from a rip in the floor at the foot of the casket. She saw him drop, all three- hundred pounds of flab and hair, dressed only in his brown plaid shirt, as the bottom fell out and the scaffold collapsed. Splintering wood and boards clapping together and a loud whump as Redbone hit home.
It was almost dusk when Hoss and Merry Gay stopped along the tram road for him to chop down the white-pine sapling she'd picked out for a Christmas tree. In the sealed cold of the woods, they loaded the tree in the bed of the wagon where the pine box had been.
"Sing to me, baby. Sing to me," he said when they'd loaded up again. He still sat slumped over but sounded cherry.
She was on back of the wagon with the bristly pine tree, fresh tar smells and Christmas pine. "Can I sing 'Jingle Bells'?"
"'Jingle Bells' is fine, long as it ain't about fast trains to gloryland." He laughed then, not laughing like he'd laughed when Redbone dropped through the floor of the box, but a wry lingering laugh, the put-on laugh of an adult who knows there is no Santa Claus.
Almost home, almost dark, but a queer light hovered after so much gloom over a field of dry broom-sage and bushy green myrtle bushes. She was singing when she saw his right hand rise, his signal for her to be still and quiet. 
He hauled back on the reins and his great body rose out of its slump and he stepped easy from the wagon, not shaking it, not a sound. Reaching inside for his rifle, he positioned the stock on his right shoulder and peered down the barrel at some secret target on his left.
There all the time, the sharp head and curved rack of a white deer, the albino, formed out of the wheat-like broom-sage, like a dream of a deer, like a ghost. Suddenly he reared up on his hind legs to see over the bushes it seemed and his rack contained too many points to count before he dropped from sight.
When Merry Gay looked at her daddy again, the rifle was down by his side and he was grinning.
Why didn't he shoot? she wondered.
"Next time," he called out. "I'll get you next time, see if I don't."


CHAPTER 9

After the dead women were placed in the trunks, Roberta went home to nurse the babies again, as much to relieve her engorged breasts as for the benefit of the babies. She had to feed her chickens before the rain got harder, she had to milk the cow before dark.
Wearing brown rubber ponchos over their damp clothes, Merry Gay and Tommy set out through the garden to slop the hogs in the parlor on the edge of the woods. Going to seed, save for the red hot peppers thriving among the sodden wilted tomato vines, Bernice's garden was laid out in blocks with wide flower-lined paths between the framed vegetable plots. Overhead twiggy vines trailed along spaced rafters, creating a dappled, banded shade when the sun shined through. 
They had to cross the vacant lot of shedding pecan trees behind Bernice's house, swinging oak buckets by the bails and dodging for cover from crib to shelter to trees along the way. From a stall in the first barn came the wicked blatting of the ram Bernice and Merry Gay had sheared on Saturday. A croaker sack of his oily dingy wool was still leaning in the breezeway where they had left it. The naked white ram with shear marks across the saddle of his back, collops of white hide, lardy waves, poked his head through the opening under the gate, feeling free now that his head was free, threaded horns setting off his pleading eyes. As soon as Merry Gay lifted the wooden latch, he burst from the pen, trotting toward the green woods.  The hoarse cawing of tattletale crows, ricocheting from the pecan trees, announced them, but they saw nobody and were seen by nobody, as best they could tell.
 They could smell and hear the hogs before they got there. They smelled rooty and sharp, their bass rumbling grunting a signal that they were starving, waiting for the two girls with their dishwater slops. The parlor was sunken in vines and bushes, located next to the creek, with tea-tinted water trickling in to the wallow on the sloped south end. When the girls approached on the high north end of the pen the hogs shuffled, snorting and beating the sour mud composed of manure and ammonia with their cloven hooves, to the south end. When they dumped the slops into the trough, the hogs shifted back in unison, a union of hogs-hectic shadows behind the slattered walls. 
The rain pecked on the tin roof, on the rank warm thicket of briars and dog fennel. Honeysuckle, trumpet and fox-grape vines spewed like fountains from the clustered scrub oaks and pines. Soon it would be fall and the leaves would be gone, woody tangles of vines like woven primal nests, but for now the woods were green, almost sun-lit green, in spite of the swirling layers of dark clouds overhead. Heat emanated from the green, a sort of living energy, quick growth before death. Tommy and Merry Gay were sweating inside their ponchos, though the rain was cool on their faces and they could imagine the heat of the waiting bodies under the ponchos on the porch.
"It'll be dark soon," Tommy said, "the rain'll get harder before it lets up."
"Why don't I go look for Buck and Charlie?" Merry Gay said, coming up the hill toward home.
"Would you let me go?" Tommy asked.
"That's different."
"You reckon how did Jean's baby get in that wood-box like that, all bedded down in that flannel sheeting?"
"I guess she put him there."
"Does that mean she didn't shoot Mama when she ripped the boards off the door and opened it, scaring her?"
"I don't know that I can think about that, Tommy, not now." Their buckets bumped together like a signal for the tears to join the rain on their cheeks. "Probably there's a lot we'll never figure out how it happened."
"If we make it through this our own selves." 
"Poor old Jean, she was sick, couldn't help how she was. I hate us calling her crazy behind her back."
Tommy laughed, dabbing at the tears on her pale cheeks. "I guess it's kinder than talking about her to her face."
"Priss used to say that was the southern way, talking about somebody behind their back and not coming right out with it. Next day, she said, we'd likely be changed our minds anyway."
Roberta had her buckboard pulled up to the garden gate when they got there. The scruffy dull brown mare stood patiently in the rain while Merry Gay, Tommy and Roberta loaded the poncho-covered trunks with the bodies inside and a shovel for digging the graves. Two wooden commissary crates full of neatly stacked fruit jars of vegetables and jellies that Roberta had put up that summer were shoved to the front of the buckboard behind the bench. In another one was a large smoked ham with bear grass strung through the hock for hanging. They loaded up, all three on the front bench of the buckboard, Roberta hissing at the mule and flapping the lead-lines.
"I guess we're having a picnic," Tommy said.
"Somebody is," Roberta said, guiding the mare from the yard to the lane along the tracks.
Tommy and Merry Gay knew who. Merry Gay figured it was the story of the old colored woman in the remains of her kitchen that had done it, that had broken Roberta's heart and brought her around. Or maybe the food was some kind of holy offering, penance for Roberta's perceived sin of sloth last night. Tommy elbowed Merry Gay in the ribs. She elbowed her back, telegraphing the message that she hadn't a clue to how Roberta planned to deliver the food to the Negroes hiding out in the west woods. 
Heading north up the lane along the tracks, they saw Nate Shirley's car stuck in the mud, the wheels slewed toward the track near the start of his yard and corner of woods. Gauzy rain closed off both the north and south tracks veeing out into a child's notion of the earth's end. Across the tracks, raindrops fried in the hot ashes from the house fires and lifted tiny puffs of smoke and ash; the houses left standing looked sodden, dark and empty. A few dogs wandered through the ashes, hiking their legs and pissing to mark their find, sniffing for bits of charred food. And way off, they could hear other dogs barking, an owl hooting from the west woods. But mostly they heard rain, rain tapping on the rubber ponchos, rain and wind and way-off thunder and the plodding hooves of the mare. Now and then voices sounded from the east rows of houses; a door slammed, and a horse neighed.
Tommy was seated between Merry Gay and Roberta, all solemn for the occasion, all in comic disguise of brown ponchos and hats. Anybody seeing them would have been hard-put to distinguish them from the men. Still, Merry Gay held the pistol underneath her poncho; she would shoot if she had to.
 She didn't really feel bad about the boys missing their mother's funeral. They should have come home again, though she hadn't expected it. She was used to their virile helplessness: Mama went to church for them, Mama represented the family at funerals; Mama would get them into heaven on her prayers. With Mama gone, they'd have to trust they could make it to old age and repentance, not having sacrificed their prime sin years.  
The three women had to go past the Shirleys' front yard, the white picket fence and gate with the hanging bell, the ancient leaking live oaks draped in wet moss and smelling of mold. Angling off the dead-end lane, they stayed to the edge of the block-shaped cane patch-a square of clustered purple and green glossy stalks. In the open gloom of the pecan orchard between the woods and the house the pole of the cane-grinding mill stood ready for a mule to hitch up and circle the muddy trench, traveling, going nowhere, where he'd gone so many times before. The syrup shed to the right of the mill looked wet and dark, brown leaves stuck to the tin roof. Along the north fence, ruddy freckled pomegranates had dropped from the rain-soaked trees, pear trees interlaced and their fruit rotting on the dark ground too, buzzed by feeding yellow jackets. The shed looked like a place gone to ruin in the rain and coming cold, a place circled round on itself like the muddy trench of the cane mill, like Tommy and Merry Gay since the train got back to Moniac Crossing.
The rickety buckboard followed the three-path road cut by horse and wagon wheels out of the wiregrass and palmettos and pines to get to the church cemetery which Shirley claimed was on his property now. Long ago Old Man Miller had deeded it to the residents of Moniac for all time, cemetery and the Moniac Methodist Church plat next to it, which Shirley now claimed too. Out of the hearing of the men, Bernice, entrusted with peacekeeping too, fumed aloud, "Wadn't it enough he laid claim to the land we live on without laying claim to our dead too?"
His justification was that the cemetery, already expanded twice since he came to Moniac territory in 1900, would continue to spill over onto his land, and somebody had to pay taxes on it. Somebody had to keep up the picket fence that kept out the free-ranging hogs and cattle, his livestock too, whether or not they wore his brand, whether or not the hogs' ears were marked with his signature sickle and swallow-fork. They soon would be. So, who could argue with that? Nobody but the Great Hoss Rose, and he was gone, laid to rest forever on Shirley land, just as Bernice would soon be.
The rain that beat the weary women down likewise provided protection. They had seen nobody at the Shirley house, and if anybody inside had seen them through the large mullioned windows made opaque by the glaring rainy light, they hadn't made themselves known. The air off the green woods was wet and cool, like the fall rains setting in after a long dry summer. Soon to be cozy after all chores were done, after the women had put up tomatoes, peas, peaches and pears for the winter, fodder, hay and corn set aside for the cattle, hogs and chickens, cords and cords of firewood cut that seemed like enough to last for months but would last all of one. 
They had no sooner entered the woods, starting along the church road northeast of the Shirley house, than Roberta turned the mare left with a sharp yank on the lead-lines. Loving to surprise, she couldn't resist cutting her mean sloe eyes at the two girls grappling at the bench on her right to keep from being thrown overboard. Myrtle bushes the size of young oaks tried to scrub the three women from the wagon. The buckboard dipped into a firebreak, clinking the glass jars like bags of marbles; then bucked up and over the hillock of dirt and a sudden clearing of blowing broom-sage already taking on wheat tones but still turquoise at the roots. Up ahead was a cypress slew, flat-topped cypresses with a single rabbit hawk like a watch-guard perched atop the tallest branch. Behind, Merry Gay expected to see the trunks in the ditch and the jars of vegetables churned to soup. But except for some shifting of the trunks and a sprinkling of tiny lavender waxberries nothing had changed. 
After being nearly thrown from the buckboard twice when the iron wheels had sunk through tunnels of gopher holes, while Roberta drove the mare hard along the westward curve of the woods-line, Merry Gay and Tommy were braced for the railroad tracks happening up out of the tall pine woods. Soon, they were in a crop of pines with glazy orange cat-faces; spotted about were the weather shelters, lean-tos of two-by-four slats, then bushes again. The rain seemed to be coming heavier but it was only heavier from the closure of shook vines and bushes. 
Tommy spied the tiny tilted cabin first and sat up.
Negro women, children and men poured out the door, running toward the black creek turning though the woods with the shrilling of tree frogs and katydids. The clean earthy smell of decaying leaves and tart pine claimed the hot rubber smell of the ponchos and the mare's sour wet hide. 
Roberta stopped the wagon, shouting through cupped hands. "We brung food. Ain't nobody here gone mess with you." She nodded for Tommy to speak.
Tommy stood in the buckboard taking off her hat and releasing her mass of glittery wet hair. Her skin looked whiter next to Roberta, whose complexion was always jaundiced yellow. "It's Tommy. Me and Merry Gay Rose and Travis Miller's wife Roberta be the only ones on this wagon." Her tone was thicker using her across-the-tracks talk. 
Merry Gay wished she hadn't added the last name because Roberta and Travis were known by the Negroes for looking down on them for being lazy-living easy and close to the dirt, as Tommy called it, and Merry Gay knew exactly what she meant.
She stood with Tommy and called out, "We'll just put out this food here and leave and y'all can eat something." They had to be hungry.
Merry Gay and Tommy climbed over the bench to the bed of the buckboard and began unloading the crates of jarred vegetables, passing a jar at a time down to Roberta. She shook her head, grinning, placing the jars inside the crate again, then slid the crate to the edge of the buckboard, lifted it off with those powerful arms and, stepping solid side to side, waddled toward the rotting one-room cabin. Her dark blond hair under her hat was stuck to her neck, narrow green eyes darting over the rain-darkened woods and the dark people they could pick out only when they moved or slapped at mosquitoes.
On the way out, in the open field of broom-sage, Merry Gay asked Roberta about the cabin. Who it belonged to, how she knew it was there?
"Belonged to the old man, Travis's daddy. He used to go there of a winter to hunt and be by hisself." She grinned, showing a yellowed front tooth, and whipped the plug mare into a smart trot to make it to the cemetery before dark. 
Merry Gay could tell by Roberta's grin that something was coming up to shock two innocent girls. She nudged Tommy in the ribs with her elbow, but Tommy was staring ahead, hat low over her eyes. Okay, so Tommy was married; she was still as chaste as Merry Gay. Never told what went on with her husband at night, and Merry Gay had been relieved but disappointed too. Roberta, on the other hand, was not much older than them but had been raised hard, as she said. She was born looking back where she'd come from and figuring how she got there. 
Looking behind her at the jostling trunks, covered in wet tarps, raindrops pecking and bouncing off them like glass beads, Merry Gay decided not to listen, if she could help it. Out of respect and shame for her dead mother scrunched inside the trunk, she wouldn't listen.
Roberta whipped up the mare, making sucking noises through her teeth. Squirrel sounds. Terrible grimace! "That cabin back yonder, girls, that's where Jay Shirley goes to do his mischief. And mind you I ain't talking about just gambling and drinking or playing with his privates." Her eyes were almost walled with eagerness to get to the good part that would make Tommy and Merry Gay gasp like Sunday-school children. 
"What he'd do was, he'd pick up a girl, or two at the time, they say... He'd pick 'em up from the quarters and take 'em up through the woods the backway to get to the cabin. Couldn't go by his daddy's house, see."
The mare had slowed, shying at a gopher hole surrounded by white sand that didn't look like it could have been dredged from beneath the dark forest floor. Roberta seemed not to notice either. Hard glint to her green slit eyes, she went on. 'Now I want y'all to know, this is just hear-tell, but I got it straight from the horse's mouth, so to speak. He'd get them ole gals in the cabin and make em strip down and put on a show for him. Bad stuff!" She spat off the side of the wagon for emphasis. "Then again, they say, he'd make em take a strop to him. You know whip him and him buck-naked." She checked the girls' faces for degrees of shock: Tommy was still staring ahead, hands tucked under her brown poncho. But Merry Gay's mouth was open wide and she was gazing Roberta square in the face, owning up to her shock or shame or both over such talk under such circumstances.
"Now. Like I say, he'd be naked as a jaybird, while they beat him and what-all; I won't go into that, not now," she said low and glanced behind, then whipped the mare up again. Wet bushes scraped the straddle of the buckboard and now and then a mud hole caused the cautious mare to balk. "I will say this though?" At that point she cackled out, so loud she could be heard all over the woods. "?I'd of liked to had a hand in that whipping, wouldn't y'all?"
At one deep muddy spreading puddle, the mare stopped. Punchline! "They say he choked a colored girl by the creek there a while back, choked her to death while he banged it to her."
Merry Gay made a croaking sound. Roberta held out her right hand and turned her head away. "Now, I don't know that for a fact, but I could tell you her name, and y'all-you, Tommy, for shore-would recollect ain't nobody seen hide nor hair of Lettie Mae Cobb in going on a month now. So, don't go telling it around. Not and my name in it." 
The mare took a chance, rocking and plunging on through the mud hole; buckboard bogging up to its axles, but then freed with a fierce lunging of the mare, water and mud flying from her hooves.
Tommy stared straight ahead, even being slung side to side into Roberta and then Merry Gay. Merry Gay looked behind at her mama's coffin, expecting her to be sitting on top of the lid shaking her head.

***

When the winter fur was on the coon, beaver and otter, Hoss would go out into the cold humming woods with his steel traps and set them in the mouth of branch runs, hooked to tussocks in the brown water streaming over scalloped sand. Early mornings he would go back to the traps, re-baiting the tripped ones with rotted fish heads and entrails, and collect his catch to be skinned for their hides. Nights, he would hunt possum with his .22 rifle and sell them in the Negro quarters for roast possum and taters.
	A man at fourteen, he would carry the possums with their hard bald tails curled around his hands, peddling them in the quarters for whatever they would give him. So cold and it night and the dogs barking way off, his face and hands would feel on-fire with cold. Smell of wood-smoke and pine tang on the air. 
On one such starry moonlit night, he walked up on a man and a girl in the middle of the road. Her back was to the man and her dress-tail was hiked. She looked merely bent, made of sticks. The man behind her was peering up at the moon, humping and moaning, holding her by the hips as if fearing he would lose her, as if his life depended on his rooting into her, on his reaching some end to whatever he had started and after he was done his life would be all better. Like somebody hungry, eating to get full so that he would never be hungry again. At once urgent and dreamlike, they stopped moving when they saw Hoss with his possums stopped in the road, watching, warming in the couple's lust.
"How do," the man said.
The woman only straightened her spine a bit, eyeing him with something not shame. A question. It was the same question in the eyes of the trapped animals-What will you do with me now? 
The possum in Hoss's right hand came to life swinging round into a fat tight furry ball and biting his thumb to the quick with its needle teeth. He slung it to the ground; it sulled again. Then he stomped its skull with his boot heel and picked it up again by its hard curled tail and walked off, passion spent and in its place, pain. He had just learned a lesson, he later told Bernice, but he'd be hard-put to say what it was.
"Make sure a possum's dead," she said and laughed.
Of course Mary Gay had overheard her daddy telling this, to her mama, a lover's sharing not meant for third-party ears. But she had heard and now she was glad she had heard. Of all things little and big that he had done in her lifetime alone, it was something before she was born that had helped shape him into the Great Hoss Rose: shame for those who couldn't feel shame and pity for the pitiable, a heart that could break, and why Jesus wept. It was his insight into the man's weakness and his hope for the woman's strength. But more than all that, what made Merry Gay's daddy great in her eyes was the humility never to have considered himself above either of them.

***

Around the bend in the woods, the road to the church cemetery was rutted and trenched with fresh wheel tracks. Roberta had to pull over for a buggy meeting them to pass. A woman and a man with their heads hung.
Another and another, mostly women, then the black surrey they recognized as belonging to the widow woman on row two. But a young man was driving.
"Who's that driving her surrey?" Tommy asked.
"Her nephew, I think." Roberta looked back, and then whipped the lead-line to start the stubborn mule again.
The next wagon they met was driven by The Other Twin. Miss Ellie sitting on the other side with her face buried in a white handkerchief. All agreed that the two women must have buried their boy and bowed their heads in silent prayer. "Aye Lordy!" Roberta said and drove on. "Hear tell that boy was in and out, all over Moniac, last night. Toting people out of burning houses and them for once glad to see him."
Merry Gay and Tommy squeezed each other's hands. 
"Course, way I hear it, you girls would know all about that."
Merry Gay and Tommy kept quiet. 
Roberta didn't. "Hear tell, he saved y'all'es hide at the commissary last night. Right?" Roberta waited for them to speak, then, "Way Anse told it, Shirley would have shot him dead then, hadn't been for the fact of y'all raring up and him having to shoot y'all dead too. Had, Shirley figured, he'd never get the straight of what come of Priss."
"He was right about that," Merry Gay said.
"So, you know?"
"I know."
"But you ain't talking, right?"
"Not yet."
Two more wagons were pulling away from the cemetery when the buckboard cleared the final blind of trees: a high white church with a steeple and live oaks so tall and branched out they formed a heavenly canopy over the church and cemetery.
All three women gasped. Before them, behind the white picket fence of the cemetery, were numerous new raw graves, mounds of white clay melting down in the rain.
"I thought everybody was staying in because they were scared," Merry Gay said. "Maybe some of these are the men from down the tracks that got killed on the train. Probably brought home to their families to put away decent."
Roberta was staring out, green slit eyes squinting and thin lips moving as she tallied up the graves. "Twenty-two all told! And this ain't the end of it. Lord, what'll it take to stop it?" 
"Not even counting my people," Tommy spoke up.
The other two women cast sidelong glances at Tommy. Her dark liquid eyes were staring out at the rain like fog falling on the woods.
"I'd like you to tell me those boys last night didn't end up drug out in the woods for the buzzards to eat," Tommy added.
 Neither Roberta nor Merry Gay spoke because they couldn't deny that was likely the way of it.

CHAPTER 10

By nightfall the women had gotten word that the State Militia would be coming to Moniac Crossing by train sometime during the night or early morning. They would keep the peace while the sheriff got some straight answers and made his arrests. Nate Shirley demanded that the sheriff call in the U.S. Regulars instead, because so many in the Militia were local men and kin to somebody in Moniac. 
Merry Gay thought it ironic that Nate Shirley would soon be trying to sort enemy from friend, much as she and Tommy had done. She suspected that the sheriff's men were camping out in the woods or holed up at one of the many portable sawmill sites around. Wherever they were, this half-hearted posse, Buck and Charlie and Ruis were there. Had to be. Word from Travis to Roberta to the two girls was Shirley and Wendell and their men were out to gun down the Rose boys and Ruis for directly or indirectly shooting dead his favorite granddaughter. Her body had been found in the dining car lavatory where he'd last seen the Rose boys and Ruis flee to. The Rose girl had seen it all, had been right there when they shot Priss, and not only didn't try to save her but lied to Shirley concerning her whereabouts. Why would she lie except to save her brothers? Shirley would have some answers or he would have some hide, he said.
Whipped beyond words from all the activity that day, and really since Sunday, Merry Gay and Tommy moved the feather mattress from on top of the cotton mattress on the bed in the front room they once shared and laid it on the floor between the bed and the wall. The point was to be where they weren't supposed to be if Shirley or one of his men came calling, though they imagined Shirley would expect them to be at Roberta's or somewhere for protection, and probably Bernice's house would be the last place he would look. Also, their little soft corner provided a bunker of sorts-all Merry Gay had to do was shoot square across the other mattress on the bed to the door, surprising their intruder who they had no picture for in their heads. He could be anybody; he could be a she.
Fully dressed but in fresh clothes, cozy inside and not out in the squalling wind and rain, the girls blew out the candle and lay in the dark, bolstered and propped on numerous feather pillows and quilts. Merry Gay next to the wall and Tommy next to the bed. Smoky wax smell and a faint oily scent of the geese they had robbed the down from, birds held between their knees and plucked till naked and chill-bumped; no mercy shown by Bernice to the girls, so they'd shown none to the geese, though later they would pity the bulky birds parading about the yard, shorn of their glory like somebody scalped. It was like one of their stay-up parties when they were girls with somebody older watching over them. They talked about that-the good old days-then even laughed because they had been so young then, acting old, amazed that they could still laugh under such dangerous circumstances. 
"I wish we'd treated Samson more human," Merry Gay said low.
"Me too."
"Do you feel like you got to know him better in that short time last night than some other people here at the Crossing?" Merry Gay made a hissing sound. "I mean, most of these other people we thought we'd known all their lives. All our lives..." 
"Guess it just goes to show."
What seemed really familiar, really nostalgic, was the swoon of sleep in the regular pecking rhythm of rain on tin, cozy, dark, protected by somebody else, except for the fact of the cold pistol in Merry Gay's grip and their straining to listen for gaps in the rain-footsteps along the hall, the door squeaking open. Eyes open it was as dark as with eyes closed. Too tired to talk, too weary to sleep, they talked anyway. Low to keep from being heard, low to keep from exerting energy. It felt good not to have to move, not to have to see, only to breathe in the close air of somebody you loved and say whatever felt right. Sleeping with somebody, you get to know him or her inside-out.
"Okay," Merry Gay said, "go ahead and tell me. Was that true about Jay? I know you know."
"I don't know." Tommy rolled onto her back bunching a pillow under her head. "What I do know is about as bad or worse and a lot more apt to get me killed."
"What?" Merry Gay sat up, eyes stretched trying to make her sister out in the dark.
"I've never told anybody but Mama. I was scared to. Will would have made me quit working there and I'd of wound up dead for sure then. Maybe I should have?I was scared to. Scared of how it would look if I did quit."
"How it would look to who? What? Mama would have told me." But Merry Gay knew better, knew she'd been spared the sexual secrets whispered behind married women's hands.
"Mama wouldn't have told God this."
"Maybe I don't want to know." Merry Gay lay down. "If it's something I don't need to know yet, something I can't handle yet?"
"You never will need to know this, but I'll tell you. Only I hate putting dirty thoughts in your head, such evil."
Then she told it. And even while she was telling it, Merry Gay knew the story would scar her mind forever, that she'd have been better off not hearing it after all.
Tommy was leaving through the back door of the Shirley house, one morning that past fall, to pick up the mail from the commissary. Too, Miss Moxie was expecting a package coming Southern Express on the morning train, by her calculation. If the package didn't arrive this morning, it would be the fault of the Express, the train, or the company through which she had ordered. Her timing was faultless and it was Tommy's job to see to it that the branch or individual guilty of disrupting Miss Moxie's timetable be brought low and humbled. Next order, she would use Wells Fargo for spite.
Just as Tommy started out, she heard a knock on the front door and started in again to answer it.
Nathan Shirley was drinking coffee and reading the newspaper at the kitchen table and he told Tommy to go on, he would get the door. The women and children were still asleep in the south and north wings of the house, all except for Priss who was at the house of her tutor.
Rather than go around the south side of the house and through the picket gate there, Tommy decided to go around the north side, pick a tea olive bloom from the tree in the corner of the front yard to take with her to the commissary.
She was on the far side of the tree, hidden from the front of the house, when she heard Nate Shirley talking to another man standing barely inside the open door. Looked to Tommy like Deputy Threet's horse, a snuff-brown mare, tied to the gate out front, stamping its hooves and switching at flies. No frost yet, and Tommy looked forward to it, because the frost would kill the flies, but it would also kill back the tea olives. 
She cropped a branch with lots of tiny white star-shaped blossoms and crisp green leaves then ducking low under the branches she started out. But she stopped when she realized the two men had stepped out on the porch, still talking low. It was the way they were talking that stopped her, she said. Low, just-between-us, buddy-buddy: Deputy Threet playing up to the big man, as usual; the big man going along only because he must. But whatever Threet was saying-and she couldn't tell at the time from where she stood-she said Nathan Shirley was all ears but appeared eager to be rid of Threet. Even walked with his arm on the shoulder of the pussel-gutted, grinning deputy down the brick walk to the gate and his horse tied there-Threet's first mistake that morning.
 Nobody tied a horse to that gate. Nobody. Once one of the turpentine hands had done it and the horse jerked the gate down and went trotting along the lane with it dragging. Next morning the man was gone.
At the gate, the men were still talking low, but from the tea olive tree Tommy could hear most of what they were saying; the rest she would fill in and be proved right by Nate Shirley's reaction.
The gist of it was that some poor old broke farmer, whose name Tommy recognized, had caught Jay Shirley the night before in his kitchen with his pants down. Tommy strained to listen, curious as to why Jay Shirley would have his pants down, why he would even be in this man's kitchen in the middle of the night. 
Then she heard Deputy Threet-the horse was snatching at the reins, yanking on the gate, shaking and rattling the pickets of the white fence all the way down to the tea olive tree-his mewling voice, repeating as he was prone to do: "Like I say, I shore hate to be the one to bring you the news, Nate old boy, but you can see how the old man would be all up in the air. Walking in like that and finding Jay standing with his britches down around his ankles. Had her legs spread out and her laying a corpse and him pumping it to her."
Nate Shirley slid his wallet from his hip pocket. He still had on his tan leather slides but was dressed in black trousers and one of his Chinese-ironed blue broadcloth shirts.
"Ain't the first time this is come up, you know," said Threet. "Wadn't for me somebody would shoot that boy of yours."
Nate handed him some cash and flipped the latch on the gate for him to step through, untied the horse's reins and handed them to Threet. "Half that money's for you. Give the other half to the old man and tell him I hate it about his wife," Nate said. "Tell him I'll get with him in a day or two if he's not satisfied."
Threet was standing next to the sidling, spooked horse, counting the bills in his hand. "Two hunderd dollars!" He laughed loud. "Listen! He can have my wife and her alive for less."
Tommy was standing still as death as well when a yellow cat dropped down through the branches from the top of the tree, shaking it like a storm, and landed with claws stuck in the bark at the main fork of limbs before Tommy's face.
Like the cat, she couldn't move. She closed her eyes. She prayed she hadn't been seen. But when she opened her eyes she saw Nathan Shirley standing on the other side of the tree, his face where the cat had been last time she looked.
"You make a habit of eavesdropping, Tommy?" He didn't sound mad but his face was red, even his scrubbed ears were red. Every morning he had to have hot water for a bath.
"I didn't?I." She held out the branch of tea olives as if offering it for him to smell.
He reached through the fork and took it, dropped it on the ground. A neat round bed of red ants was working alive at his feet and she kept her eyes on it, his bare feet in those slippers, hoping the ants would crawl up his pants legs, distracting him, and she would be spared.
"No sir, I didn't hear nothing." She wanted to lean into the tree trunk for support but she would be too close and the flowers smelled too clean and sweet.
"Tell me what you did hear, and don't lie. I don't like a lie."
"I don't know what I heard? I couldn't hear."
"I know you did or you would've come on out." Holding to a branch, he swung around the tree, easy in those soft leather slides, right in her face and speaking low as a lover. "Okay, I'll grant you, you weren't eavesdropping. You were picking flowers"-he reached up and plucked a single bloom close and held it under her nose-"and you got scared to come out."
He twiddled the flower, tickling her nose. He was smiling. She stepped away.
"Have I ever been mean to you?"
"No sir."
"Have I ever hurt you?"
"No sir, never have, sir."
"Well, I don't aim to now."
"Yes sir."
"Know why?"
"No sir."
"I like your coffee. I like you, Tommy."
She was terrified, feet itching to run. He was blocking her on one side of the tree and could hem her up between the tree and the fence on the other side. She could outrun him in those slides.
"I need to go get the mail, sir," she said. "Train's done come."
He grabbed her face then, striking like a snake and squeezing her jaw. She could feel, see, her cheeks pressed up. She caught her breath, held it in case it was her last.
Tommy stopped talking to Merry Gay and only lay there. The room felt steamy, stifling, from Tommy's heavy breathing maybe. The only thing surprising her was that he'd let Tommy stay around so long. That he hadn't killed her. He couldn't fire her for fear she would talk. 

Merry Gay, why would Jay make Muffin take cold baths at night?
Why, I?I don't know. 
I mean even when it's cold, real cold, he makes her. Have you ever heard of that?
Priss, that's probably something you're not supposed to talk about. It's nothing but grownup stuff, I imagine.
Something else I wonder about. I wonder how come he has all those wigs and lady's silk underclothes in that trunk in his closet.
Priss, you know better than to mess around in people's stuff.
I didn't. Gramma Moxie did. She was looking for her fur muff and thought Muffin stole it from her and went in their room nosing around.
And she told you this? Miss Moxie told you?
Not exactly. She came out of their room and right in front of the whole family at the breakfast table she went to screaming, White trash! White trash! and throwing stuff up in the air. Even a blond wig looked like a dead lady's head.
You're making that up, Priss. I know you are."
Quit laughing. Go ask Tommy. She came in right after that and heard Gramma Moxie squalling, My own grandson! My own grandson! I would've expected it of Wendell but not Jay. Granddaddy was kicking the clothes and the wig down the hall and shouting for Gramma to shut up or he'd send her to Sister's. She hates Aunt Sister because she's got all those old dogs and lives Upnorth. 
Tommy saw all this? She knows all this?
Not about the baths I don't think. But anyway, Gramma Moxie took to her sick bed and all that day she was down with a headache, crying low to keep Granddaddy from hearing and sending her to Aunt Sister. Tommy was back and to with cold compresses for her head. What was really really strange was the way Muffin jumped up from the table when Jay said it was her stuff in the trunk. She said, You've done it now, you've gone too far. 
"What did Nate Shirley say then?" Merry Gay asked Tommy.
"He'd said it all, he'd said enough. He just walked off across the grass to the porch and went inside." Tommy stiffened as if to demonstrate how stiff she was, scared stiff, as she would say. "I went straight to Mama's. I told her what happened. She told me to quit work. 'Quit now,' she said. Then she said, 'Don't. On second thought, I want you to go on over to the commissary like you started. Go on!'
