Right after that, I mean right after dinner, she was visiting with Miss Moxie, sitting on the porch and getting talked to. Acted like wadn't a thing in the world the matter. I mean even when she looked at me, she made like she was happy as could be.
"Well, that crazy old lady Moxie took a shine to Mama. Wasn't nobody else to talk to and Mama was as good a listener as she was an actor. I swear! Everybody in the house was glad to see her coming. Mr. Nate taught her how to iron his shirts cause she acted like she couldn't stand not trying it at least once. 
"Come end of the week and she didn't show up one day, and I figured she'd had enough of Miss Moxie and maybe taken a liking to Nate Shirley and thought I'd heard him wrong, that I could take care of myself. I could flat run and she knew that too. 
"Next day the women were after Mr. Nate to go get Bernice. `Pay her whatever she wants,' Sara said. Miss Moxie was half the time under their feet, bossing them around and talking her stuff. She would even talk to me while I worked. Never could catch on if she was insulting somebody. Told me I'd make a good mistress to one of them rich men in New Orleans, me being `high-yellow' and 'right attractive.'" Tommy laughed. 
"What did you tell her?"
"I said I hadn't planned on ever doing that kind of thing, besides I just got married. She hummed something back. Then she said, 'Oh, sugar plum, but it's not like being a whor~e.' She dropped it in a whisper about two or three times before I figured out she'd read all this in one of her old books."
"I don't get it."
"See, Merry Gay, she'd only ever read the word, had been so shut up inside with all the ladies, she had never even heard anybody say the word 'whore.'"

***

Merry Gay was almost asleep when she realized that Tommy was still awake, lying there scared probably or thinking about Will. She too had been dozing then startling herself awake with drifting thoughts before sleep of her mama being dead, really dead, and she would see her no more. A chink in the wall near the window was puffing air from outside, rain bolting from the side of the house, then back.
"Tommy," Merry Gay said, "you can talk to me about Will if you want to. Him or Mama either one. I mean I'll listen and not tell you to not cry or say they're better off than we are, in a better place."
She laughed, actually laughed. "I hadn't thought about that till you said that. How that's what everybody says when somebody dies."
"I guess they don't know what else to say but it does look like they'd change up from time to time, doesn't it?"
Both were laughing, the low tee-heeing of kids out in the night. Or at church. Both believing against all reason that it was so, what everybody said. Bernice and Will were in a better place if only asleep and not asleep in Jesus. But both had been born again, both saved. 
"You know what else they say, people who lose their husbands or wives?" Tommy asked. "They say I feel like part of me died. Well, that's the truth. I loved Will's funning ways and I loved his body. I loved sleeping next to him and waking up the next morning. Smelling his sleep-warmed skin, seeing his great brown back when I opened my eyes."
"Tommy, we didn't do you any favors by keeping you on this side of the tracks, did we?"
"It's not that. It's just that after I married, after I moved in with Will, I was living the life I'd been born to live. And it was different?not the same as what we could see from here, this side of the tracks."
"Mama knew that, don't you think?"
"I know she did. But I'm thankful she raised me good. All my family scattered from here to yonder, where would I have gone? What would have come of me?"
"I don't know. Maybe the same, I mean, you'd have fit in over there, and there's some good old grannies and aunties that make a life of taking care of the children."
"It was confusing, growing up that way. That's all I can say."
They quit talking and listened to the wind flapping a loose corner of tin on the north edge of the front porch. To the wind howling and the rain squalling suddenly, its rhythm spent. 
Merry Gay broke the silence with a question. She had to know. "Tommy, why didn't you go with us on the train to St. Augustine?"
"I couldn't chance some stranger seeing me and Will together and figuring me for a white woman running with a nigger man. Or other way around. Besides?" She sat straight up as if seeing a light in the dark room. Then she laughed. "I didn't go because I was afraid trouble would break out if they made me ride in the colored coach or smoking car."
She lay again and covered her face with her pillow, muffling laughter. Maybe crying. Merry Gay was crying. She placed her left hand on Tommy's narrow quivering ribcage, beneath her small breasts, and kept it there till her heart formed its beat out of the quivering.
"Not Mama and Will, not both of them gone," Tommy said.
 They lay there so long and still that each thought the other might be sleeping, so long that the soft mattress grew lumpy with clumped feathers. Merry Gay rolled to her side, facing Tommy, though she couldn't see her. "Do you think it's over?" she asked low, testing.
"I'll feel a whole lot easier once the Militia gets here. But I bet you anything, nobody'll talk even with the Militia to back up the sheriff from Folkston."
"Yeah. I wonder if Aunt Leoti's boy Sam is still in the Militia."
 "I hope he's not looking for us to feed and do for him if he stops by."
"That's the very reason Nate Shirley insisted on the Regulars."
"Do you think Buck and Charlie will be arrested?"
"Maybe. I can't quite feature them not busting out of jail though."
"What about a woman? Can they arrest you just because of what happened to Priss?"
"I've already thought about it and it all depends on how much Shirley's word has to do with it."
"You plan to tell the whole story about him shooting Priss in the lavatory if it comes down to that?"
"If I have to, only if I have to." Merry Gay lay down again. "I'll probably have to, yes."
"Can I ask you something else?" Tommy asked.
"What?"
"Why didn't you sing over Mama's grave with me and Roberta this evening?"
"Maybe I wanted to hear your voices. Maybe there's no song left in me now."

***

Merry Gay woke to flickering light of torches on the facing walls and window, frantic shouts and horse hooves clopping, the musical rales of the wind and the rain batting at a tilt on the window glass next to the spot where she lay with her pistol dropped by her side.
She had been dreaming that she was singing, simply singing, no particular situation, no setting for the dream, but only the sweet peace of being, that feeling of only living with nothing expected of you, nothing to run from, nothing to run to, no time: that heavenly stasis called childhood.
"It's not over," she said, not moving.
"What time do you think it is?" Tommy asked. "How long did we sleep?"
Merry Gay didn't answer because she couldn't even make a guess. She felt heavy and light at the same time. She sat up, then saw Tommy in silhouette kneeling on the cotton mattress of the bed at the window over the porch and crawled over to kneel beside her. Outside she could see horsemen with torches, dark furtive figures spiriting along the lane and across the tracks toward the commissary. No circle of lanterns south along the tracks that she could see, meaning the dead had been buried and the wounded had been moved. She wondered where. She felt as cut off from her own people as Tommy did from hers.
"What do you think they're up to?" Tommy asked.
"To no good, that's for sure."
"Do you hear a train?"
"No. It's just the wind. Maybe a twister sent by God to suck them up."
"Is that what it'll take to stop it?"
The room felt damp and warm, rank with their sleepy breath. Not quite foul but close.
"I still think maybe I hear a train?the Militia coming. Or maybe we slept right through the train bringing them earlier."
One shot, two shots, three shots, four sounding far away in the wind, rain and rumble.
Three men on horses with torches passed, one turning his hatted head toward the house and the two girls at the window. They ducked, even knowing they couldn't possible be seen in the dark room.
"Shirley and his boys," Merry Gay breathed, though the thought was only instinct and fear. What if the older landowners, the ones standing up to Shirley, were arrested and she and the other women were left alone to fear Shirley, to serve him? Like her mama breaking in Miss Moxie's torturous shoes. More shots.
"I don't know about you," Merry Gay said, "but I'd as soon be out there in the storm as holed up in here not knowing what's going on."
"You're not scared, are you?" Tommy was shaking the bed.
"Being scared hasn't killed me yet. I've been scarder."
"You mean in that smoking car Sunday coming home?"
"Yes." Really Merry Gay was terrified to go out. And too, all of a sudden, she couldn't imagine that Shirley would let Tommy go unpunished for shooting in his yard on Sunday night. A public spitting in a white man's face. She thought about his big-shot friends on the yacht; even if Shirley were arrested, he would be set free. She had little faith in the Militia. She didn't want Tommy, who was doomed, to know that though. She had to hold on to something and ironically it was the symbolic east side of the tracks, her white side.
"Let's go put on our ponchos and hats and head out." Merry Gay walked on her knees across the bed, stepping to the cold damp floor and feeling for the door. Unlatching and sucking in her breath. Anybody could be waiting in the dark hall. Anybody could grab them. Merry Gay held the pistol out, her finger on the trigger, stepping light on the creaking floorboards. Tommy held to her waist, stepping in her tracks.
On the un-ceiled back porch the rain on the tin was deafening. They put on their boots, ponchos and hats. Firelight from the commissary lit the slanting rain between the house north of them and their own.
Again they crept in the shadows of back yards, shrubs and trees and houses, blinking against the rain and the firelight, mesmerizing sounds and flickering. In the dark, in the rain, the smells of animals and the lime of the outhouses smelled sharper, the ashes so bitter the smell burned their eyes
At the slippery gumbo crossroad running by the commissary and forking off at the rows of houses, they crossed over, creeping through the woods and bushes toward the torch and campfires and shouts, even laughter. 
"At least the two mobs aren't warring," Tommy said. "What you reckon?"
"Sounds like the Fourth of July to me." They were walking into the enemy this time; no friend to rescue them if they got caught. "Stay out of the light, Tommy, and don't even breathe."
But the rain drumming on the tin roofs and ticking on the leaves of the trees was so loud they couldn't be heard if they yelled. 
From the cover of vines and trees they stood on the south end of the commissary, peering out. Only Shirley's men as far as they could tell-a royal 25 or so. What looked like three croaker sacks were hanging from a low stout branch of the white oak, stuffed with something alive, hogs maybe being smoked over the rain-stung fire, and men gathered round, shooting up into the already ruptured sky. Like a celebration. 
"Shoot me, shoot me, mister." The voice sounded muffled, seemed to come from the tree.
"What is that?" Tommy asked. "Can you tell?"
"No. The wind maybe. Let's get closer. Could be they're celebrating the Militia coming after all. Shooting to be shooting."
Merry Gay led off through the wet slapping vines and bushes, toward the rear of the commissary and a cattle trail often used by the Negroes coming in from the woods for food and drink. Smells of wood-smoke, wet greenery and raw mud. She was still holding to the pistol but no longer with a finger on the trigger. Not so afraid of Shirley and his boys as eager to confirm their mood of celebration. She could sense Tommy was feeling the same. It was almost an adventure except for the soreness following the ache of the Sunday killing spree.
Coming up on the northwest corner of the boxy white commissary, parting wet myrtle and gall-berry bushes in order to watch the men gathered around the front, they heard somebody yell, "Hey, you hear that? Sounds like they's some more niggers up there."
Deputy Threet, clear as day, ran toward the outside stairs, not twenty feet from where the girls were standing, and bounded up with his shotgun raised. The top story of the commissary seemed to explode with gunfire; Deputy Threet's shotgun dropped to the ground by the stairs, and he tumbled headlong down the stairs clutching his chest, and splashed into a puddle of water. A shield of water seemed to hang there, isolating him, till it settled with the settling of his solid round body. 
The men around the white oak rushed to him, then ran up the stairs single file firing into the dark and the fireworks of gunfire from the top story. The American flag hanging from the balcony snapped and popped in the wind. 
Nate Shirley sat his horse under the smoking tree, watching; arm still in a sling, alone but for Wendell Shirley riding up on another horse, stopping short next to his daddy. Merry Gay hadn't realized how she'd dreaded facing Wendell, telling him how Priss had died. What perfect revenge! What opportunity now with the two of them together and their own men witness to her announcement.   
The eerie howls no longer sounded like wind but human screams. The hung croaker sacks swung wildly, bumping each other. A musky, wild, gamy odor of roast flesh and singed hairy burlap tainted the clean rain and sweet burning wood smells.
"They're smoking somebody," Tommy said out loud, "in the croaker sacks."
Merry Gay had heard of that. She was stricken mute with terror that it could be Buck and Charlie and Ruis.
All was quiet upstairs, the shots fading out over the woods, and only the wind and the snapping and popping of the flag and the screams from the sacks remained. The men bounded down the stairs, stepping over Deputy Threet's body.
"We got em," one said. "They was holed up in there. Storm flushed em in out of the woods, whole bunch of em. Starving I reckon, here to rob the commissary like them boys sacked up in the tree there."
 "Guess our little lesson here was wasted on them after all," Nate Shirley said. "Y'all get Deputy Threet's body and take him over there to his house. Put him on the front porch and say your sorrys to the wife then come on back to the barbecue." He laughed, shining his gritted teeth as if in pain, or misery from the cold wet and strain of all that had happened and still might. Or maybe he was deranged.
Recovering, he raised his pistol to eye level. Then squinting through the sight, he shot left-handed the American flag from the roof to the ground at the foot of the stairs.
One of his boys said, "If that don't beat all!"
All agreed, laughing.
"Shoot me, shoot me, mister!" someone, a young boy, sounded like, yelled from one of the hanging sacks.
The men placed their rifles and shotguns on the ground, forming a litter, then lifted Deputy Threet onto it and began bearing his body away toward the tracks. 
"I hate to say it," one said anyway, "but I couldn't of looked the county over and found nobody I'd ruther it be than Threet."
The rain on the fire under the tree, under the sacks, produced braids of smoke and steam mixed and slowly rising. Glister of firelight on the wet magnolias in the background of woods. Two of the croaker sacks still bumped and swung furiously, erratically. Elbows, knees, heads, feet and rumps protruded from the smoking burlap, screams issuing forth as from hell itself. The other sack only swayed in the wind, throwing smoke. 
The men who had carried Deputy Threet away were crossing the tracks, coming back with their guns. The horses gyred and snorted, and Nate Shirley shifted in his saddle, facing Wendell and muttering something. Wendell nodded and reset his hat. 
"Ain't no picnic, all these doings, boys, and don't y'all forget it," Nate shouted. "Let this be a lesson-you get out of hand and you pay for it the hard way."
Panther-like screams and thrashing in one of the hanging sacks caused the rope gathering the top and knotted over the limb to wind and unwind like a tree swing.
Suddenly, coming alive from her stunned state, Tommy dashed from the bushes to the stairwell of the commissary, picked up Threet's shotgun and braced the stock against her right shoulder, sidling along the shadows of the wall with the barrel leading the way.
"No, Tommy, no." Merry Gay called low. "Don't. They'll kill you." She felt frozen in the wet bushes, trapped. Hypnotized. So much time to do something and she did nothing but call out no, no, no. Later, gauging the actions and moments, she knew there hadn't been time.
Tommy got off one bosh shot, aiming for the sacks in the tree, while running into the throng of men. Then she stumbled and fell face down before the Shirleys on their horses. Nate's chestnut gelding stepped back, front hooves together as if to begin bucking and stomping. 
Shirley shouted, "Hoo now!" leaning forward to see Tommy on the ground, on her face, peering up, not at him but at the sacks in the tree. "Shoot her, shoot her!" Nate Shirley shouted. "Shoot that nigger gal before she shoots me."
Merry Gay recalled in the melee the screams dying in the croaker sacks or the wind no longer howling and shots unnumbered but couldn't recall having stepped out from the bushes and running along the shot-pocked ground to where Tommy lay wheeling, propelled by her booted feet, twitching from the shots, vomiting and leaking blood through her brown eyelet poncho.
No sounds except the ringing in her ears, the cringing of her eardrums, Merry Gay dropped to the ground and caught Tommy's head and upper body in her arms, lifting her to her own chest and rocking as if to staunch her undignified spinning fit. 
"Stop it," Tommy gurgled through blood-so much red blood, not black and not white. "Stop it!" Tommy slapped at Merry Gay's face, at her breasts. Then she went limp, black eyes glaring in that broad white face.
Merry Gay laid her on the ground gently, stood and walked toward the stairs, expecting a shot in the back but beyond caring. She picked up the muddy red, white and blue flag with its splintered wood stake and began walking up the stairs. Walking in the gutted shadows of the forbidden bachelor quarters of the top story, rank with the smell of men and decorated with the lifeless bodies of men, and out to the balcony and the firelight.
Holding the flag high she stepped up to the wobbly wood banister, all eyes and guns below on her. She stood there a moment, begging her voice from a whisper deep inside, a target for real now, hair blowing in the wind like the flag. She could see Nate Shirley raise his left hand in the firelight to halt his men from shooting; Wendell beside him, on his horse, waiting for what he didn't know. She couldn't see Tommy from there; she tried not to.
 The parched bodies inside the smoking croaker sacks were still now but the sacks swayed like abandoned swings of children, or something mechanical out of synchrony. No, like what they were, sacks of parched bodies, so close she could smell their cooked flesh and excrement. 
She opened her mouth to speak, to tell about Priss, about Nate Shirley shooting her, but instead began singing, precious notes measured and rising from the leftover strains of the song on Sunday, gazing out at the clearing east sky of rose-streaked clouds and the long dawn.




EPILOGUE
The Militia maintained a semblance of order while the sheriff from Folkston conducted his investigation and made arrests. Jails in three Georgia counties-Charlton, Clinch and Ware-were filled, according to local records. Florida opened her boundary and permitted the sheriff, with National Guard reinforcement, to enter the Baxter community, barely across the state line from Moniac; there they captured twenty-one of the loyal Shirley men hiding out at the various houses and in the woods. Any state except Georgia they deemed safe territory apparently. The prisoners, under military guard, were taken by train to Jacksonville, Florida, for security. From the Bay Street Station they were marched to the Duval County jail under additional armed guard.
The Honorable Jackson Coffer of the U.S. Senate and C.C. Shert of the Florida State Senate launched a bail campaign for the men right away. Senator Coffer was vacationing in France at the time and his correspondence with Senator Shert provided much of the information for this feature on the massacre at Moniac Crossing. Also found among Senator Shert's letters and papers were several receipts for bail payments, lawyer fees, and family subsistence. None was ever repaid.
Senator Shert was joined by the influential St. Augustine merchant and legislator Roland Mason on a trip through the Florida and Georgia areas involved, advising against panic and cooperation. 
"Now, don't be witnessing against your neighbor just to get even," they reasoned. "Stay quiet and know nothing. Neither state will have a case against anybody if you all keep quiet."
And they did. The Charlton County Sheriff was heard to remark as he rounded up his suspects, "This ain't going to do nobody no good. They won't get anything out of them."
Buck and Charlie Rose and Mike Ruis were tried in Folkston on numerous charges, ironically none of which was homicide, though in the beginning Nathan Shirley threatened to have them hung for the shooting death of Priscilla Aden Shirley. The Roses had kin-people all over Charlton County and they showed up at the courthouse in Folkston on the day of the trial. All were armed and one nervous townsperson and county official was overhead to say, "If them Roses want to burn down the courthouse, I'll not only let'em I'll holp 'em strike the match."
 The Charlton County courthouse was a handsome frame structure. On court day, it was circled round by the Militia and hundreds of curious natives and out-of-towners. Nobody doubted for a moment that the relatives of the Moniac old-family men being tried would make good on their threats to burn the courthouse and shoot up the town if any were convicted. The trial was soon over and all were acquitted.
 A second trial for the murder of Tommy and Will Thomas, and the other Negroes killed at the commissary both nights, was of an even shorter duration: no determination could be made as to who fired the shots that killed them. The same was true of the boys rumored to have been smoked to death in the croaker sacks. Nobody saw a thing; nobody was talking. No bodies were found.
The tight-lipped attitudes of the witnesses frustrated the proceedings of justice, resulting in delays and re-scheduling. The new governor, it was rumored, owed much of his independent Democrat victory to certain strings skillfully pulled by Charlton and Ware county politicians. The Governor also had blood and in-law ties within the county. Perhaps, in view of those facts, it can be understood why he chose to help bog down what came to be known as the case of the Moniac Massacre in judicial loopholes and governmental red tape until it became a forgotten subject in Atlanta.
Mr. Nathan Shirley did not show up for the hearings and supposedly ended up near Madison, in North Georgia. Mr. Randolph Harrell came to Moniac Crossing from that area a decade later to set up a sawmill operation. Shirley strongly advised him not to go to Charlton County. "Those people there," he said, "they're crazy." 
Mr. Harrell answered, "Maybe, just maybe, it's how you treat people and approach them that makes the difference. You can't expect to become an insider with an outsider's mindset. The provinces of kings have long been built upon that simple wisdom."

***

Claude Miller steers his rust and blue pickup from the paved road onto the dusty dirt lane between the railroad tracks and the front row of houses on the west side of Moniac Crossing. On both sides of the tracks, pines have been limbed and treated for power poles, the tiered black wires an ideal place for the fat doves to perch. Crepe myrtles, between poles, are shouting, lush frilly pink blooms that look hot to the touch. Dried gourds for martin houses are strung up and crucified on crosses of wood, white against the blue sky. The ancient live oaks along the lane strike various poses, their trunks having been shaped by generations of wind and climbing children. Resurrection ferns growing along the branches, which are bigger than the average tree trunk, look dry as hay stubble from lack of rain. Up the tracks, veeing out to the piney woods, heat waves shimmer and almost allow for the ghost of a train, slow-moving, silent, at once heat-slunk and springing up on the numb turpentine camp at midday.
The wide commissary porch displays black and white turpentine hands, eating Vienna sausages, sardines, pickled eggs and pigs' feet, cheese and crackers. Dinnertime break from the turpentine woods. A faded American flag looks wilted atop its wooden pole on the roof peak of the second story. It's a sad fact that few people ever see the flag anymore, but that's the way of things inset on the atmosphere for so long and having little to do with the hard livelihood of the flatwoods. Georgia flag, Florida flag, they might notice, but being on the Bend, neither here nor there, the American flag will have to suffice-if that's the way the boss lady wants it-though it hardly seems to pertain. Politics, the law, history, geography, even reading and writing, are the responsibility and virtue of the educated man, opposite of the working man.
The burnt motor oil of Claude's pickup smells at odds with the cough-syrup odor of creosoted crossties, with the tangy resin in the barrels on back of a two-ton truck juddering past. Dust meeting dust as the gum truck brakes at the end of the lane, at the start of the paved road. But all else mingles in the heat like the present coming to pass, the before, now and after, the will-be and is ticking toward was, already history save for setting in print. There's a finger on the buzzer hum of locusts in the oaks across the tracks, and the crickets and grasshoppers click along the rail-bed borders of crisp sere grass parched daily by the hot wind of freight trains. Few people hear the insects, or the train, few people pay homage to the live oaks, older than the oldest of them.
One, Mrs. Randolph Harrell, stands in the double doorway of the dim commissary, on the hearth of daylight charged with chafe of cottonseed meal and dust. 
Claude Miller pulls his truck over into the shade of the commissary yard, broad leaves of the white oak cast in shadow on the swept dirt by the overhead sun. Getting out, he feels his reddish beard and what's left of his hair netting the dust furls of the truck's wake. His hair is the strange burnished beige color of a born redhead's gone gray.
The Negro hands are straggling from the porch, down the tall doorsteps-"How do?" They look weary but attuned to the proximity, the odd power, of an educated man on the place. But he is merely a little-town man, a simple man with simple needs. Why he can operate almost single-handed his own gutsy, gritty, money-glutting newspaper in Folkston.
Under the edge of the steps is an old wooden mallet bound in rusty bands of metal. And a piece off the mallet, a heavy, gritty concrete and sand tar cup, reminder of the metal shortage and the tar cup plant in Homerville during World War Two. Hard times then and turpentine exports almost came to a standstill. 
"Hot ain't it?" Claude says, standing aside while they step down. He can smell their musky sweat and the pine tar on their army green twills. It's a ripe odor he knows from having worked alongside them in the woods before college; he never smelled that rich, that rank, he was never quite the man. He'd spent about half his time growing up trying to lug buckets of gum without sounding winded. The other half he spent trying to attract women, till he figured, rightly so, that whatever inclination or drive he had was wasted on either.
Take for instance Merry Gay Rose, sixteen, seventeen years older than him. He had it all figured out at one point, or thought he did, how to make her love him. Then along came Mr. Harrell?and the rest is history as they say.
Merry Gay has aged into a large mannish woman with a kind, drooped face and short silver hair like a Christmas angel's, not the church-lady kind of do, but naturally wavy, neatly combed. Graying has a way of changing the texture of hair, but usually it changes coarse, curly hair, like Claude's, to straight and fine instead of the other way around, like Mrs. Randolph Merry Gay Rose Harrell's hair. He has seldom seen her without her uniform of boxy white pressed shirt and dark pants or skirt. She has never worn glasses; she has never worn a watch. Doesn't own a purse to her name. He has never seen her not-smiling, her small scalloped teeth ground down and yellowed only enough to be believably hers, real teeth. Her smile is all that's left of the face he fell in love with as a boy. Her frame of bones is what's left of that body.
There is no fire in his feelings for her now; no fire for anything unless it's fried catfish, corndodgers and grits, with lots of commissary wedge cheese. Makes his mouth water, thinking about it, and he hasn't been one hour having eaten dinner at the caf in Folkston before heading down to Moniac. A good story going in his head and another knocking on his skull to get in, he has a passion for that, or is it a lonesomeness for life lived off the page? He tests himself that way, standing there, and discovers he has more passions than he realized: hunting, fishing, riding dirt roads, camping out in the Okefenokee Swamp. Mostly his passions have to do with getting food or eating it. 
"What's got you out and moving in this heat?" she says. 
He grins like a suitor, holding to the left banister of the porch steps and pulling up, hand over hand. "Would you believe a soda water and a porch to sit on?"
"They must be fresh out in Folkston?" She slings her head, laughing. "Come on in. Soda water's on the house but you can pay for your own peanuts."
The scent of mowed grass steeps in the hot sun, claiming the dry hay smell of curing grass. 
Always, when he is with her, he tries to act older, closer to her age. So, now that he is finally really old he comes off decrepit, somebody's uncle, nobody's husband, body gone to ruin from lack of use. Soon, if he keeps up this act, he will have acted himself straight into a wheel chair. 
She sits in her large rocking chair with the cowhide bottom, her daddy's old chair, on the left side of the doorway. Claude ambles on inside, stepping up from the porch to the store floor with an exaggerated groan and one hand on his right knee. It's a habit, much as anything, a habit hard to break. Probably one of those annoying habits you are the last to notice and only a wife could break. One of those annoying habits that make you groan recalling all those times you have done it, and groaning over knowing comes off just as stupid.
Out West one time-hell, the only time!-he had stopped off at a Texas roadhouse for a bull-size grilled steak. Eating, paying, fishing a toothpick from one of those little shot glasses, he sauntered out picking his teeth. When he got in his truck, sinking his body now weighted with cow into the panned out place on the seat, he sat, picking his teeth and staring off at the fireball of sun guttering out on the treeless horizon. His pale green eyes had caught his image in the rear view mirror, teeth shining like a jackass's. 
 He goes over to the red drink box at the end of the mahogany-painted counter, opens the lid and fishes a cola from the stale icy water. She still picks chunks of ice from the blocks delivered by truck to cover the floated bottles of Orange Crush, R.C.'s, Coca Colas and Tab. He should have a diet cola; he takes an R.C. though. Pleasure is pleasure, he has found, whether it's scratching a mosquito bite or eating chocolate cake.
Opening the drink on the side of the box without looking at the cap-prying adder head, he closes his eyes to exercise his powers of observation and breathes in the smells of cheese, crackers, croaker sacks, dust, waxed paper, fly paper or maybe cane syrup. He opens his eyes. Yes, fly paper, spirals of the sticky black tape spinning in the draft of the droning paddle fan. He is surprised as always to see the old wood stove standing with its cat feet dug into the frame of littered white sand in the left corner at the back; the black metal tree of tiered bins filled with odd screws and nails; shiny lard cans; tin wash tubs; screened cover over a wheel of cheese. Standard commissary, if not for the owner.
She is rocking, fanning gnats and heat, waiting when he comes out on the porch again and takes the sturdy straight chair with the flat wood bottom next to hers. Spit cans filled with sand between chairs and walls lined with wide plank benches that have been there as long as he can remember. North end of the porch is a rusty old kerosene drum with a hinged bonnet of corrugated metal and perforated pump on top. He's pumped many a galvanized bucket of kerosene for lamps, though he'd never tell his age by telling that. Not that he is vain in that way, but who wants to hear it, this day and age?
Raring so that his pale buckskin boots clear the floor, he takes a long swig of the R.C., checks the level in the neck and sets it between his stout thighs. Then he tears open the cellophane pack of Tom's roasted peanuts with his capped teeth and shuffles about a third of the pack into the bottle, watching them fizz. "I put the quarter on the counter by the cash register, okay?"
He drinks, and then chews. There's a slight bay-scented breeze out of the south but it's hot. 
"So?" she says.
"So that's what you said do, pay for the peanuts."
"No, I meant why are you here?"
"Oh!" He blushed, drank some R.C. to cool his face. "What did you think about the piece in Sunday's paper?'
No answer. She seems spellbound by the goings on across the tracks, though nothing out of the ordinary is going on that he can detect at first. Then he spies an old lady in a blue duster and white tennis shoes walking in the flocked shadows of the oaks sheltering the lane. Arms by her side and swinging. The houses facing the tracks are mixed old and new but all are painted and neat with borders of flowers growing from mowed lawns. Few fences now, unlike in the old days, but there are still plank benches built around a couple of the old oaks. The big house where he was raised at the end of the lane up the tracks still sports its white picket fence. Gone are the house paints in shades of yellow with brick red and green trim though there are a few brown doors remaining. The old Miller house is still the biggest, the grandest, where Roberta hails from on this fine afternoon. 
He had kind of hoped that Merry Gay wouldn't have been able to see him from here a while back acting the fool with Roberta's wheelchair. She had just got home from the hospital, sick with her heart, and he'd no sooner got her settled in bed than she put in to go out on the front porch. He figured then it was probably her new medicine acting up-the doctor had warned him about side effects, said she might become irrational, but what else was new? 
So Claude had loaded her up in her new wheelchair and pushed it out on the porch, parked it next to one of the big white rockers and sat down in the chair. Her yard, still fenced in with white pickets, was an exhibit of junk she called antiques: cast-iron wash pot, plow stocks, an anvil, a wagon wheel. Roberta always did like her history run through a strainer, collecting the old ways from the old days and throwing out the rest. Why she read those trashy romances-sex in abstract-and watched the soaps. Readers Digest she considered serious literature.
 Claude had been known to indulge her and her historical society by writing about surface stuff like cane-grindings and quilting bees. When he wrote in 1973 about the Supreme Court ruling lifting the ban on abortions, she hadn't read it; if she had she would have made him write a retraction, if not a retraction then an apology to the good ladies of the historical society for printing the unspeakable. So, this story about the Moniac massacre would be his first test of her reaction to fact.
But back to the wheelchair incident: they had been sitting watching the squirrels cutting capers in the live oak out front when Roberta suddenly stood and toddled off toward the tall brick doorsteps and hugged the spooled newel porch post in her path. 
"Travis, you come back here?" she hollered out toward the empty lane running to woods. "Hear me, you come back here. You meet that woman at the hunting cabin one more time and I'll fix you both."
Claude had set out after her the second she left the chair, but when he got to where she was hugged up to the porch post, she was already sliding down to the floor. He approached her from behind like something wild and caught her under both arms, lifting her up and trying to stand her on her feet. The heels of her furry pink booties were all that was touching the floor when she lay into him with her toes sticking up and him dragging her backwards toward the wheelchair. 
She was out of her head, talking crazy, stuff Claude never wanted to know, would have been happier not knowing. He wanted to clamp a hand over her mouth but he had to hold on to her till he could get her in the wheelchair again. Problem was, every time he would reach out for it, the sucker would roll. It rolled to the north end of the porch and he dragged her over there, trying to reach it with his foot. One touch and it shot southward like a star, or like a lizard whose tail you have touched only to see it flash out of eyesight.
Again, he set out after the chair, dragging Roberta, limp and mumbling about Travis and that woman like she was talking in her sleep. Said she had seen them together at the old hunting cabin and knew for a certainty that that baby was his. 
Claude touched the toe of his right brown loafer to the chair wheel and again it shot off like a rocket to the southwest corner of the railing. "If that ain't the dangdest ball bearings I ever heard tell of!"
Almost there, almost to the chair finally hemmed up between a potted fern and the spoked railing, dragging Mama ever step and about to pass out from the chase, and she just sat down on the floor with her legs spread like a doll twisted to sitting position, crying and begging somebody whose name he couldn't make out and didn't want to make out to forgive her for shooting Jean after she shot Bernice.
 A screen door slams somewhere up the tracks. Children call out and laugh. Dogs bark. Roberta is still struggling along the lane, other side of the tracks. Whites still live on the east side and blacks on the west side. Some things never change.
"Here she comes," Merry Gay says. 
 "You did read it?" He is reading her face in profile, while she fans, lost in its contradictory vulnerability and strength. The fan sends whispers of earth scents his way, plus Merry Gay's share of the gnats.
"Didn't have to read it since I'm the one gave you the story."
He drinks, chews, says, "Yes ma'am, you got a point there." But he's disappointed-seventy-three and he can still be disappointed as a boy showing his first paper to his teacher. 
"I did read it though." She stops fanning and looks at him with those filmed-over huckleberry eyes. Still those dilated pupils of the passionate young. "You did a fine job telling it like it was. Didn't leave out a thing I could tell. Roberta hasn't spoken to me since."
He plops both boot soles down on the floor and ditches his elbows on his knees. Cold drink in his right hand and the cellophane peanut pack rolled at the top and placed in his blue-striped shirt pocket. "I hate that, I do. Cussfire if I don't! Said she didn't like you telling me I'm really Jean and Anse's boy."
They watch her across the tracks, crabbing on along the lane, in full sun now. Elbows cocked and on a mission. Every so often she waves at nobody, like an insane woman escaped from the state asylum in Milledgeville. But it's the gnats.
Rumor of a train up the track. And from the swamp south down the tracks come the sounds of  dipping irons rapping on metal gum buckets, followed by the clunking of buckets being dumped into the oak-stave barrels. Men used to identifying themselves as I or me, us or we, and not their own names, call them out anyway to receive credit from the tally man for each bucket of gum dipped and dumped, though the names don't want to go anywhere and fall false on their ears.
On the way in to Moniac after noon, Claude had admired their handiwork: the shiny new tins tacked to the streaked cat-faces of the pines, all facing east to catch the sunrise. Of course the faces would yield more gum when sun-struck. Any turpentine man, or woman, in this case, with a lick of sense knows that, but how many warm to the beauty of the new tin?  
Merry Gay says, "Roberta didn't like me talking, period." 
"It didn't change anything, you know that."
"You're hers sure as Tommy belonged to my mama."
"I couldn't love her any more," he says and laughs. "I'm just messing with her-what keeps her going."
"What keeps her going is bossing the whole place." Merry Gay waves a slender bony hand out in an arc before her face.
He sits straight, checks his R.C. for the salty peanuts that give it fizz and cuts the sweet taste. He drinks; he chews. Then the ritual of shaking nuts into the neck of the bottle and rolling down the pack, so forth and so on starts over. "Power goes to some people's heads."
A green pickup whips in at the lane and rips past the commissary, dust wheels chasing it.
"We get along generally, me and Roberta. We keep order."
"Sounds like somebody else we both know."
"We just bought out the ones selling out anyway." She fanned. "Rather have a new car, mobile home, most of them."
"I know. You and Mama's been the saving of this place. Wasn't for y'all the big timber companies would have taken over away back."
Roberta is trudging up the rail bed, stopping and waving gnats as she squints up the tracks.
 Dozens of white children dash from the yards to the tracks and line up close as they dare on the east side and that many more black children on the west side, laughing, shouting, jumping on and off of the nearest rail. Always a surprise, the dark freight train embodies all sound, all motion, clips Roberta from view as surely as if the engine has swept her south along the tracks like a wayward cow. It is a monstrous roar, a calamity of socketed rattles and clanks, the sure limit of the human ear's endurance without jolting blank the brain. Belching burnt diesel and smelting iron, the train has maybe twelve empty cars-black with a red caboose as faded as the two women and the flag rotting in the high sun. 
The trains have long ago quit stopping at Moniac Crossing and the depot platform on the south end of the commissary has been torn down. A once-prized Hoover buggy with flat rubber tires, dip-wagon relic of the Forties, sits forgotten, stove-in wood body gathering leaves and trash. In a patch where the depot once stood no grass or weeds will grow, but one mimosa with fuzzy pink blooms thrives like corn. Cola caps and snuff lids seem to hatch from the dead dirt like fleas when it rains. It's a poor man's pavement.
Claude and Merry Gay wait until the train passes, then resume talking the way people do who are used to train breaks.
 "You know, before long Roberta and I will both have to retire from the Savannah Cotton Exchange board. Too old and too ornery to make the trip, for one thing. Besides, old biddies like us have no business helping set market prices that affect the whole world. We'll both have to step down, generally speaking, then the big timber companies will take over here unless you?" 
"No ma'am," he laughs, sets his empty bottle down easy next to his chair. Long pause. "I'm a newspaper man; don't aim to do anything else." 
"We need a newspaper here. Good for keeping at the truth of things." 
He leans forward and crosses his ankles so that the soles of his boots turn out. "What y'all need is a good accountant, you and Mama. Y'all can't count." He demonstrates with a checkmark chopping motion of his left hand. "I'm seventy-three years old and looks to me like y'all are gonna outlive me by a few years. I'm thinking about putting Mama in my will." He takes off his glasses and holds them up to the light to check for smudges and spatters likely put there either while he was eating at some caf or piddling along some side street. Roberta steps into the dirty, warped bubbles of glass. 
 The locusts are humming again; the crickets are clicking along the tracks. The caboose had barely passed before the children from both sides met on the tracks to throw rocks and chase after it. Roberta is trudging up to the rail bed, hot wind from the train having freed gray wisps from the scant bundle of hair on her nape and a small spotted dog is yapping at her heels. Her body is thick but her legs and arms are sticks. All that was hard about her has gone soft, soft as mush, but she still speaks feisty and steps quick though listing leftward, frowning and correcting as if calling her stubborn self back. It could take a while. 
"Ain't nobody else, so you really don't have any choice," Merry Gay says low. "The rest of them are long gone from here and Mr. Harrell and I were never blessed with children."
"My brothers and sisters. Yep, scared to death they're gonna get stuck with Mama and who can blame them?" He does blame them, some days he does. "No, ma'am, I guess I never did have a choice."
A slow white car passes. The brown woman driving waves, they wave.
Merry Gay finalizes it by patting her chair arm with the flat of her left hand. "It'll all fall in place if you do your part."
Roberta is within earshot now, though practically deaf, so they wait on the porch, looking down and off but not at her, not speaking to her. They will wait till she steps into the straightedge of the eave shadow before they look at her. The dog is gone, running sleek and low back across the tracks to greet the gang of white children now playing hopscotch under the oaks. The black children are still down the tracks, train forgotten and on another lark.
"Reckon how they're taking to integration?" he asks Merry Gay.
"Can't tell. School's a whole lot different from chasing trains on the same track."
"Bout the only way it's gonna work, in my estimation, is to put the younguns in charge and let em do what comes natural." He waves his left hand out, still looking off but not at Mama.
Merry Gay laughs. "You know better."
"Well, look at you and Tommy." He wrenches round to eye her and not have to eye his mama, moving up to the doorsteps.
"Tommy and I?well, that was different. We were raised in the same house, had the same color of skin. What if we'd been raised on the west side of the tracks, the situation turned around, and I was white but had dark skin, both of us? It would have likely turned out the same. But it was Mama made it work, by example. Either that or my mindset that Tommy was white. I can't recall when I found out she wasn't, not blood-white, but I do recall that we didn't play with the children across the tracks. Not really. Not anymore than those children out there right now. It's born into you to know where you fit, and you fit where you are."
Claude stands tall. "Mama," he says loud and bows his head. Rimming his warped brown belt with his thumbs, he shuffles his feet as if getting set to dance with her.
A radio north of the commissary, west side of the tracks, is competing, a snappy country song. 
"Claudie." Roberta's white tennis shoes look as if they've absorbed the sunrays. The flesh of her broad shoulders has worn down to bone. She looks silly in the dainty blue duster with tiny pink embroidered flowers on the yoke. She reminds Claude of a pine stump that has melted away to hardened snags and heart.
 Too, the way she says Claudie makes him drop about sixty years, and he is back, he and his brothers, smoking under the white, many-winged Miller house he can see clearly at the end of the lane eastside of the tracks. She was waiting with a switch when they crawled out, dazed, dirty and barefoot. Somehow they had held for one day a notion, a fiction, that they could escape her. Maybe the nicotine did it. Before she could catch hold of a one of them, they squirreled up a pecan tree in the back yard, refusing to come down. God help him, Claude had even laughed. She wheeled and went inside and before he could draw in his lips to a grimace of almighty fear she was there with her shotgun and enough birdshot to keep them picking steel from their backsides as a pastime till they were grown men.
"How are you, Roberta?" Merry Gay fans, rocks, indulges a long breath. 
When she has pulled herself up to the top step, close enough they can smell her talcum, Roberta speaks to Claude. "Tell her I'm fine under the circumstances."
He turns up the volume. "Mama, Mrs. Harrell didn't tell me the story to cause trouble. I'd already figured I wasn't yours and Daddy's by blood."
"That's not it, Claude," Merry Gay shouts for Roberta's benefit, though not speaking to her. "Not exactly."
He flushes with fear that Merry Gay is about to tell him she knows that Roberta shot Jean, or that he's a Miller for real. For a fact, Roberta may have told her though she never remembered telling him after the run-away wheelchair episode and he's decided to keep what he knows to himself: the story has been written and that's the way it will stand.
One minute the white children across the tracks are playing and laughing, and next minute they are quarreling, pairing off. 
"It was a lie you put in there about me being weak." Roberta waves at gnats with a roll of her right wrist.
"Not weak, Mama, sick. You were sick. I think I made that clear."
"Same difference."
"You never overed it, did you, Roberta? You never overed letting Mama and Jean down." Merry Gay is no longer fanning but she is still rocking. Stiff as a corpse. Then she adds low, almost mumbling, "Say you were scared and not sick, who'd have blamed you?"
	To keep Mrs. Harrell from being shot out of a tree, Claude speaks up. "You didn't let anybody down. You didn't have a thing to do with what happened that night, Mama. Sit down, will you? Sit down before you fall down." 
"If I wanted to sit down I would. And if I'd of wanted it told, I'd of told it. We all would." Roberta shakes her finger in his face. "And don't go giving me that bit about the truth. Ask her this...ask her who's better off for her having talked to you and you writing the whole thing down for the world to see?"
"Who's worse off, Roberta? Who's worse off for me telling what happened?" Merry Gay sits forward, holding her knees.
Their loud voices are a familiar shock like the trains.
"Son, that's what comes from marrying an educated Yankee." Roberta is swaying on her feet; pale with the liver pieds on her face standing out. She was ninety-five last birthday and has congestive heart failure. Several times she has died and been brought back to life. Still she refuses to sign one of those orders not to resuscitate. Over her dead body, she says.
"Mr. Harrell was not a Yankee, and since when is it a sin to be educated?" Merry Gay rocks hard, paddling the floor with her woven straw sandals.
Roberta's pupils grow larger and her nickel-colored irises shimmer iridescent as mosquito hawk wings. "Tell her I didn't say it was a sin, son. Tell her I don't begrudge her marrying an outsider. Tell her I never wanted you to know your real mama was crazy and have you wonder if you'd end up the same way." She gives him a saved-up tender look and drops her hiked shoulders. "I didn't want you to use your mama-or your daddy for that matter-as some excuse to just slide by in life." 
Spitting, sputtering, she turns on Merry Gay.  "Education!  You wanta talk education? Look who put all four younguns through school after her husband dropped dead on her." 
"Roberta, you're gonna give yourself a stroke." Merry Gay starts to stand, but only perches on the edge of her chair, hands clasped between her knees. "It's written, it can't be unwritten."
"I'd as soon we left it alone and moved on. Now everybody and his brother'll be hounding us. Looking at us to talk."
"I had to do it, Roberta. I had to do it and I can't say why exactly. Only that it didn't matter anymore whether I talked about it or not."
They were talking to each other now with Claude only listening in, innocently, leisurely polishing his glasses in dainty circles with his white handkerchief and those blocky hands.
 "Don't tell me you're gonna tell me it was for the sake of history, like this boy here did. I don't buy that, not for a minute."
"It was the right thing to do, Roberta, the right time. Would you rather we went on just not talking, leaving Moniac Crossing with a legacy of its people having been stubborn fools? You and me, we're the only ones left living to tell it like it was, and we won't be around much longer to give the straight of it. Remember I lost a mother and a sister, and Priss? I have my own guilt over that night. Or I did. I might have stopped Tommy, but I don't think I'd have stopped her from what was coming anyway. I'd have done nothing but slow it down."
"I don't want to hear that if you're about to start in on fate and all that pagan stuff again."
"The will of God, I've told you that before."
"Well, it was the devil made you tell this boy here all that dirty stuff about the Shirley boy, Jay. And of all things, to lay it to his own mama, saying she was the one told you."
"Mama, I didn't even write that. You were reading between the lines a bit, weren't you?"
"But she told you, didn't she?"
"Not as much as you think, I don't imagine. If nothing else, I was circumspect in what I chose to write."
Roberta socks her fists on her boxy hips and sets her square jaw. "That's all I've got to say on the subject." She pinches her right thumb and pointer finger together and draws a line along her thin pale lips, snapping her head to one side to show her lips are now zipped. End of discussion.
Suddenly it comes to Claude in a chill of heat that the bottled-up story was what had kept them both going, hashing it over, debating it, harboring it. Their present is the past and has been since the massacre. And now that it has been let, like blood, they can die. Having passed it on to him-and who else would care, who else would take hold?-they can both die.
He'd been fairly sure Roberta wouldn't last after her most recent bout with her heart in the Macclenny hospital; she had run through all the Georgia doctors, after having fallen out with them about her salt. Then she had started on Florida, and Claude figured there wasn't much need in getting too used to the place because she was already referring to the doctor as "him" and the hospital as "in here."
All day long her vital signs had been dropping and her doctor said she probably wouldn't live through the night. She looked dead already, her body a straight mound under the sheet, arms and worn-to-the-bone hands like potato rakes. Not moving and yellowed where her skin was showing. Comatose, but her eyeballs were twitching under crepe lids.  
Claude had pulled up a chair to her bed and stretched out as best he could and he lay watching her as he fell asleep, a light dozing state in which he could hear the nurses talking up the hall and their soft-soled shoes creeping like cats past the door they'd left ajar.
Suddenly he felt a sharp pain in his right shin and sat up to see Roberta sitting up on the edge of the bed, eyes about half-open and mean as a snake's.
"Mama, what in the world!" he said, laughing at the miracle wrought by all the money she'd been sending to Oral Roberts maybe.
"I'm fixing to get up and go find me something to eat. A body would starve on what they feed you in here."
She huffs on into the commissary now, listing toward the left doorjamb and a handhold. She groans, stepping up. In a second, the pressurized cooler door slams and she is back with a pound of butter pressed to one sallow sagged cheek. "Put it on my account," she says and steps down, right white tennis shoe waiting for the left to join it. New white canvas shoes, her sole extravagance.
"What account?" Merry Gay shouts.
"By the way," Roberta yells in that curdled voice, "one of your cows got hit by the train up there at Bamburg Brake."
"How do you know it was mine and not yours?" Merry Gay shouts with her hands each side of her mouth.
"Because it's dead." Roberta cackles out, walking on.
"Y'all need to put up some fences." Claude sits again.
And again they watch her labor up the sloped leftward-listing path worn to a sandy trench by her tennis shoes and step high over the wheel-shined tracks, sun like water darting along the jointed rails. Some woman from either the second or third house-row in the east calls out, "Jimbo! Allen! Y'all come on." A car whizzes past on the highway south of the commissary.
"Mama, I swear!" Claude says to himself and looks down at his stiff freckled hands latched between his parted legs. "I wouldn't hurt her for the world. I'm crazy about her, Daddy too?Travis. I used to follow him ever step he took."
"Travis was a good man, overbearing, but good as they come. Did me good to see him move back in on his old homeplace."
"She got mad as fire when I asked her on the phone yesterday about my real daddy, what happened to him."
Merry Gay takes it as a question. "He gave the sheriff the slip, as they say, just dropped off the face of the earth seems like."
Alone, together, they easily lapse into what people call southern dialect, the cracker tongue of their raising. Whatever, it's not for lack of knowing better, it's simply comfortable, heart to heart, porch talk. Fitting in.
"No hearing from him or nothing, right?"
"Not that I know of. No. I know not."
"If I had it to do over with I wouldn't write it." 
"You would, if it killed you and her both you would."
They watch the pyramid shadow of the commissary inch toward the tracks. A crop dusting plane is dipping and rising, buzzing the blue sky, then gone beyond the woods. But the bitter chemical mist remains. 
"I reckon so," he says and rares again, crossing his hairy brown-spatched arms over his loose round belly. "I'd leave some stuff out though. Like you did." He stares at Merry Gay, cool and calm now in the heat. White scalloped teeth showing but not smiling as she gazes out at Roberta gimping along home. 
"Is that why you're here, tying up loose ends, so to speak?"
"Part of it." The other part he won't tell as he had planned. To keep from telling, he turns the question transfigured into a statement back on her. "I do wonder why you stayed quiet until now, why you waited all those years to tell your story."
"Nothing noble if that's what you're looking for. Let's just say I was tying up some loose ends too."
"What ever happened to Jay Shirley?" 
"Jay Shirley." She laughed. "I absolutely had to stop and think a minute to recollect the name-a dangerous thing to do and him so evil. Some said Jay came home later, after the Militia showed up and things were beginning to shape up; some of the Negroes claimed he showed up in a skirt and a blond wig the night of the fires." She laughed. "Anyway when he got home, he had a son born, a niece dead-so much changed since he took off downtown at the train depot in St. Augustine that evening. But that's just my thinking, how I'd look at it. Not how it was maybe?like everything else I told?close as I could get at the truth."
A tall gauche-faced black man in black rubber boots strides up the lane this side of the tracks. He has a hand-rolled cigarette stuck in one corner of his lips, smoking without touching it with his fingers. "Home middle of the evening again," Merry Gay says. "Just ain't cut out for the woods."
She stands, stepping to the square hewed post next to the porch steps and leaning into it with her hips cocked. "Hey, you! Coot Harris! You knocking off early again?"
The man turns, shielding his eyes from the sun and its reflection on the tin roof. "Bear-caught." He laughs, stumps at the hard white dirt with his boot toe. Decades of crushed glass shards glitter like mica. 
"Wouldn't be you're trying to keep from working that cypress slew down the tracks, would it?"
"Nome, ain't that." The cigarette looks stuck to his lips, still smoking on its own. 
"You go on home now. Get your stuff and come back by for your wages. Hear?"
"Yessum, bosslady." He slumps off, then breaks into a gait almost loping.
She sits again. "Roberta had the same trouble with him. Bad to drink, then can't work."
Claude rests his head on the wall behind him. "Them moccasins in that slew, I don't blame him."
"Dry time like this is about the only time we can dip those boxes."
"At least the mosquitoes have let up. But these gnats?" He blows at the busy specks flying around his eyes, nose, ears and arms. Really, they tickle his neck like feathers.
Cottony clouds drift in the blue sky above the tree tops eastside of the tracks. The chemical mist from the crop duster has lifted or burned off, and in its place is the green-peanut scent of cooling earth and oak-moss. It is that slow time, of an evening in Moniac, between trains, between customers, and he would like nothing better than to sit and count the tine marks on the raked dirt. Maybe count the leaf shadows of the old white oak, or the seconds till the katydids begin shrilling in the woods. See how close he can come to guessing, for no reason, no purpose. He would like to stay close to Merry Gay and listen to the sun ticking on the tin roof a while longer without reason or purpose. 
He can absolutely and for certain feel that settling in of only-love in old age. And it seems like enough, compensation for all those years and years of longing. Longing for rapture. First love, last love. Did you ever know I loved you?love you?
"I'm sorry," he said.
"Sorry for what?"
"Sorry I used to spy on you."
She laughed. "I started to go to your mama."
"Why didn't you?"
"It was harmless; you'd grow out of it."
"I thought I was in love with you." He didn't say for how long.
"Love?"
At thirteen he'd thought about stepping out in front of a train on the off-chance that she might take pity and sing at his funeral. He's never heard her sing, only heard about her singing. In part, that was the intrigue, he guesses. But it was sexual too: it was love at first breast-sighting, it was love at first arm-brushing; it was heat in the groin and in the heart.
"This writer by the name of Marquez," he says, "claims that unrequited love, and not happy love, is what makes the world go round."
Her eyes search out his eyes. "Hum, interesting." 
Peeping on her, once, he had tripped on his shoe strings, giving away his hiding place between the honeysuckle vine and her bedroom window, four houses down from his own. But she'd only crossed her arms over her white chemise-clad breasts and stepped to the window. "Are you okay, honey?"
He had walked around for days trying to make "honey" into a sweetheart's endearment, honey with a capital "H", though he knew it was lower-case. "honey" was what his mama called him when he was on her good side.
Like after cemetery cleanings with the whole community there and he started putting two and two together and coming up with four. Too many graves with the same death dates, and the holy hush that would come over them-the old ones-when they hoed stinging nettles and prickly pears from those graves. One, Ewell (Samson) Elton, had only the word "hero" engraved on his headstone. Everybody else was "Asleep in Jesus." Claude would ask his mother what happened to the people buried there and she would say, "When you're older, honey." 
And his birth-date: too close to his youngest brother's to be born one after the other; not close enough to be twins. He hadn't known who he belonged to then but it wasn't the mean-loving, hard-working, honest people he lived with.
For something to say to Merry Gay Rose and because he could sense she was smart and wise, he had finally asked her about the co-incidence of death dates in the cemetery at the Methodist Church. Her reply could have been, Get lost, kid! for all he could recall because at the time he asked her she was bathing naked in the creek swimming hole with her arms crossed over her breasts again. He'd thought to throw her off guard by happening up out of the palmettos from which he might have been plucked shortly after hatching, but instead he was shocked senseless by the sight of those pink-tipped creamy mounds on her chest before she got shocked by the sight of her red-headed Peeping Tom.
Really, he had no longer cared at that moment-for years even-why the duplicated death dates on the headstones. He no longer cared where he'd come from, only where he was bound; nestled deep between those breasts in her bed which he had come to know like his own from peeping. He couldn't go lower on the human anatomy, hadn't the smarts or sensitivity for that yet, so in his fantasy he only slept while she stroked his hair.
Later, at sixteen or seventeen, he would come to know that he had lost her the day that Mr. Randolph Harrell showed up at the train depot at Moniac Crossing. Tall, dark, handsome? No. He was short and stocky like Claude himself, but bold and bald where Claude was timid but blessed with a full head of hair. Red curls that drove Merry Gay Rose crazy! She couldn't keep her hands out of his hair. So, it was no wonder that he fantasized about her stroking his hair and got hair all mixed up with the other thing, even after he'd found out that she would marry the wealthy, though bald, Mr. Randolph Harrell. Even after Claude had been educated into the thrill of loving women after dark.
His education came from his first night peeping on the couple and it must have been not too long after she got married. Lady that she was, it would have taken a while for her to get that wanton with Mr. Harrell. The muslin curtains at his favorite peeping window to her bedroom-their bedroom now-were drawn, but a convenient gap had been left just for him it seemed. What with the drawn curtains and the dark outside, he could rest easy that he wouldn't be discovered and so enjoy the show. Hell, they had the gas light on! 
Every so often he had to touch his curls to bring himself around from having spirited into the body of Mr. Harrell, sitting buck naked in the middle of the bed, facing Mrs. Harrell, who was finally naked and oblivious of Claude viewing for pleasure, at his leisure, her fine jouncing breasts. Their legs were locked about each other's hips, groin to groin, both of their hands spread behind them for support and gazing into the other's glazed-over eyes. Smiling, not talking, thrusting from the hips. 
Next night when Claude came to watch again, the unbearable heat in his groin area had been cooled by the closed gap between the curtains and the fact that they were quarrelling. He didn't hang around to find out about what. He expected that Mr. Harrell with his bags would be at the train depot the following morning.
But that night they were loving again, and Claude was forced to listen to the bedsprings squeaking, the headboard knocking against the wall, and Mrs. Harrell moaning like the wind in a chimney.
Hearing made him crazier than seeing! It was a wonder he hadn't been snake bit working in the woods during the day, he was so blind. He had heard that could happen from spilling his seed on the ground. There was a whole garden of unplanted little Millers under her window. 
And so it went, one night they would be fighting and the next night they would be loving. Capping off his education, Claude surmised that love abounds from bouts of anger and passion.
Still, he often considered adultery, risking hell and all for her. He had to quit peeping though; like everybody else in the community he was too scared of "the bossman." But for different reasons, he'd recently learned. Moniac folk feared a repeat of Nathan Shirley and treated their dogs kinder than they treated Mr. Randolph Harrell.
How did Mrs. Randolph Harrell handle them? With a smile until her husband could prove himself to be on their level. He tended to his own business, swept around his own back door, hampering the etched-in-oak habits of the red-headed Peeping Tom. 
The late Mr. Randolph Harrell even earned the respect and admiration of one Mr. Claude Miller: he was a simple man with simple needs.

***

Claude's chair legs clank to the wide pine floor, shaking it. He cackles out. "Yaa Lordy! And y'all want me to take over the land and turpentine timber, mollycoddling a bunch of drunks with better sense than to wade out in a hammock of moccasins? Tell me, why would I?why?" He stops to study her face; she isn't laughing, she isn't even studying on an answer, not to that question. The real question is laid bare. Serious of a sudden, he turns it into a statement: "You never told me if Nathan Shirley ever found out he killed his own granddaughter."
"Off the record or on?"
"Off the record, I promise." He raises his right hand as much for his own sake as hers: if he swears, he will never tell it, never write it. Secretly he is swearing too not to tell her about Travis and Jean, about Roberta shooting Jean. He feels like a traitor, a friendly betrayer, but why complicate Merry Gay's hard-earned understanding by pointing out another angle to the story for her to reflect on the rest of her born days? 
For instance, Jean had to have had a lethal injury to another part of her body besides her mouth, unless she allowed Roberta to stick the barrel of the pistol into her mouth, which didn't make sense and Jean a raving maniac. Or what if Roberta spurred on by jealousy and nerves that night had decided to set Jean free to wander, to get killed or lost, by ripping loose the boards from the back door her own self and Jean had surprised her, standing there with a pistol ready to blast Roberta to Kingdom Come? What if Roberta had caught Jean's wrist, say, trying to wrestle the gun from her and Bernice had happened by and tried to help and got shot accidentally?
 So many ways to tell the story, so much he'd never know. He would give a pretty to know what had happened that night between Jean and Roberta or maybe between Travis and Anse-mix it up among any of the four. He would never know. But he did know that anybody who had killed, for whatever reason, that night had long been absolved: not-guilty of going mad, maybe doing in his or her own heart what was there all along, but may never have been carried out under normal circumstances. If the dead could talk, there would probably be hundreds of different stories from those who died that night or died afterwards, taking with them to their graves their own versions of what really happened, how it all went. But one thing was clear-the women had been at war too that night. Two of them still were. 
"Well, did Nathan Shirley find out he shot Priss or not?"
"He did find out." Merry Gay shifts in her chair, resting her head on the back with her eyes closed. The hollow of her throat sucks in like weathered rubber with each threat of heartbeat. "I told him."
"Out of revenge for Tommy's death, I guess."
"Partly. Partly to bribe him into using his influence to get my brothers and a bunch more out of trouble."
She sits up, opening her eyes to find him staring at her aghast. Not breathing, waiting for the rest of it with a look of dread. "Swear," she says.
"I swear." His right hand is still raised and his fingers are growing numb. His tongue feels numb but his brain is on fire. 
"I blackmailed him into leaving and giving back our land. I told him I would tell exactly how he shoved through the door of the dining car and began shooting through the lavatory door without making sure who was in there. I told him I would see to it his family, every last member, knew him for the scoundrel he was. I would tell his new wife about how he had Tommy shot down like a dog after his cows. I would tell about him paying off some poor old broke farmer to keep word from spreading that Jay had raped his wife while she lay dead on her kitchen table. I said more, I said a lot more that I probably never will remember and don't need to. I know I threatened to go to the governor with what I knew about him sliding over landlines and taking advantage of landowners by getting them in debt to him and then foreclosing.
"He, Shirley, had this little compass he kept in his pocket and he would go along the lines of adjoining landowners. He used it to gauge the old lines' trajectory, he said. Nine times out of ten, he would swear the lines were off, in his favor. If the landowners had the time, money or power to prove he'd lied, which was rare, Shirley would blame it on his little compass; he'd shake it, put it to his ear like a pocket watch, and say, 'Well, I be dang! Old contrary thing. I guess I oughta break down and buy me a new one. No hard feelings, bud.'"
"My lord! My lord!" Claude feels flushed and breathless. Suddenly fearing to look at her, he has to look, to check?for what he doesn't know except maybe for some change. She looks the same, she looks redeemed. 
The sun is going down behind the commissary; the men are coming in from work. Screen doors slam. Supper's cooking-bread, it smells like buttermilk biscuits to Claude. The commissary porch seems set back in its own shadow, in its own woodsy nook. 
 "Sorry cuss used to have his men pour water over capped barrels of resin to make them weigh more at the market." She shook her head as if to rid herself of static from memory interference. "I even got hold of some land records with plats showing where he'd moved lines. Foreclosure information only a fool would write down, much less hang onto." She glares at Claude, just shy of an old lady's iron frown. "But best of all I got the compass."
"How?"
"Now Mr. Miller," she laughs out, shifting in her chair to face him as if to be done with the spot where she'd been sitting stiff, straight and serious for so long. Or perhaps to change the mood. "If I told you that you'd think I'm a hussy." 
"A hussy! You! I doubt it." He laughs too though he, for one, is not done with serious questions, serious conversation.
"I did what I set out to do and that's all." She turns in her chair again, facing out and waiting for the train they hear north up the track, maybe meaning by "that's all" that she's ready for him to go.
 "So that's what broke Nathan Shirley and broke his hold on this place!"
 And then the smile, her face wan in the spent daylight. "Let's just say it was the price he had to pay for my last song."


???


Acknowledgments 



The late Gene Barber did the original, factual research for this novelized rendering of the true story, "The Baxter Rebellion." His scintillating newspaper piece by that title was published in 1977 in his hometown of Macclenny, in north Florida. I didn't read it until three decades later, and then I lost track of it. But my cousin Robert Daugharty, and my friend Morris Smith, the real history finders and keepers, kept me in copies. They believed in my clairvoyant and narrative powers, in my insight into the hearts of these ordinary fictional people doing the best they can till they can do better.
I'm very grateful to them for their faith in me. 
Thanks also to Billy Langdale for his wonderful stories and to his brother Harley Langdale, Jr., my favorite turpentine man, for sharing his experiences and knowledge of turpentine and trains. Thank you to Ed Connell, my neighbor, for inspiring the big-hearted story about Hoss Rose building a coffin for a friend; and to Andy Hall for sharing his humorous stories about the sad life-endings of his ancient grandparents-nobody can tell it better than Andy! 
As for my own research, I did it the fun way, most of it, by visiting our local Agrirama, a reconstructed rural village in Tifton, Georgia, depicting farm life in the early 1900's; not only are the physical details authentic but you can smell the dirt and sawdust, hear train whistles and donkeys braying.
 As ever, my husband Seward lavished me with images, information and detail. And then there's Laney, my then-nine-year-old granddaughter, who literally stood behind me and breathed down my neck while I worked on this novel. She forced me to concentrate harder, to go deeper inside, and to keep it clean.
